  - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 2 Write First Script with Playwright Pytest and Al Introduction In this chapter, we will explore the core architecture of Playwright and understand how it supports fast, reliable, and modern automation. You will learn how to setup Playwright with Pytest, one of Python’s most popular testing frameworks, and begin writing clean, scalable test scripts. We will guide you through executing tests both through the intuitive user interface ( UI ) mode and the efficient command line interface ( CLI ) mode, preparing you for different development and CI environments. Finally, we will introduce the role of artificial intelligence ( AI ) in automation and walk you through building your first test script using a generative AI ( GenAI ) tool, unlocking the future of intelligent and adaptive test creation. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Understanding the Playwright architecture •&#9; Playwright with Pytest setup •&#9; Writing Playwright test with Pytest •&#9; Test execution with UI Mode •&#9; Test execution with CLI options •&#9; Introduction to AI in automation •&#9; Writing your first script using GenAI tool Objectives By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of how Playwright functions under the hood and how to set it up seamlessly with Pytest for writing robust test automation scripts in Python. You will be confident in executing your tests using both UI and CLI modes. Additionally, you will be introduced to the transformative role of AI in automation, and you will gain hands-on experience by writing your first script using a GenAI tool, paving the way for smarter and more efficient test creation. Understanding the Playwright architecture Playwright’s architecture integrates a multi-language client that sends commands via WebSocket to a Node.js-based server running Playwright, which then automates tests across multiple browsers. This setup offers a scalable, efficient, and versatile solution for modern web testing. Playwrights use socket-based protocol to facilitate communication between the client (your test script) and the server (Playwright server), a key factor in its efficiency and speed. When initiating a test, the test script establishes a WebSocket connection to send commands to the Playwright server. These commands, which guide the server to perform tasks such as navigating to a URL, interacting with elements, or taking screenshots, are typically transmitted in JavaScript ( JS ) Object Notation ( JSON ) format. In the following figure, you can see the architecture of Playwright: Figure 2.1 : Playwright architecture Here is an explanation of each component based on the figure: •&#9; Client : The Client represents the testing environment where test scripts are written and executed. It acts as the initiator of the automation process, sending commands to the server via a WebSocket connection. Playwright supports multiple programming languages, reflecting its versatility. The client can be coded in Python, C#, Java, JS, or TypeScript ( TS ). Each language allows users to craft test cases tailored to their expertise or project requirements. •&#9; WebSocket : It serves as the communication bridge between the Client and the Server, enabling real-time, bi-directional data exchange. Unlike traditional HTTP requests, WebSocket maintains a persistent connection, allowing the client to send Commands instantly and receive responses without the overhead of repeated connection setups. This ensures low-latency interaction, helpful for executing rapid test sequences or handling dynamic web applications. The architecture leverages this protocol to streamline the flow of instructions and feedback. •&#9; Server : It is the core execution engine that processes the commands received from the client and interacts with the target browsers. Hosted on a Node.js environment, the server uses Playwright’s robust framework to manage browser automation. It interprets the commands, controls browser instances, and executes the test actions. The server’s design supports scalability, allowing it to handle multiple browser sessions simultaneously, which is essential for cross-browser testing. •&#9; Playwright : It is the central automation library that powers the server-side operations, providing the tools to control browsers and execute tests. Playwright is a modern automation tool designed for reliability and speed. It supports advanced features like auto-waiting for elements, network interception, and headless browser execution. Its architecture is built to handle complex web applications, including those with single-page architectures ( SPAs ) or dynamic content, making it a powerful choice for quality assurance ( QA ) engineers. •&#9; Node.js : Node.js is the runtime environment that underpins the server, enabling the execution of JavaScript-based Playwright code. As an asynchronous, event-driven platform, Node.js ensures efficient handling of multiple browser instances and concurrent test executions. Its integration with Playwright allows for a lightweight yet high-performance server setup, leveraging JavaScript’s ecosystem to manage dependencies and browser interactions seamlessly. •&#9; Browsers : These are the target environments where the tests are executed, representing the end user perspective across different platforms. Support for major browsers, Chrome , Firefox , Safari , and Edge , each controlled directly by Playwright. This cross-browser capability allows end users to verify application compatibility and performance across diverse rendering engines (e.g., WebKit for Safari, Blink for Chrome/Edge, Gecko for Firefox). Playwright’s ability to automate these browsers natively, without relying on external drivers, enhances test consistency and speed. In the next section, you will see why Playwright is fast, you will see the reason from an architectural perspective, and some other factor that makes it fast. Fast execution with Playwright Playwright has rapidly gained a reputation as one of the fastest and most reliable browser automation frameworks available. Playwright&apos;s speed and efficiency can be attributed to its well-designed architecture and several unique features from the ground up to overcome the bottlenecks of older tools. The following section explores the core reasons behind Playwright&apos;s impressive performance. Core architectural advantage The following lists the old way (Selenium&apos;s HTTP architecture): •&#9; Client-server model : Selenium operates on a classic client-server model. Your test script (the client) sends a command (e.g., click this button ). •&#9; HTTP requests: This command is translated into a JSON payload and sent over HTTP to a WebDriver server (like ChromeDriver or Geckodriver ). After each command or request is executed, the connection between the client (where the automation script resides) and the server (where the WebDriver instance resides) is terminated. A new connection is re-established for every subsequent command. In the following figure, you can see how an HTTP request works: Figure 2.2 : HTTP request •&#9; Translation : The WebDriver server then translates this HTTP request into the browser&apos;s native protocol. •&#9; Response : The browser performs the action, sends a response back to the WebDriver, which then sends an HTTP response back to your test script. &#9;This architecture involves multiple network requests and data serialization/deserialization for every single command. The following are the Playwright (WebSocket architecture): •&#9; Single connection : When a Playwright test starts, it establishes a single WebSocket connection to the browser. This connection remains open for the entire duration of the test. •&#9; Bi-directional communication : The WebSocket protocol allows for full-duplex, bi-directional communication. Commands are sent from the test script to the browser, and events are sent from the browser back to the script over the same connection without the overhead of creating new HTTP requests: Figure 2.3 : WebSocket connection •&#9; No middleman : This approach effectively cuts out the middleman (the separate WebDriver server acting as an HTTP translator). The communication is more direct, resulting in dramatically lower latency for each command. Eliminating flakiness and delays Another crucial factor is Playwright&apos;s intelligent auto-waiting mechanism. Test scripts are often plagued by flakiness, where a test fails because an element was not ready when the script tried to interact with it. Playwright includes a built-in auto-wait feature that intelligently delays actions until the necessary conditions are met (e.g., an element is visible or clickable). This eliminates manual waits or sleep commands, which can slow down scripts in other tools. By dynamically adjusting to page states, Playwright ensures tests run at their maximum possible speed without compromising reliability. The traditional solution was to add hardcoded waits ( Thread.sleep() ), which slows down the entire test suite. Playwright solves this by performing a series of actionability checks before executing any action. When you command Playwright to page.click(&apos;#submit&apos;) , it does not just try to click immediately. Instead, it automatically waits for the element to be: •&#9; Attached to the DOM •&#9; Visible (not hidden by display: none or visibility: hidden ) •&#9; Stable (not animating or moving) •&#9; Enabled (not disabled) •&#9; Receives events (not obscured by another element) This eliminates the need for manual waits in your code, making tests not only much faster (as they proceed the instant an element is ready) but also vastly more reliable. Efficient isolation with browser contexts Launching a full browser instance for every test file is a resource-intensive and slow process. Playwright introduces the concept of browser contexts. A browser context is an isolated, incognito-like session within a single browser instance. Contexts are completely separate from one another; they do not share cookies, local storage, or sessions. Its speed advantage is that creating a new browser context is orders of magnitude faster than launching an entirely new browser. This allows you to run tests in parallel with clean, isolated environments for each one without the heavy overhead of spinning up multiple browser processes. This makes parallel test execution incredibly efficient. Network interception Page load time is a major component of overall test execution time. Playwright gives you powerful tools to control the network, which can be used to dramatically speed up tests. You can: •&#9; Block unnecessary resources : Easily abort requests for non-essential assets like images, fonts, stylesheets, or third-party analytics scripts that do not affect the functionality you are testing. •&#9; Mock API responses : Instead of waiting for a real backend to respond, you can intercept application programming interface ( API ) calls and provide an immediate, mocked response. This decouples your frontend tests from backend services, making them faster and more stable. Parallel browser execution Playwright architecture supports running tests across multiple browsers simultaneously via separate WebSocket connections. You could test on Safari and Edge at once, cutting down the total runtime from hours to minutes, perfect for meeting tight deadlines. This is especially valuable in fast-paced development environments where rapid feedback and cross-browser validation are crucial. Playwright with Pytest setup Playwright is an open-source automation framework by Microsoft for testing web applications across Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit browsers. It supports headless and headed modes, auto-waiting, and cross-platform testing on Windows, Mac, and Linux. Pytest is a popular, open-source testing framework for Python that simplifies writing and running tests for Python applications. It is widely used for unit testing, functional testing, and API testing due to its flexibility, ease of use, and powerful features. Pytest is a highly effective testing framework for Python, widely adopted in QA automation due to its simplicity, flexibility, and robust features tailored for testing complex software systems. In the context of automation, Pytest streamlines the process of writing, organizing, and executing automated tests for web applications, APIs, databases, and more, ensuring software reliability and performance. The following are the key features of Pytest: •&#9; Simple and concise syntax : Pytest allows for writing test cases with minimal boilerplate code. Test functions are typically named starting with test_ , and assertions are made using standard Python assert statements, making tests highly readable and easy to write. •&#9; Automatic test discovery : Pytest automatically discovers test files (typically named test_*.py ) and test functions/methods within them. You do not need to explicitly register or import tests, which streamlines the testing process. •&#9; Fixtures for managing test state : Fixtures are a powerful feature that provides a reliable way to setup and tear down test environments. They allow you to define reusable setup code (e.g., database connections, temporary files, mock objects) that can be injected into test functions. Fixtures promote code reusability and ensure consistent test conditions. •&#9; Plugins and extensibility : Pytest boasts a rich plugin ecosystem. Users can write their own plugins to extend Pytest&apos;s functionality or leverage a wide array of pre-built plugins for tasks like code coverage reporting, parallel test execution, mocking, and more. This makes Pytest highly adaptable to diverse testing needs. •&#9; Parameterized testing : Pytest makes it easy to run the same test function multiple times with different sets of input data using pytest.mark.parametrize . This is incredibly useful for testing various scenarios and edge cases efficiently without writing repetitive test code. •&#9; Detailed and informative test reports : Pytest provides clear and comprehensive output after test execution. It clearly indicates which tests passed, failed, or were skipped, along with detailed tracebacks for failures, making debugging much easier. •&#9; Assertion rewriting : Pytest automatically rewrites standard Python assert statements to provide detailed information on assertion failures. Instead of just showing AssertionError , it shows the values of the variables involved in the assertion, greatly aiding in pinpointing the cause of a failure. •&#9; Scalability for large test suites : Pytest is designed to handle small and large test suites efficiently. Its modular design, fixture system, and support for parallel execution make it suitable for projects of any size. •&#9; Integration with existing code : Pytest integrates seamlessly with existing Python projects and does not impose strict project structures. It can run tests written using the unittest framework as well, making it a flexible choice for projects transitioning to Pytest. Benefit of using Pytest with Playwright Pytest with Playwright offers a powerful combination for automating and testing web applications, particularly for browser-based testing. The following are the unique benefits of integrating Pytest, a robust Python testing framework, with Playwright, a modern browser automation library, written in a way to ensure originality: •&#9; Pytest’s simplicity : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s concise syntax and minimal boilerplate make writing tests for Playwright scripts intuitive and efficient. Unlike other testing frameworks, Pytest does not require complex class-based structures, allowing developers to focus on test logic rather than setup overhead. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright’s API for browser automation (e.g., navigating pages, interacting with elements) integrates seamlessly with Pytest’s simple function-based test definitions. This reduces the learning curve for teams automating browser tests. For example, a developer can write a Playwright test to verify a login flow in a single Pytest function, leveraging Pytest’s assert statements for validation without needing extensive configuration. •&#9; Powerful fixture : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s fixture system allows for reusable setup and teardown logic, which is particularly useful for managing Playwright’s browser instances, contexts, and pages. Fixtures ensure that browser sessions are initialized and closed efficiently, reducing resource leaks. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : It requires careful management of browser resources (e.g., launching browsers, creating contexts). Pytest fixtures can encapsulate this logic, providing a clean way to share browser instances across multiple tests or customize configurations (e.g., headless mode, browser type). For example, a Pytest fixture can automatically launch a Chromium browser instance with specific viewport settings for a suite of Playwright tests, ensuring consistent test environments. •&#9; Parallel test execution : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s pytest-xdist plugin enables parallel test execution, significantly reducing the runtime of Playwright test suites. This is critical for browser automation, where tests can be time-consuming due to network latency and rendering. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : It supports cross-browser testing (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit), and running these tests in parallel with pytest-xdist ensures faster feedback loops, especially for large test suites. For example, a continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) pipeline can run Playwright tests across multiple browsers simultaneously using pytest-xdist , cutting down execution time for end-to-end ( E2E ) tests. •&#9; Reporting and debugging : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest provides detailed test reports, including pass/fail statuses, stack traces, and custom output, which enhance debugging of Playwright tests. Plugins like pytest-html or pytest-cov further improve reporting with visual dashboards and code coverage metrics. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright’s built-in tracing and screenshot capabilities (e.g., capturing screenshots on failure) integrate well with Pytest’s reporting. Developers can quickly pinpoint issues in browser interactions, such as failed element selectors or timeouts. For example, when a Playwright test fails due to a missing element, Pytest’s verbose output combined with Playwright’s screenshot-on-failure feature helps developers diagnose the issue faster. •&#9; Easy cross-browser testing : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s parameterized testing allows developers to run the same Playwright test across multiple browsers (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) with minimal code duplication. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright’s native support for multiple browsers pairs perfectly with Pytest’s @pytest.mark.parametrize decorator, enabling seamless cross-browser test execution. For example, a single Pytest test function can be parameterized to validate a web form’s behavior across Chrome, Firefox, and Safari, ensuring a consistent user experience ( UX ). •&#9; Test filtering and markers : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s marker system and test filtering allow developers to organize and selectively run Playwright tests based on criteria like test type (e.g., UI, API), priority, or environment. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright tests often target specific scenarios (e.g., mobile emulation, authentication flows). Pytest markers enable fine-grained control, such as running only mobile-specific tests or skipping flaky tests in certain environments. &#9;For example, a developer can mark Playwright tests with @pytest.mark.smoke to run only smoke tests. •&#9; CI/CD integration : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s compatibility with CI/CD tools like Jenkins , GitHub Actions , or GitLab CI makes it easy to integrate Playwright tests into automated workflows. Pytest’s exit codes and JUnit XML output ensure compatibility with CI reporting. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright’s ability to run in headless mode or with specific configurations (e.g., slow-motion for debugging) complements Pytest’s CI-friendly features, enabling robust automated testing pipelines. For example, a GitHub Actions workflow can trigger Pytest to run Playwright tests on every pull request, generating a report for stakeholders to review test results. •&#9; Plugins and extensibility : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s vast ecosystem of plugins (e.g., pytest-playwright for Playwright-specific fixtures) extends its functionality, simplifying Playwright test setup and execution. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : The pytest-playwright plugin provides pre-built fixtures for Playwright’s browser, context, and page objects, reducing manual setup and ensuring best practices. Example use case, using the pytest-playwright plugin, a developer can access a pre-configured page fixture to write Playwright tests without manually initializing browsers. •&#9; API testing and mocking : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s flexibility allows combining Playwright’s browser automation with API testing (e.g., using requests or Playwright’s API request capabilities) within the same test suite, enabling comprehensive E2E testing. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright’s network interception features (e.g., mocking API responses) work seamlessly with Pytest’s test structure, allowing developers to simulate backend behavior during UI tests. For example, a Pytest test can use Playwright to mock an API response, then verify the UI updates correctly without relying on a live backend. •&#9; Error handling and retry mechanisms : o&#9; Benefit : Pytest’s plugins, like pytest-rerunfailures , allow automatic retries for flaky tests, which is common in browser automation due to timing issues or network instability. o&#9; Enhancing Playwright : Playwright tests can occasionally fail due to browser-specific quirks or transient issues. Pytest’s retry mechanisms help mitigate false negatives, improving test reliability. For example, a Playwright test that intermittently fails due to a slow-loading page can be configured to retry up to three times using Pytest’s rerun plugin, ensuring stable test results. Setting up Pytest with Playwright Setting up Playwright with Pytest on both Mac and Windows involves similar steps, primarily focusing on Python environment setup and package installation. The following are the prerequisites (for both Mac and Windows): •&#9; Python : Ensure you have Python 3.8 or higher installed. You can download it from the official Python website ( https://www.python.org/ ). Verify installation by running python3 --version (Mac/Linux) or python --version (Windows) in your Terminal/Command Prompt. •&#9; pip : Python&apos;s package installer, usually comes with Python. Verify by running pip3 --version (Mac/Linux) or pip --version (Windows). Here is a brief, step-by-step guide for both operating systems: 1. Mac steps : a. Open Terminal : Launch your Terminal application. b. Create a project directory : mkdir CHAPTER_2_PW_PY cd CHAPTER_2_PW_PY c. Create and activate a virtual environment (recommended) : python3 -m venv venv source venv/bin/activate In the following figure you can see virtual environment is ready: Figure 2.4 : Setup Pytest d. Install pytest-playwright with the following command: pip install pytest-playwright This command will install both pytest and playwright . In the following figure, you can see the above command integrates Playwright with Pytest. Allowing you to write browser automation tests using Playwright inside your Pytest test functions, and provides useful fixtures (like page, browser, and context): Figure 2.5 : Install pytest-playwright e. Install Playwright browsers by using the following command: playwright install The above command will download the necessary browser binaries (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) that Playwright uses. 2. Windows steps : a. Open Command Prompt or PowerShell : Search for cmd or PowerShell in your Start menu. b. Open Terminal : Launch your Terminal application. c. Create a Project directory : mkdir CHAPTER_2_PW_PY cd CHAPTER_2_PW_PY d. Create and activate a virtual environment (recommended) : python -m venv venv .\venv\Scripts\activate e. Install pytest-playwright : Use the following command: pip install pytest-playwright This command will install both pytest and playwright . f. Install Playwright browsers : Use the following command: playwright install This command downloads the necessary browser binaries (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) that Playwright uses. g. Run the command : pip list to display a list of all Python packages installed in the current environment. In the following figure, you can see a list of packages installed: Figure 2.6 : List of packages installed Writing Playwright test with Pytest Using Playwright with Python and Pytest for automated testing offers a powerful and flexible approach to ensuring the quality of web applications. This combination leverages Playwright&apos;s robust browser automation capabilities, Python&apos;s simplicity and readability, and Pytest&apos;s efficient testing framework. Without Pytest, you would likely be writing more verbose and less organized test scripts. You would need to manually manage browser and context lifecycles, which can be error-prone. Running tests in parallel would require custom scripting, and you would lack the rich reporting and plugin ecosystem that Pytest provides out of the box. We have done the step of Playwright/Pytest. The next steps are to write a script using Playwright, Python, and Pytest. Follow these steps to create a basic script in Pytest: 1. Create a folder with the name tests . 2. Under the created folder, create a file with the name basicScript.py . In the following figure, you can see that we have created basicScript.py : Figure 2.7 : Basic Python script file 3. Create a script under the created basicScript.py file. In the script below, we are verifying by the following steps: a. Navigate to the login page https://ecommerce-playground.lambdatest.io/index.php?route=account/login . b. Fill in credentials. c. Log in to the application. d. Verify a successful login by verifying the URL, the title of the page, and the text My Account visibility verification. The following code for the above test case: 1. import pytest 2. from playwright.sync_api import expect 3. EXPECTED_URL_AFTER_LOGIN = &quot; https://ecommerce-playground.lambdatest.io/index.php?route=account/account &quot; 4. def test_login(page): # Navigate to the login page 5. page.goto(&quot;https://ecommerce-playground.lambdatest.io/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) # Fill in the username and password fields 6. page.fill(&quot;[id=&apos;input-email&apos;]&quot;, &quot; timcook@yopmail.com &quot;) 7. page.fill(&quot;[id=&apos;input-password&apos;]&quot;, &quot;Test@1234&quot;) # Click the login button 8. page.click(&quot;input[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) # Check user logged-in successfully 9. expect(page).to_have_url(EXPECTED_URL_AFTER_LOGIN) 10. expect(page).to_have_title(&quot;My Account&quot;) 11. expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;heading&quot;, name=&quot;My Account&quot;)).to_be_visible() Let us go through the above code and try to understand the meaning of each line: •&#9; import pytest : This line imports the pytest library, which is used to write and run tests in Python. It helps you organize and run test functions easily. •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import expect : This imports the expect function from Playwright&apos;s synchronous API. You will use expect to check (assert) that something is true in your test, like checking if you are on the right page, etc. •&#9; def test_login(page) :This defines a test function named test_login . When you run this test, Pytest will automatically detect and run it. The page parameter is provided by Playwright’s Pytest plugin, which gives you access to a browser page to interact with it. •&#9; page.goto(&quot;https://ecommerce-playground.lambdatest.io/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) : This opens the login page in the browser when you type a URL and press Enter in your browser. page.fill(&quot;[id=&apos;input-email&apos;]&quot;, timcook@yopmail.com ) page.fill(&quot;[id=&apos;input-password&apos;]&quot;, &quot;Test@1234&quot;) The above two lines will fill the data in the email and password fields: page.click(&quot;input[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) This finds the login button using the locator type=&apos;submit&apos; and clicks on it: expect(page).to_have_url(EXPECTED_URL_AFTER_LOGIN) expect(page).to_have_title(&quot;My Account&quot;) expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;heading&quot;, name=&quot;My Account&quot;)).to_be_visible() The above three lines for the assertions after login to verify the URL is correct or not, to verify the title, and to verify the text My Account should be when the user lands on the logged-in page. Comparison of Playwright Python vs. Pytest The following table lists the comparison between using Pytest and not using Pytest: Aspect Playwright + Python Playwright + Pytest Setup Script-based, quick to start. Needs Pytest and pytest-playwright plugin. Setup/Teardown Manual with custom code. Built with setup_method , teardown_method , or fixtures. Fixtures and hooks Not available. Powerful fixture system, scope control, shared setup. Assertions Assert with conditions. Supports assert and Playwright’s expect() API. Test isolation You need to manage the session/page/browser manually. Browser, context, and page fixtures automatically manage resources. Reporting Very basic, unless integrated manually Native compatibility with pytest-html , Allure , etc. Test structure Tests are written as regular Python scripts. You manually manage browser instances, contexts, and pages using Playwright’s APIs (sync or async). Tests are written as Pytest functions, using fixtures like page, browser, or context provided by the pytest-playwright plugin for automatic resource management. Documentation and community support Good Playwright docs, but less guidance for structuring Python-only tests. Massive Pytest community and docs; standard practice in Python testing. Execution Scripts are run like any Python script: python script.py . No built-in test discovery or reporting. Tests are executed via Pytest CLI: pytest . Automatically discovers test files (e.g., test_*.py ) and functions (e.g., test_* ). Supports CLI options like --headed or --browser . Parallel execution No built-in parallel execution. You must implement multiprocessing or threading manually. Supports parallel execution via plugins like pytest-xdist or pytest-playwright built-in parallelization, speeding up test suites. Cross-browser testing Manually configure browsers (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) in scripts using playwright.chromium.launch() or similar. pytest-playwright plugin allows running tests across multiple browsers via CLI flags (e.g., pytest --browser chromium --browser firefox ). Sync vs. async Supports both sync ( playwright.sync_api ) and async ( playwright.async_api ) APIs. Async requires manual asyncio handling. pytest-playwright plugin uses the sync API by default. Async testing requires pytest-playwright-asyncio plugin and async fixtures, adding complexity. Debugging Playwright provides tools like trace recording ( context.tracing.start() ), screenshots, and videos. Debugging relies on Python’s tools (e.g., pdb ). Inherits Playwright’s debugging tools (traces, screenshots, videos) and adds Pytest’s verbose output, test filtering, and trace viewer integration for easier debugging. Table 2.1 : Comparison Playwright + Python vs. Playwrite + Pytest The following are the key considerations: •&#9; Playwright + Python : It is lightweight and flexible, suitable for ad-hoc automation or scenarios where you want full control over the script. However, it lacks test organization, reporting, and scalability for large projects. •&#9; Playwright + Pytest : It is the recommended approach for E2E testing, as it leverages Pytest’s powerful features like fixtures, test discovery, and parallel execution. It is ideal for professional testing workflows, especially in CI/CD pipelines. The following is when to choose: •&#9; Use Playwright + Python for quick prototyping, non-testing automation (e.g., web scraping), or small scripts where Pytest’s overhead is unnecessary. •&#9; Use Playwright + Pytest for robust, maintainable test suites, cross-browser testing, or projects requiring detailed reporting and integration with testing tools. Test execution with UI Mode UI Mode (also referred to as headed mode ) in the context of running tests with Pytest and Playwright refers to executing browser automation tests with a visible browser window, as opposed to headless mode, where the browser runs in the background without a graphical interface. In Playwright, UI Mode is enabled by passing the --headed flag when running Pytest, e.g., pytest test_script.py --headed --browser chromium . This allows testers to visually observe the browser’s behavior during test execution, making it particularly useful for debugging, development, and manual inspection. About UI Mode In UI Mode, Playwright launches a browser (e.g., Chromium, Firefox, or WebKit) with a visible UI, showing all interactions such as page navigation, form inputs, clicks, and assertions in real-time: •&#9; Enabling it : Using the pytest-playwright plugin, you activate UI Mode via the headed command-line flag. For example, pytest test_opencart_login.py --headed --browser chromium •&#9; Behavior : The browser window opens, and you can watch the test script perform actions like navigating to URLs, filling forms, clicking buttons, and verifying elements, as shown in the earlier OpenCart login example. •&#9; Configuration : No additional code changes are needed in the test script to enable UI Mode; it is controlled entirely through the command line. The script provided earlier (e.g., filling email/password and verifying text) works seamlessly in UI Mode. •&#9; Performance : UI Mode is slightly slower than headless mode due to rendering the browser’s graphical interface, but this is negligible for most test scenarios. Use case of UI Mode Let us take an example to understand UI Mode in detail by executing the following steps in UI Mode itself: 1. Open the login page: https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login 2. Log in using credentials : a. Email : johnpeter123@yopmail.com b. Password : Test@1234 3. Verify successful login by checking the dashboard sections: a. My Account&#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; b. My Orders c. My Affiliate Account&#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; d. Newsletter 4. Click the Naveen Automation Labs logo to navigate to the homepage. 5. On the homepage, add the MacBook product to the cart. 6. Verify the success message confirming MacBook was added to the cart . 7. Click on the cart icon (top right corner). 8. Verify the cart dropdown shows: a. Text MacBook . &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003;b. A Checkout link. 9. Click on Checkout and verify that the page shows the heading Checkout . 10. Click on the My Account menu (usually a dropdown). 11. Select and click Logout to sign out of the application. The following is the code: import pytest import re from playwright.sync_api import Page, expect def test_opencart_login(page: Page): # Open the login page page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) # Enter login credentials page.fill(&quot;input#input-email&quot;, &quot; johnpeter123@yopmail.com &quot;) page.fill(&quot;input#input-password&quot;, &quot;Test@1234&quot;) # Click on login button page.click(&quot;input[value=&apos;Login&apos;]&quot;) # Visibility check using Assertions expect(page.locator(&quot;h2:has-text(&apos;My Account&apos;)&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.locator(&quot;h2:has-text(&apos;My Orders&apos;)&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.locator(&quot;h2:has-text(&apos;My Affiliate Account&apos;)&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.locator(&quot;h2:has-text(&apos;Newsletter&apos;)&quot;)).to_be_visible() # Add Items into the cart page.click(&quot;img[title=&apos;naveenopencart&apos;]&quot;) macbook_card = page.locator(&quot;div.product-thumb&quot;).filter(has_text=&quot;MacBook&quot;) macbook_card.locator(&quot;button[onclick*=&apos;cart.add&apos;]&quot;).click() expect(page.locator(&quot;div.alert-success&quot;)).to_contain_text(&quot;Success: You have added MacBook to your shopping cart!&quot;) # Verify addded Items into the cart and proceed to checkout page.click(&quot;span#cart-total&quot;) cart_dropdown = page.locator(&quot;ul.dropdown-menu.pull-right&quot;) expect(cart_dropdown.get_by_text(&quot;MacBook&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(cart_dropdown.get_by_text(&quot;Checkout&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.wait_for_timeout(2000) cart_dropdown.get_by_text(&quot;Checkout&quot;).click() expect(page).to_have_url(re.compile(r&quot;.*/checkout.*&quot;)) expect(page.locator(&quot;h1:has-text(&apos;Checkout&apos;)&quot;)).to_be_visible() # Remove the item from the cart and verify message &quot;Your shopping cart is empty!&quot; page.wait_for_timeout(2000) page.click(&quot;span#cart-total&quot;) page.wait_for_selector(&quot;ul.dropdown-menu.pull-right&quot;, state=&quot;visible&quot;) page.click(&quot;button[title=&apos;Remove&apos;]&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;ul.dropdown-menu.pull-right&quot;)).to_contain_text(&quot;Your shopping cart is empty!&quot;) # Click &quot;My Account&quot; and Logout from the application page.locator(&quot;a[title=&apos;My Account&apos;]&quot;).click() logout_link = page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Logout&quot;) logout_link.wait_for(state=&quot;visible&quot;, timeout=5000) logout_link.click() Command to execute the above code Run the following command to execute the test case in headed/UI mode: pytest ./tests/test_opencart_login.py --headed In the following figure, you can see the test case executed in headed (UI mode): Figure 2.8 : Headed mode In the following figure, you can see the test case executed successfully: Figure 2.9 : Test case execution Benefits of UI Mode Though UI Mode is slow compared to headless mode, it still has many benefits, a few of which are explained in the following: •&#9; Visual debugging : o&#9; Benefit : You can see exactly what the browser is doing during test execution, making it easier to identify issues like incorrect selectors, timing problems, or unexpected page behavior. o&#9; Example : In the above OpenCart login test, you can watch the browser navigate to the login page, enter credentials, and check if the My Account text appears, helping you spot if the login fails or if a locator is wrong. •&#9; Interactive development : o&#9; Benefit : During test script development, UI Mode allows you to observe interactions in real-time, helping you refine selectors, verify page navigation, and ensure actions (e.g., clicks, form inputs) work as expected. o&#9; Example : While writing the OpenCart test, you can see if the page.fill(&apos;input[name=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, ...) correctly targets the email field, or if the login button click triggers the expected page transition. •&#9; Validation of UI behavior : o&#9; Benefit : UI Mode helps verify that the application’s UI behaves as intended, such as ensuring elements are visible, clickable, or rendered correctly. o&#9; Example : In the above OpenCart test, you can confirm visually that the My Account , My Orders , My Affiliate Account , and Newsletter sections are displayed properly after login. Using UI Mode Although UI Mode trades some speed for visibility, it offers multiple benefits highlighted below: •&#9; Development phase : Use UI Mode when writing or debugging test scripts to ensure actions and assertions work as intended. •&#9; Complex UI interactions : For tests involving dynamic elements (e.g., dropdowns, modals), UI Mode helps verify behavior visually. •&#9; Demonstrations : Use UI Mode to showcase test execution to stakeholders or during training sessions. •&#9; Local testing : Ideal for running tests on a developer’s machine before deploying to a CI/CD pipeline, where headless mode is typically preferred for speed. Test execution with CLI options Playwright with Pytest allows you to write and execute browser tests using a powerful CLI-based workflow. With CLI mode, you can run tests via the Terminal, generate reports, run in headless or headed mode, and integrate easily into CI pipelines. Refer to the following table: Flag Description pytest This command will run all test cases in headless mode. --browser This command will help to run the test cases in the provided browser. --headed This command will run all test cases in headed mode, where we can see the execution of test cases in the actual UI. --screenshot Capture screenshots ( on , off , only-on-failure ): •&#9; on : Screenshot captured when we pass this flag. •&#9; off : Screenshot not captured when we pass this flag. •&#9; only-on-failure : Screenshot only captured on test case failure when we pass this flag. --video This command records the videos ( on , off , only-on-failure ): •&#9; on : Video record when we pass this flag. •&#9; off : Video not recorded when we pass this flag. •&#9; only-on-failure : Video record only on test case failure. Table 2.2 : CLI options Let us see some of the examples of the above options: •&#9; Command: pytest --browser firefox : By default, tests run on Chromium. Use the --browser flag to specify browsers. This command executes the test cases on Firefox. •&#9; Command: pytest --browser webkit --browser firefox --browser chromium : To run the test cases on multiple browsers (WebKit, Chromium, Firefox), we can use this command in the Terminal. •&#9; Command: pytest --screenshot on --video on : To run the test cases on multiple browsers (WebKit, Chromium, Firefox), we can use this command in the Terminal. In the following figure, you can see a screenshot and a video recorded for both test cases: Figure 2.10 : Screenshot and video To run a single test case, pass in the name of the test case that you want to run. •&#9; Command : pytest ./tests/test_opencart_login.py To run multiple test cases, pass in the names of the test cases that you want to run. •&#9; Command : pytest ./tests/test_opencart_login.py ./tests/test_amazon_search.py Introduction to AI in automation AI is revolutionizing various industries, and software automation testing is no exception. AI in software automation testing refers to the integration of intelligent algorithms, machine learning ( ML ), and data-driven techniques to enhance the efficiency, accuracy, and speed of testing software applications. By mimicking human-like decision-making and learning capabilities, AI enables testing processes to become smarter, adaptive, and less dependent on manual intervention. This introduction explores the role of AI in software automation testing, its benefits, and a practical example to illustrate its application. Software automation Software automation testing involves using tools and scripts to test software applications for bugs, performance issues, and functionality without manual effort. Traditionally, automation testing relies on predefined scripts and rules, which can be time-consuming to create and maintain, especially for complex or frequently changing applications. AI enhances automation testing by introducing capabilities like pattern recognition, predictive analytics, and self-healing scripts. It allows testing tools to learn from data, adapt to changes, and make intelligent decisions, thereby reducing human effort and improving test coverage. AI-powered testing tools leverage techniques such as: •&#9; ML : To analyze historical test data and predict potential defects. •&#9; Natural language processing (NLP) : To interpret requirements or user stories and generate test cases. •&#9; Computer vision : To validate UI by analyzing visual elements. •&#9; Predictive analytics : To prioritize test cases based on risk or impact. The following are the benefits of AI in software automation testing: •&#9; Improved test coverage : AI can analyze vast amounts of data to identify edge cases and scenarios that manual or traditional automation might miss. •&#9; Faster test creation : AI can automatically generate test scripts by analyzing application code or requirements, reducing setup time. •&#9; Self-healing tests : AI can detect changes in the application (e.g., UI updates) and adapt test scripts accordingly, minimizing maintenance efforts. •&#9; Enhanced accuracy : AI reduces human errors by automating repetitive tasks and identifying defects with precision. •&#9; Time and cost efficiency : By automating complex tasks and prioritizing high-risk areas, AI reduces testing time and resource costs. •&#9; Scalability : AI-powered tools can handle large-scale testing for applications with frequent updates or multiple platforms. Example of AI in software automation testing Let us consider a real-world scenario involving a web-based e-commerce application that frequently updates its UI and features, such as product filters, checkout processes, and payment gateways. Consider a scenario where a QA team is tasked with testing the application’s UI to ensure buttons, forms, and navigation work as expected across different browsers and devices. Traditionally, the team writes Selenium scripts to automate UI testing. However, every time the development team updates the UI (e.g., changes a button’s ID or layout), the scripts break, requiring manual updates, which delays the testing cycle. The AI-powered solution is that the team adopts an AI-based testing tool like KaneAI , Testim , Applitools , Autify , or Robonito , which uses machine learning and computer vision to enhance automation. Some of the features of AI-powered tools are as follows: •&#9; Test case generation with NLP : The AI tool uses NLP to analyze the application’s user stories or requirements (e.g., User should be able to add a product to the cart ). It automatically generates test cases, such as clicking the Add to Cart button and verifying the cart updates. For example, the tool reads the requirement Ensure the checkout button redirects to the payment page and creates a test script to simulate the click and validate the redirect. •&#9; Self-healing scripts with ML : When the UI changes (e.g., the Add to Cart button’s ID changes from add-to-cart to cart-btn ), the AI tool detects the change by analyzing the application’s Document Object Model ( DOM ) and historical test data. Instead of failing, it updates the script to locate the button based on its text, position, or functionality. For example, the tool identifies that the button’s functionality remains the same despite the ID change and adjusts the test script automatically, saving hours of manual rework. •&#9; Visual testing with computer vision : The AI tool uses computer vision to validate the UI’s appearance across browsers. It compares screenshots of the application against a baseline and flags visual defects, such as misaligned buttons or incorrect fonts. For example, if the checkout page’s Pay Now button is misaligned on Chrome but correct on Firefox, the tool highlights the issue with a visual diff, helping developers fix it quickly. •&#9; Test prioritization with predictive analytics : The AI analyzes past test results and code changes to prioritize test cases for high-risk areas, such as the payment gateway, which is critical for the e-commerce platform. For example, if recent code changes affect the payment module, the AI tool prioritizes tests for payment flows, ensuring defects are caught early. Let us take another example of AI in software automation testing. Consider a scenario of testing a login page automatically using AI. Let us say you are working on a website that has a login page. The page looks like this: •&#9; A field to enter your username &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; A field to enter your password •&#9; A Login button &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; After login, it says: Welcome Python Traditional automation testing approach We can write the following code in Playwright. This method works well but requires programming skills and time to write and maintain. # Navigate to the login page page.goto(&quot;https://example.com/login&quot;) # Fill in username and password page.fill(&quot;#username&quot;, &quot;testUser&quot;) page.fill(&quot;#password&quot;, &quot;testPass123&quot;) # Click on the login button page.click(&quot;#login-button&quot;) # Assert that &quot;Welcome back!&quot; message appears assert page.locator(&quot;text=Welcome Python&quot;).is_visible() # Close browser browser.close() Now, comes AI-based automation. Using an AI tool, you can skip writing the above code. Instead, you just describe the test in plain English like this: Verify that users can login using valid credentials and see the welcome message The following is the working of the AI: •&#9; Understands the intent and recognizes you want to test the login flow. •&#9; Finds UI elements automatically for fields that look like username and password fields, even if the IDs or class names are different. •&#9; Fills test steps by typing values, clicking buttons, and waiting for results. •&#9; Generates actual test code behind the scenes, which you can review and run. •&#9; If the button name changes, for example, from #login-button to #sign-in , traditional test scripts would fail because they rely on exact selectors. However, AI-powered testing tools use self-healing capabilities, meaning they understand the context of the element (like its role, label, or position), not just the code. So even if the code changes, the test can still find and interact with the correct button without manual updates. Writing your first script using GenAI tool Let us see how we can write a Playwright Python script using an AI tool . In this chapter, we are covering only GitHub Copilot to write the script. Other AI tools and their usage are discussed in detail with examples in the upcoming chapters. Setup GitHub Copilot in VS Code by following these steps: 1. Install Visual Studio Code. 2. Sign in to GitHub. 3. Install GitHub Copilot extension. a. Open the EXTENSIONS sidebar in VS Code. b. Search for GitHub Copilot . c. Click Install on the official extension (by GitHub). In the following figure, you can see GitHub Copilot is installed and ready to write the prompt: Figure 2.11 : GitHub Copilot In the following figure, you can see the GitHub Copilot chat window in response to the prompt: Figure 2.12 : GitHub Copilot chat window Write script using GitHub Copilot Let us give the following prompt to the GitHub Copilot: •&#9; Navigate to the URL: https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login •&#9; Enter E-mail Address : johnpeter123@yopmail.com , Password : Test@1234 •&#9; Click on the Login button •&#9; Search text iMac •&#9; Click on Add to Cart •&#9; Open the cart •&#9; Verify product iMac is added In the following figure, you can see that the script is generated, and when we run this script, it passes successfully: Figure 2.13 : GitHub Copilot script In the following figure, you can see the executed script using GitHub Copilot: Figure 2.14 : GitHub Copilot script execution Conclusion In this chapter, you gained a solid understanding of Playwright’s core architecture and how it enables fast and reliable automation.
