  - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 5 Reports, Assertion, and Annotations in Playwright Introduction In the previous chapter, we explored how to work with various elements in Playwright, including input fields, buttons, checkboxes, dropdowns, and radio buttons. You also learned advanced interactions like multi-select dropdowns, drag-and-drop, and file uploads/downloads. The chapter covered handling inline frames ( iframes ), JavaScript alerts, shadow DOM elements, and multiple tabs, common scenarios in real-world automation. Mastering these fundamentals prepares you to interact with almost any web element reliably. In this chapter, we will shift our focus toward reporting, assertions, and annotations. You will learn about different types of reports available in Playwright and how to integrate Allure Report for detailed test insights. We will also discuss assertions, their role in verifying test outcomes, and the various assertion types supported by Playwright. Furthermore, we will discuss the annotations and demonstrate them with detailed examples to enhance test clarity. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; About reporting &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Reports in Playwright •&#9; Allure Report integration in Playwright &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; About assertion •&#9; Different type of assertion in Playwright &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Annotations with detail example Objectives The objective of this chapter is to equip learners with essential knowledge of reporting, assertions, and annotations in Playwright. Learners will understand the purpose of reports, explore Playwright’s built-in reporting features, and integrate Allure to generate detailed, interactive test reports. They will gain clarity on assertions, their role in verifying test outcomes, and learn different types of assertions to write reliable and stable tests. The chapter also introduces annotations, demonstrating how to use them effectively for controlling test behavior, adding metadata, and managing execution flow with practical examples. This chapter will equip learners with essential skills in Playwright reporting and validation. By the end, you will understand the importance of reporting in Playwright and how to configure built-in options to track test execution effectively. You will also learn to integrate and use the Allure Report framework to generate detailed, visually rich test reports for deeper analysis. The chapter further covers the concept of assertions how they work, how to apply them accurately, and how to differentiate between various assertion types to ensure strong test validation. Additionally, you will gain hands-on knowledge of Playwright’s annotation system, learning how to customize test execution behavior and apply annotations effectively through practical examples. About reporting In the past, generating test reports was often a manual or semi-automated process, requiring significant effort to produce actionable insights. Today, modern tools like Playwright have revolutionized this process by offering robust, built-in reporting capabilities. Evolution of test reporting In the early days of test automation, reporting was a labour-intensive task. Quality assurance ( QA ) engineers relied on custom scripts or basic logging mechanisms to capture test results. For example: •&#9; Manual logging : Teams used print statements or basic log files to record test outcomes, requiring manual parsing to extract meaningful data. •&#9; Framework-specific reports : Tools like Selenium required third-party libraries (e.g., ExtentReports, Allure) to generate detailed reports, involving complex setup and maintenance. •&#9; Continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) challenges : Integrating reports with CI/CD pipelines often meant writing custom parsers or adapters for tools like Jenkins , leading to inconsistent formats and additional overhead. •&#9; Limited visualizations : Reports were primarily text-based or lacked interactivity, making it difficult to share insights with stakeholders or debug failures efficiently. These approaches were time-consuming, error-prone, and lacked standardization, often requiring significant customization to meet team needs. Enter modern frameworks like Playwright, which have transformed test reporting by providing a suite of built-in, configurable reporters. These eliminate the need for external dependencies, streamline integration, and offer diverse output formats to cater to various use cases, from local debugging to enterprise-scale CI/CD pipelines. Need of test reports Test reports are essential for validating software quality, identifying defects, and ensuring reliable application performance. They serve as a comprehensive record of test execution, providing actionable insights for developers, QA engineers, and stakeholders. The key purposes of test reports include: •&#9; Result tracking : Reports document test outcomes (pass, fail, skip, or flaky), enabling teams to monitor application stability and test suite reliability. •&#9; Debugging and root cause analysis : Detailed error logs, stack traces, and attachments (e.g., screenshots, videos) help pinpoint the cause of test failures. •&#9; QA : Reports validate whether the application meets functional and non-functional requirements, ensuring alignment with business objectives. •&#9; Stakeholder communication : Clear, visual reports facilitate collaboration with non-technical stakeholders, such as product managers or clients, by presenting results in an accessible format. •&#9; CI/CD integration : Standardized reports (e.g., JSON, JUnit) enable seamless integration with CI systems, supporting automated workflows and trend analysis. •&#9; Compliance and auditing : In regulated industries, reports provide evidence of testing coverage and compliance with quality standards. Benefits of test reports The advantages of robust test reporting, particularly with tools like Playwright, are significant. These benefits address the shortcomings of past practices and enhance testing efficiency: •&#9; Improved visibility : o&#9; Description : Reports provide a clear, structured view of test execution, including pass/fail rates, execution times, and error details. o&#9; Impact : Enables quick identification of problematic areas, reducing the time spent sifting through logs. For example, Playwright’s Hypertext Markup Language ( HTML ) reporter offers an interactive interface with screenshots and videos, enhancing visibility into test failures. o&#9; Contrast with past : Unlike manual logs, modern reports aggregate data automatically, eliminating the need for custom parsing. •&#9; Enhanced debugging : o&#9; Description : Detailed reports include stack traces, error messages, and attachments, making it easier to diagnose and resolve issues. o&#9; Impact : Accelerates root cause analysis, particularly for complex failures. Playwright’s List and HTML reporters, for instance, provide granular details for local debugging. o&#9; Contrast with past : Earlier text-based logs lacked context, requiring engineers to manually correlate errors with test steps. •&#9; Seamless CI/CD integration : o&#9; Description : Standardized formats like JSON and JUnit integrate effortlessly with CI/CD tools (e.g., Jenkins , GitLab ), enabling automated result processing and trend analysis. o&#9; Impact : Supports continuous testing by providing consistent, machine-readable outputs. Playwright’s JSON and JUnit reporters are optimized for such integrations. o&#9; Contrast with past : Custom adapters or scripts were needed to integrate reports with CI systems, increasing maintenance overhead. •&#9; Time and effort savings : o&#9; Description : Built-in reporters eliminate the need for external libraries or custom reporting solutions, streamlining setup and execution. o&#9; Impact : Reduces configuration time and maintenance, allowing QA engineers to focus on test development. Playwright’s reporters are ready out-of-the-box, configurable via a single command or file. o&#9; Contrast with past : Third-party tools required significant setup and ongoing updates, diverting focus from core testing tasks. •&#9; Stakeholder collaboration : o&#9; Description : Visual and interactive reports, such as Playwright’s HTML reporter, present results in a user-friendly format accessible to non-technical audiences. o&#9; Impact : Facilitates clear communication of test outcomes, ensuring alignment across teams. Stakeholders can explore detailed results without needing technical expertise. o&#9; Contrast with past : Text-based or raw logs were difficult for non-technical stakeholders to interpret, often requiring additional summarization. •&#9; Flexibility and customization : o&#9; Description : Playwright supports multiple reporters (e.g., List, Dot, Line, JSON, JUnit, HTML) and custom reporter development, catering to diverse use cases. o&#9; Impact : Allows teams to tailor reporting to specific needs, such as minimalistic CI outputs or detailed local reports. Custom reporters enable integration with proprietary tools or workflows. o&#9; Contrast with past : Custom reporting required extensive scripting and was less integrated with the testing framework. •&#9; Scalability : o&#9; Description : Playwright’s reporters scale efficiently for large test suites, handling thousands of tests without performance degradation. o&#9; Impact : Supports enterprise-grade testing by providing consistent, reliable outputs for complex projects. o&#9; Contrast with past : Manual or semi-automated reporting struggled to scale, often resulting in incomplete or delayed insights. Reports in Playwright Playwright reports are like scoreboards for your tests. They show you what happened when you ran tests on a website, like which tests passed, failed, or took too long. These reports help developers figure out what is working and what needs fixing. Here are the different kinds of reports Playwright can create, explained in a way that is easy to understand: •&#9; List reporter : o&#9; What it does : This is the default report when you run tests (unless you are using a CI system, or CI, where it switches to another type). It is like a checklist that shows each test’s name, whether it passed or failed, and how long it took. o&#9; Why it is useful : It is super clear, like reading a report card. You can see every test’s status one by one, which is great for figuring out exactly where something went wrong. o&#9; Example : If you run 10 tests, it might show something like: &#9; Test 1 : ✓ Passed (0.5 seconds) &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; &#9; Test 2 : ✗ Failed (0.8 seconds) o&#9; When to use it : Best for when you are running tests on your computer and want to see detailed results right in the Terminal. •&#9; Dot reporter : o&#9; What it does : This one is super simple, it shows a dot (.) for each test that passes, an F for failed tests, and other symbols for things like skipped tests. It is like a quick glance at your game progress without all the details. o&#9; Why it is useful : It is short and sweet, perfect for when you do not want a ton of text clogging up your screen. It is the default when running tests on CI systems (like in a team’s automated setup). o&#9; Example : A test run might look like: ....F.. (6 tests passed, 1 failed). o&#9; When to use it : Great for quick checks or when you are running lots of tests and want less clutter. •&#9; Line reporter : o&#9; What it does : Similar to the dot reporter, but instead of dots, it uses lines (—) to show test progress. It is like a progress bar in a game, giving you a visual sense of how things are going. o&#9; Why it is useful : It is a bit more stylish than dots and still keeps things simple. o&#9; Example : Might look like: ———F—— (3 passed, 1 failed, 3 more passed). o&#9; When to use it : Good for when you want a clean, minimal report but with a different vibe than dots. •&#9; HTML reporter : o&#9; What it does : This creates a fancy, interactive webpage with all your test results. It is like a leaderboard with extra details, including screenshots, videos, and error messages if something went wrong. o&#9; Why it is useful : It is super visual, like watching a replay of your game. You can click around to see what happened in each test, making it easier to debug. o&#9; Example : After running tests, you get an HTML file you can open in a browser to explore results. o&#9; When to use it : Awesome for sharing results with your team or when you need to dig deep into why a test failed. In the next section, you will see all reports in detail with example, and how to execute these reports in actual in Pytest. List reporter List reporter generates a comprehensive, itemized output of test results, displaying each test case or module along with its outcome (e.g., passed, failed, error, or skipped). It provides a detailed summary for easy tracking of individual test statuses. How to run list report: Command : pytest -v Purpose : Prints a detailed list of each test with file name, function name, and result. Use case : Best for debugging or when you want to track exactly which tests passed/failed. Example output : In the following example, you can see that we have test cases that we run with the above command are executed successfully, and list reporter generates a comprehensive, itemized output of test results, displaying each test case or module along with its outcome: Figure 5.1 : List report Dot reporter Dot reporter is an ultra-compact reporting style, using single characters to represent test outcomes (e.g., P for passed, F for failed, E for error, S for skipped). It prioritizes minimalism and visual clarity. How to run list report: Command : pytest tests/*.py -q Purpose : Suitable for quick result overviews, particularly in CI systems or when detailed output is unnecessary. Use case : Ideal for frequent test runs or CI/CD pipelines where concise output is preferred over detailed reports. Example output : In the following example, you can see we have test cases that we run with above command are executed successfully and dot report is generates. Dots (.) indicate the number of passed tests: Figure 5.2 : Dot report Line reporter The line reporter provides a concise summary of test results, typically condensing the outcome of each test module or file into a single line. How to run list report: Command : pytest -q -rA Purpose : Minimal inline progress (like dot) but includes extra summary lines after running for all tests (passed, failed, skipped). Use case : Good balance concise run view but still gives one-line summaries at the end. Example output : In the following example, you can see we have test cases that we run with above command are executed successfully and line reporter display inline progress ( like dot ) but also includes summary lines after running for all tests (passed, failed, skipped): Figure 5.3 : Line report In the above screenshot, dots (.) indicate passed tests, F denotes a failure, and other symbols (e.g., S for skipped) may appear, with each line representing a module. HTML reporter The HTML reporter in Playwright generates a visual, interactive report of test execution results in an HTML file. It includes details like test status (pass/fail), duration, errors, screenshots, videos, and traces, making it easy to analyze test outcomes. To achieve HTML report in Playwright, Python we have to install the plugin pytest-html . The following are the steps: 1. Install pytest-html is a plugin for Pytest that generates a HTML report for test results. 2. Configure Playwright’s HTML reporter to generate a detailed report for tests. 3. Run tests and view the report to debug failures and share results. Command : pip install pytest-html In the following screenshot, you can see pytest-html plugin is installed. After installation, you can generate an HTML report by running your tests with the --html option, specifying the desired output file name: Figure 5.4 : Pytest-html plugin Execute the report : To generate the .html file we have to run the given command with providing the path of test cases. Command : pytest tests/*.py -s -v --headed --html=pwreport.html The preceding command executes the test cases in headed mode and finally generate the .html report. pwreport.html file is created. In the following screenshot, you can see all test cases are executed with 7 failed, 7 passed, 3 skipped, 2x failed. We can filter the report with status failed, passed, skipped, expected failures ted failures, unexpected passes, errors. Figure 5.5 : HTML report In the following screenshot, you can see the failed test cases list with log: Figure 5.6 : Failed Html report In the following screenshot, you can see the, passed test cases list: Figure 5.7 : Passed HTML report Allure Report integration in Playwright Allure is an open-source reporter that transforms raw test results into comprehensive reports with features like test categorization, step-by-step execution details, attachments (e.g., screenshots), and historical trends. Integrating Allure Reports with Playwright in Python enhances test automation by providing detailed, interactive, and visually appealing test reports. In Playwright with Python to generate the Allure Report we use allure-pytest plugin that hooks into test runs and automatically collects test execution data (status, steps, logs, attachments, etc.). Why should you use Allure Reports with Playwright Playwright is a robust automation tool for end-to-end ( E2E ) testing across multiple browsers. While it offers built-in reporters (e.g., list, JSON, HTML), Allure Reports stand out by offering: •&#9; Interactive user interface (UI) : Rich dashboards with collapsible sections, filters, and visual charts. •&#9; Detailed insights : Step-by-step test execution, parameters, and attachments like screenshots or logs. •&#9; Team collaboration : Easy-to-share HTML reports that do not require deep technical knowledge. •&#9; CI/CD integration : Seamless integration with tools like Jenkins or GitHub Actions for continuous testing. Steps to integrate Allure Report in Playwright The following are the steps for how to integrate Allure Report with Playwright: Step 1 : Install Allure Report using plugin allure-pytest : Run the command : pip install allure-pytest In the following figure, you can see allure-pytest install successfully: Figure 5.8 : Allure-pytest plugin Step 2 : Install allure-commandline (in case it was not installed previously): Run the command : npm install -g allure-commandline --save-dev Step 3 : Execute the test cases: Run the command : pytest --alluredir=allure-results tests/*.py In the following figure, you can see folder with name allure-results is created with .json files for the executed test cases: Figure 5.9 : Allure result Step 4 : Generate the report. Run the command : allure generate ./allure-results –clean As we execute the command folder with name allure-report is created. In the following figure, you can see folder with name allure-report : Figure 5.10 : Allure Report Step 5 : Open the report. Run the command : allure open allure-report In the following figure, you can see report is generated. Here is a screenshot for the allure dashboard: Figure 5.11 : Allure dashboard In the following figure, you can see suites wise report where you can see some test cases are passed, some skipped, and some are broken: Figure 5.12 : Allure Report results About assertion When you write automated tests, simply clicking buttons or filling forms on a website is not enough. You need to make sure the website does what you expect—like showing the right text, displaying elements, or navigating to the correct page. This is where assertions come in. Think of assertions as checkpoints in your test: they verify that the website’s behavior matches your expectations. Without assertions, your tests might run but will not tell you if something went wrong. In Playwright with Python, assertions are powered by the expect API. This simple yet powerful tool lets you check if elements are visible, contain specific text, have certain attributes, or meet other conditions. These checks ensure that your application’s UI and behavior align with what you designed. Assertions are like a quality control step in your tests. They confirm that: •&#9; The right content appears (e.g., a welcome message after login). •&#9; Elements are in the correct state (e.g., a button is clickable). •&#9; The website navigates as expected (e.g., to a dashboard after login). •&#9; Errors or unexpected behaviors do not occur. Without assertions, you are just hoping the website works, which defeats the purpose of automation. Playwright’s expect API makes it easy to add these checkpoints, with methods like to_be_visible() , to_have_text() , to_have_url() , and negative assertions like not_to_contain_text() or not_to_be_disabled() . Let us discuss how Playwright’s expect API works. The expect API is built into Playwright’s testing framework and works with locators (ways to find elements, like get_by_role() or get_by_text() ). It automatically waits for conditions to be met, so you do not need to add manual delays. For example, expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;Welcome Python&quot;)).to_be_visible() waits for the text to appear. If it does not, the test fails with a clear error message. This auto-waiting makes tests reliable and reduces flakiness. Different type of assertion in Playwright Playwright provides different kinds of assertions to validate application behavior during test automation. In this section, we will highlight the most commonly used assertions that are particularly helpful in real-world automation scenarios. 1. Element visibility assertions : Check things about a specific element. # Element is visible: expect(page.locator(&quot;h1.page-title&quot;)).to_be_visible() # Button is enabled, hidden: expect(page.locator(&quot;button.add-to-cart&quot;)).to_be_enabled() from playwright.sync_api import Page, expect def test_element_visibility(page: Page): page.goto(&quot;https://example.com&quot;) # Assert element is visible expect(page.locator(&quot;#login-button&quot;)).to_be_visible() # Assert element is hidden expect(page.locator(&quot;#error-message&quot;)).to_be_hidden() 2. URL, page assertions : Validate navigation and whole page: def test_page_assertions(page: Page): page.goto(&quot; https://example.com/login&quot; ) # Assert current URL expect(page).to_have_url(&quot;https://example.com/login&quot;) # Assert page title expect(page).to_have_title(&quot;Login - Example Site&quot;) 3. Attribute assertions : Check element attributes to validate proper HTML structure and values: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_ContextAsseration(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login”) expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) browser.close() 4. Text assertions : Verify the text content of elements to ensure correct information is displayed: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_ContextAsseration(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php&quot;, wait_until=&quot;load&quot;) # Assert element has specific inner text expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;)).to_have_text(&quot;Login&quot;) browser.close() 5. Count assertions : Verify the number of elements matching a selector: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_Login(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) # Click the login button login_button.click() page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Shop Now&quot;, exact=True).first.click() # Assert specific count of products expect(page.locator(&quot;.product-item&quot;)).to_have_count(20) browser.close() 6. Negative assertions : Negative assertions in Playwright, like not_to_be_disabled() , not_to_contain_text() , not_to_have_value() , and not_to_be_empty() , are used to verify that certain conditions are not true. These are critical for testing failure scenarios or ensuring elements do not have unexpected states. Here, we provide clear examples using https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ and https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ to demonstrate how these negative assertions work in real-world: •&#9; Using not_to_be_disabled() : This assertion checks that an element (like a button or input field) is not disabled, meaning it can be interacted with. Let us test the login button on shop.qaautomationlabs.com to ensure it is active before submitting the form. In the following code, we navigate to the login page and checks that the Login button is not disabled. If the button is disabled (e.g., due to a form validation issue), the assertion fails, ensuring the button is ready for user interaction: from playwright.sync_api import expect def test_login_button_enabled(page): page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php&quot;) login_button = page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;) expect(login_button).not_to_be_disabled() •&#9; Using not_to_contain_text() : This assertion verifies that an element does not contain specific text. Let us test that an error message does not appear after a successful login on https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ : def test_successful_login_no_error(page): page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;E-Mail Address&quot;).fill(&quot;johnpeter123@yopmail.com&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Password&quot;).fill(&quot;Test@1234&quot;) page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;).click() expect(page.locator(&quot;body&quot;)).not_to_contain_text(&quot;Warning: No match for E-Mail Address&quot;) •&#9; Using not_to_have_value() : This assertion checks that an input field does not have a specific value. Let us test that the email field on https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ is cleared after an invalid login attempt, assuming the form resets. def test_email_field_cleared_after_invalid_login(page): page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Email&quot;).fill(&quot;wrong@demo.com&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Password&quot;).fill(&quot;wrongpass&quot;) page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;).click() # Assuming the form resets the email field on failure expect(page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Email&quot;)).not_to_have_value(&quot;wrong@demo.com&quot;) •&#9; Using not_to_be_empty() : This assertion ensures that a locator (e.g., a list of elements) is not empty, meaning it contains at least one element. Let us test that a product search on https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ returns results: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_ContextAsseration(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=common/home”) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Search&quot;).fill(&quot;MacBook&quot;) #expect(page.locator(&quot;.product-layout&quot;)).not_to_be_empty() macbook_product = page.locator(&quot;.product-layout&quot;).filter(has_text=&quot;MacBook&quot;) expect(macbook_product).not_to_be_empty() In the next section, we will see some examples with assertions we discussed. Example 1 : Let us take an example of https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login , where we login into the application and verify user is successfully logged in with various assertions. In the following code we are verifying login functionality with applying various assertions: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_Login(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login”) # Verify login form elements are present expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-password&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;)).to_be_enabled() # Fill login credentials page.fill(&quot;#input-email&quot;, &quot; johnpeter123@yopmail.com &quot;) page.fill(&quot;#input-password&quot;, &quot;Test@1234&quot;) # Verify input values expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_value(&quot;johnpeter123@yopmail.com&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-password&quot;)).to_have_value(&quot;Test@1234&quot;) # Submit login page.click(&quot;input[value=&apos;Login&apos;]&quot;) # Verify successful login by checking account page elements expect(page).to_have_url(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/account&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;#content&quot;)).to_contain_text(&quot;My Account&quot;) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see test cases passed with successfully executing various assertions: Figure 5.13 : Assertion pass -01 Example 2 : Let us take an example of https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php , where we are applying multiple assertions to validate key functionalities. These include verifying the login process, checking the item count before and after adding a product, and confirming through assertions that the added product, Black T-Shirt , reflects a quantity of exactly one: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_AddProduct(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) # Assert button is visible and enabled expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() # Click the login button login_button.click() # Verify initial cart is empty expect(page.locator(&quot;#cartCount&quot;)).to_have_text(&quot;0&quot;) # Click Shop Now under Men Fashion page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Shop Now&quot;, exact=True).first.click() # Add Black T-Shirt to cart page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Black T-Shirt&quot;).click() page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Add to Cart&quot;).click() # Verify cart count updated expect(page.locator(&quot;#cartCount&quot;)).to_have_text(&quot;1&quot;) # Click Cart icon page.locator(&quot;#cartdesk&quot;).click() # Verify Black T-Shirt is in cart summary assert page.locator(&quot;#cartTable&quot;).inner_text().count(&quot;Black T-Shirt&quot;) &gt; 0 cart_table = page.locator(&quot;#cartTable&quot;) # Wait until cart table is visible expect(cart_table).to_be_visible(timeout=30000) # Assertion: verify table text includes &quot;Black T-Shirt&quot; expect(cart_table).to_contain_text(&quot;Black T-Shirt&quot;) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see test cases passed with successfully executing various assertions: Figure 5.14 : Assertion pass -02 Best practices for using assertions in Playwright Python assertions are key to making sure your web tests check if an application works correctly. In Playwright with Python, the expect() function makes it easy to write tests that check things like whether a button is visible or a page has the right URL. To make your tests strong, easy to maintain, and reliable, follow these simple best practices. We will use examples from https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ and https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ to show how they work. Below are some of the best practices for using assertions. 1. Use clear and smart locators : Playwright has easy-to-use locators like get_by_role() or get_by_text() that work well even if the website’s design changes. Avoid using tricky CSS selectors like. Button-class because they can break easily. For example, to check if the login button is visible on https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ : from playwright.sync_api import expect def test_login_button(page): page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php&quot;) expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;)).to_be_visible() This method is simple and keeps your tests working even if the website updates. 2. Let Playwright wait automatically : Playwright’s assertions wait for elements to appear or change (like becoming visible) for up to 30 seconds. Do not add manual waits like page.wait_for_timeout() unless you really need to, as they can make tests unreliable. For example, to check a welcome message after logging into https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ : def test_login_message(page): page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;E-Mail Address&quot;).fill(&quot;johnpeter123@yopmail.com&quot;) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Password&quot;).fill(&quot;Test@1234&quot;) page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;).click() expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;My Account&quot;)).to_be_visible() # Waits automatically This ensures the test only passes when the element is ready. 3. Do not check too much or too little : Only test what is important for your test case. Checking too many things can make tests flaky while checking too little might miss problems. For example, when testing a search on https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ : Focus on key results, not small details like button colors, unless they matter: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_ContextAsseration(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=common/home”) page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Search&quot;).fill(&quot;MacBook&quot;) page.locator(&quot;.input-group-btn&quot;).click() expect(page.locator(&quot;.product-layout&quot;)).to_have_count(3) # Check number of results expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;MacBook&quot;).first).to_be_visible() # Check product name browser.close() 4. Use negative assertions : Normally, we use positive assertion it is a good practice if we use some negative assertion as well during our automation. It is just as important to verify that something is not present as it is to verify that something is present. Use the .not chainable method for this: submit_button = page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Submit&quot;) spinner = page.locator(&quot;.spinner&quot;) submit_button.click() # Assert that the spinner is no longer visible expect(spinner).not.to_be_visible() 5. Custom messages for faster debugging : When a test fails, a clear error message can save you a lot of time. The expect() function allows you to add a custom message that will be displayed in the test report if the assertion fails. In the following code, we have added the custom message that going to help in debugging when we have n number of tests case: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_ContextAsseration(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login”) expect(page, &quot;The page title did not match the expected value.&quot;).to_have_title(&quot;Account Login1&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) browser.close() Output : In the following figure, you can see the custom message display in console which going to help when we run our test cases in CI/CD as well: Figure 5.15 : Custom message Annotations with detailed examples Annotations in Playwright for Python are a powerful feature used primarily in testing to add metadata, control test execution, or provide additional context to test cases. They allow developers to mark tests with specific behaviors, such as skipping tests, marking them as flaky, or assigning custom attributes for better test organization and reporting. Annotations in Playwright Annotations in Playwright are special markers or decorators applied to test functions to influence their behavior or provide metadata. They are part of the Playwright test runner ( pytest-playwright ), which is built on top of the pytest framework. Annotations help customize test execution, such as skipping tests under certain conditions, marking tests as expected to fail, or grouping tests for selective execution. Common annotations include: •&#9; @pytest.mark.skip : Skip a test entirely. •&#9; @pytest.mark.xfail : Mark a test as expected to fail. •&#9; @pytest.mark.only : Run only the annotated test. •&#9; @pytest.mark.custom : Create custom metadata for tests. In Python/Playwright, there are various way to implement the annotation but here we are implementing annotations through Pytest markers. They provide a clean and flexible way to tag tests, run subsets, control execution flow, and manage test suites similar to annotations in Java/C# testing frameworks. Markers are special labels you can attach to test functions. They allow you to group, skip, or parametrize tests. In the context of Playwright, they are especially useful for organizing browser tests, running subsets of tests, or handling different environments. Built-in marker These come out-of-the-box with Pytest. Some of the built-in marker are as follows: •&#9; @pytest.mark.skip : Always skip a test: @pytest.mark.skip(reason=&quot;Not ready yet&quot;) def test_feature(page): ... •&#9; @pytest.mark.skipif(condition, reason=&quot;...&quot;) : Skip only if a condition is true: import sys @pytest.mark.skipif(sys.platform == &quot;win32&quot;, reason=&quot;Does not run on Windows&quot;) def test_linux_only(page): ... •&#9; @pytest.mark.xfail : Expected failure (will not break CI if it fails): @pytest.mark.xfail(reason=&quot;Known bug, waiting for fix&quot;) def test_known_issue(page): ... Custom marker You can define your own categories for tests (like annotations in Java/C#). For a custom marker, we have to create an .ini file at the root level, and in that file, we can keep all markers. In below example you can see the example of various markers smoke, sanity, regression, slow etc. pytest.ini: [pytest] markers = smoke: quick checks regression: full regression suite slow: performance or long-running tests Use of marker : @pytest.mark.smoke def test_login(page): ... @pytest.mark.regression def test_checkout(page): ... Example of built-in marker So far, we have seen just a high-level of overview of marker. Now, let us see some practical examples of marker. In the following code you can see we have used built-in marker skip , xfail , skipif , skip_browser , only_browser : import pytest import sys from playwright.sync_api import expect @pytest.mark.skip(reason=&quot;Feature is under development&quot;) def test_homepage_loads_testCase(page): page.goto(&quot;https://qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) assert page.title() == &quot;Home - QA Automation Labs&quot; @pytest.mark.xfail(reason=&quot;Known bug in checkout flow&quot;) def test_annotation_fail_testCase(page): page.goto(&quot;https://qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) assert page.title() == &quot;Home - QA Automation Labs1&quot; @pytest.mark.skipif(sys.platform == &quot;darwin&quot;, reason=&quot;does not run on mac&quot;) def test_annotation_skipIf_testCase(page): # Use the pytest-playwright `page` fixture — no sync_playwright() here page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) # assert title expect(page, &quot;The page title did not match the expected value.&quot;).to_have_title(&quot;Account Login&quot;) # check email field attribute expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) @pytest.mark.skip_browser(&quot;firefox&quot;, reason=&quot;Firefox doesn’t support this feature&quot;) def test_annotation_skipBrowser_testCase(page): # Use the pytest-playwright `page` fixture — no sync_playwright() here page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) # assert title expect(page, &quot;The page title did not match the expected value.&quot;).to_have_title(&quot;Account Login&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) @pytest.mark.only_browser(&quot;firefox&quot;, reason=&quot;Run only in Firefox&quot;) def test_annotation_onlyBrowser_testCase(page): # Use the pytest-playwright `page` fixture — no sync_playwright() here page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) # assert title expect(page, &quot;The page title did not match the expected value.&quot;).to_have_title(&quot;Account Login&quot;) # check email field attribute expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) Let us execute the preceding code using the following command: Command : pytest tests/annotations.py -v In the following figure, you can see we have run the test cases in chrome browser: Figure 5.16 : In-built marker Here is why each result is what it is: •&#9; test_homepage_loads_testCase[chromium] SKIPPED : o&#9; We explicitly marked this wit h @pytest.mark.skip(reason=&quot;Feature is under development&quot;) . o&#9; Correct : Pytest skips it. •&#9; test_annotation_fail_testCase[chromium] XFAIL : o&#9; We marked with @pytest.mark.xfail(reason=&quot;Known bug in checkout flow&quot;) . o&#9; The assertion assert page.title() == &quot;Home - QA Automation Labs1&quot; is intentionally wrong. o&#9; Correct : Pytest marks it as expected failure ( xfail ). •&#9; test_annotation_skipIf_testCase[chromium] SKIPPED : o&#9; We have used @pytest.mark.skipif(sys.platform == &quot;darwin&quot;, reason=&quot;does not run on mac&quot;) . &#9; On macOS : It is skipped. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; &#9; On other OS : It would run. o&#9; Since we run the test case in Mac, the test run shows SKIPPED , and this is expected. •&#9; test_annotation_skipBrowser_testCase[chromium] PASSED : o&#9; Decorated with @pytest.mark.skip_browser(&quot;firefox&quot;, reason=&quot;Firefox doesn’t support this feature&quot;) . o&#9; We have run on chromium , so it is not skipped. o&#9; Correct : It passed. •&#9; test_annotation_onlyBrowser_testCase[chromium] SKIPPED : o&#9; Decorated with @pytest.mark.only_browser(&quot;firefox&quot;, reason=&quot;Run only in Firefox &quot;). o&#9; We have run test cases on chromium, so it is skipped. o&#9; Correct : It did not run. Example of custom marker Custom marker are labels you add to your tests to organize them better. Think of them like tags on emails, they help you find and run specific types of tests quickly. Instead of running all 100 tests every time, you can run: •&#9; Only the important tests when you are in a hurry. •&#9; Only the slow tests overnight. •&#9; Only the login tests when you change authentication code. You can create custom marker to suit your project&apos;s specific needs. The following are some of the examples: •&#9; @pytest.mark.smoke : For smoke tests that verify basic functionality. •&#9; @pytest.mark.regression : For regression tests that ensure existing features work. •&#9; @pytest.mark.api : For API-related tests. •&#9; @pytest.mark.ui : For user interface tests. Now, let us take an example to explain some of the markers, but before creating the test case, we have to create pytest.ini at the root level. Pytest.ini : Mention all the markers under .ini file. In the following figure, you can see we have added all marker under .ini file. Figure 5.17 : pytest.ini The following are the example of markers: import pytest from playwright.sync_api import expect # Base URL and credentials BASE_URL = &quot; https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php &quot; EMAIL = &quot; demo@demo.com &quot; PASSWORD = &quot;demo&quot; @pytest.mark.smoke def test_website_smoke_test(page): &quot;&quot;&quot;Most basic test - can we even load the website?&quot;&quot;&quot; page.goto(BASE_URL) page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=account/login&quot;) expect(page, &quot;The page title did not match the expected value.&quot;).to_have_title(&quot;Account Login&quot;) expect(page.locator(&quot;#input-email&quot;)).to_have_attribute(&quot;type&quot;, &quot;text&quot;) print(&quot;Website loaded successfully&quot;) @pytest.mark.sanity def test_website_sanity_test(page): &quot;&quot;&quot;Can we login with demo account?&quot;&quot;&quot; page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php&quot;) page.goto(BASE_URL) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;email&apos;]&quot;, EMAIL) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;password&apos;]&quot;, PASSWORD) page.click(&apos;#loginBtn&apos;) # Check if login worked - look for a logout button or user menu page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) # If we can see navigation, login probably worked nav = page.locator(&quot;nav&quot;).first expect(nav).to_be_visible() print(&quot;Login works!&quot;) @pytest.mark.regression def test_website_regression_test(page): &quot;&quot;&quot;After login, check all menu items are there&quot;&quot;&quot; page.goto(BASE_URL) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;email&apos;]&quot;, EMAIL) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;password&apos;]&quot;, PASSWORD) page.click(&apos;#loginBtn&apos;) menu_items = [&quot;Home&quot;, &quot;About&quot;, &quot;Courses&quot;, &quot;Events&quot;, &quot;Shop&quot;, &quot;Testing&quot;, &quot;Blog&quot;, &quot;Contact&quot;] for item in menu_items: menu_link = page.locator(&quot;#navbarCollapse&quot;).get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=item) expect(menu_link).to_be_visible() print(f&quot;Found {item} in menu&quot;) Executing the test case Now, let us execute the above test cases: Command executes smoke test : pytest tests/customAnnotation.py -m smoke –v in the following figure, you can see smoke test cases are executed only: Figure 5.18 : Smoke test Command executes regression test : pytest tests/customAnnotation.py -m regression –v In the following figure, you can see regression test cases are executed only: Figure 5.19 : Regression test So, this way we can execute the selected test cases instead of executing all the test cases. Conclusion In this chapter, we gained a clear understanding of how Playwright supports reporting, assertions, and annotations to build reliable and maintainable test automation. Playwright’s built-in reporters, such as HTML and JSON, provide quick feedback on test execution with useful artifacts like screenshots, videos, and traces. For advanced reporting needs, integrating Allure Reports delivers detailed dashboards, historical tracking, and categorization, making results easier to analyze and share with the team. In the next chapter, we will explore key debugging techniques in Playwright that improve test stability and reliability. Codegen accelerates script creation by recording user actions, while the Playwright Inspector enables step-by-step execution and selector exploration. The Trace Viewer provides detailed insights into failed tests through DOM snapshots, network logs, and replay options. Additional debugging methods, such as breakpoints, slow motion, and verbose logging, further enhance analysis. With the rise of AI debugging, teams gain faster error analysis and suggested fixes.
