You will learn to how manually we can identify elements using different selector types such as CSS and XPath, and perform common user interactions like clicking, typing, filling forms, and selecting options. Additionally, you will explore effective waiting strategies to handle dynamic elements and reduce flaky tests, along with implementing various types of assertions to validate application behavior and ensure reliable test outcomes. About selector Selector is a way to identify and locate specific elements (like text fields, buttons, links, or images) on a webpage or app so your automation script can interact with them. Think of a selector as an address or a unique ID that tells your automation tool (like Selenium, Playwright, or Cypress) exactly which element to verify, type, and click. Normally, selectors are used to: •&#9; Find elements on a webpage (e.g., a Submit button or search text box). •&#9; Perform actions like clicking, typing, or checking if the element is visible. •&#9; Validate that the element behaves as expected (e.g., a button changes color when clicked). Let us try to understand selectors with an analogy. Analogy to find a book in a library Imagine you are in a library (the webpage) filled with thousands of books (HTML elements like buttons, checkboxes, radio buttons, or links). Your automation script is like a librarian who needs to find a specific book to perform an action, like checking it out or reading a page. A selector is like the instructions you give the librarian to locate that exact book. Here is how it works: •&#9; Library = Webpage : The webpage is the library, with tons of elements (books) organized in a structure (sections, shelves, etc.) •&#9; Book = Element : Each book represents an element, like a button or text field. •&#9; Selector = Instructions : The selector is the specific instruction you give the librarian to find the book. For example: o&#9; ID selector : Find the book with the catalog number #12345 . (Unique and precise, like id=submit-btn ). o&#9; Class name selector : Find all books on the Mystery shelf. (Targets multiple books with the same class, like class=btn ). o&#9; CSS selector : Find the book in the third row of the second shelf in the Fiction section. (More detailed, like div.section | a.link ). o&#9; XPath : Find the book by following this path: go to the fiction section, then the second shelf, then the third book. (Like //div[@class=&apos;section&apos;]//a ). o&#9; Link text : Find the book titled Click Me . (Like a link with exact text). If your instructions (selector) are clear and unique, the librarian (automation tool) quickly finds the book (element) and performs the action (e.g., clicks the button). If the instructions are vague or incorrect, the librarian might grab the wrong book or fail to find it, causing your test to fail. The following are some of the common selectors that normally we use to locate elements in the webpage: •&#9; ID selector : A unique identifier for an element (e.g., id=login-button ). •&#9; Class name selector : Targets elements with a specific class (e.g., class=btn-primary ). •&#9; Name selector : Uses the element’s name attribute (e.g., name=username ). •&#9; Tag name selector : Targets elements by their HTML tag (e.g., input , div ). •&#9; CSS selector : Uses CSS patterns to locate elements (e.g., div.header | a ). •&#9; XPath selector : Uses a path-like syntax to navigate the HTML structure (e.g., //input[@type=&apos;text&apos;] ). •&#9; Link text/partial link text selector : Targets links by their text (e.g., Click here ). In the following figure, you can see different types of locators that normally we use during automating any application: Figure 3.1 : Various selectors Importance of selectors Selectors are critical in web automation testing, including with tools like Playwright in Python, because they enable precise identification and interaction with web elements (e.g., buttons, checkboxes, dropdowns) on a webpage. Their importance arises from their role in ensuring reliable, efficient, and maintainable test scripts. The following are some of the points that help you to know why selectors are essential when you are testing any web application. •&#9; Accurate element identification : Selectors (e.g., CSS, XPath, or Playwright’s role-based locators) pinpoint specific web elements in the DOM. For example, to interact with a checkbox or dropdown, you need a selector like input[type=&apos;checkbox&apos;] or select#dropdown to locate it accurately. Without precise selectors, automation scripts may interact with the wrong element or fail, leading to unreliable tests. •&#9; Enable interaction with web elements : Selectors allow Playwright to perform actions like clicking, typing, or selecting options. For instance, in the checkbox example ( page.locator(&quot;input[type=&apos;checkbox&apos;]:nth-child(1)&quot;).check() ), the selector ensures Playwright targets the correct checkbox. •&#9; Improve test reliability : Robust selectors (e.g., IDs, role-based locators like page.getByRole(&apos;button&apos;, name=&apos;Submit&apos;) ) are less likely to break when the webpage’s structure changes, reducing test flakiness. •&#9; Handle complex scenarios : Modern web applications often use iframes, shadow DOMs, or dynamic elements. generating selectors for such cases, like page.frame_locator(&apos;#mce_0_ifr&apos;).locator(&apos;#tinymce p&apos;) for an iframe.Proper selectors ensure your scripts can navigate and interact with nested or dynamically generated elements. •&#9; Enhance test maintainability : Well-crafted selectors make scripts easier to read and maintain. For example, a clear selector like #table1 tbody tr:nth-child(1) td:nth-child(1) for a web table cell is more understandable than a vague or overly complex one. Maintainable selectors reduce the effort needed to update tests when the application evolves. •&#9; Enable scalability : In large test suites, consistent and reliable selectors ensure tests scale across different pages and scenarios. For example, using role-based selectors page.getByRole(&apos;combobox&apos;) for dropdowns ensures compatibility across similar elements. In the next section, you will see how we normally can inspect the elements of a webpage. Inspect the element Inspecting elements is one of the first steps in understanding how a webpage is structured. It helps quality assurance ( QA ) engineers, developers, and testers interact with specific parts of a page, like buttons, text fields, links, etc. Let us see how to inspect elements on a webpage, using the example of https://selectorshub.com/xpath-practice-page/ . The following are the steps to inspecting elements on a webpage: 1. Open the webpage : Navigate to https://selectorshub.com/xpath-practice-page/ in a web browser (e.g., Google Chrome , Firefox , or Edge ). 2. Access developer tools : Right-click method; right-click anywhere on the webpage (e.g., on a button, text field, or image) and select Inspect or Inspect Element from the context menu. 3. Explore the elements panel : Once developer tools open, the Elements tab (Chrome/Edge) or Inspector tab (Firefox) displays the HTML structure of the webpage. You will see the DOM tree, showing the hierarchy of HTML elements like &lt;div&gt; , &lt;input&gt; , &lt;button&gt; , etc. In the following figure, you can see that for the element User Email , we have different types of locators, e.g., id , class , title , etc., that we can use to locate the field: &lt;input id=&quot;shub76&quot; class=&quot;selectors-input jsSelector&quot; title=&quot;Email&quot; spellcheck=&quot;false&quot; autocomplete=&quot;anyrandomstring&quot; name=&quot;email&quot; value=&quot;&quot; placeholder=&quot;Enter email&quot; type=&quot;email&quot; dataid=&quot;sh_email1&gt; Figure 3.2 , you can see we have highlighted the element User Email , and we have different locators available: Figure 3.2 : Different available locator 4. Select specific elements : a. Hover and highlight : Hover over elements in the DOM tree to highlight the corresponding part of the webpage. For instance, hovering over the &lt;input id=&quot;shub76&quot;&gt; tag will highlight the User Email text field. b. Element selector tool : Click the magnifying glass or arrow icon (top-left in developer tools) to activate the element selector. Then, click any element on the page (e.g., the Submit button) to jump to its HTML code in the DOM tree. 5. Inspect element attributes : a. Check attributes like id , class , type , or placeholder to understand how to interact with the element. For example, the Submit button might have a value attribute, see the following figure: &lt;button value=&quot;Submit&quot;&gt;Submit&lt;/button&gt; In Figure 3.3 , we have highlighted the value attribute, which can be used as a locator: Figure 3.3 : Inspect elements b. These attributes are useful for writing locators (e.g., XPath, CSS selectors) for automation testing. In the next section, you will see how SelectorsHub https://selectorshub.com/ helps in generating the selectors for various web objects. Introduction to SelectorsHub SelectorsHub offers several free tools that are highly beneficial for the testing community. A few of them are briefly explained here: Figure 3.4 : SelectorsHub tools •&#9; SelectorsHub : A powerful plugin to generate and validate XPath and Playwright locators. •&#9; TestCase Studio : Records test steps with screenshots for easy manual test case creation. •&#9; Exploratory Tester : Helps generate test reports during exploratory testing sessions. •&#9; Check My Links : Instantly detects broken links on a webpage with a single click. •&#9; SelectorsHub PRO : Advanced version of SelectorsHub with enhanced locator capabilities. •&#9; TestCase Studio PRO : Premium recorder with advanced features for detailed test documentation. •&#9; Screenshot with URL : Captures screenshots along with the browser URL for better traceability. •&#9; Page Load Timer : Measures and displays page load time automatically. •&#9; Testing Daily : Provides updates and trends from the software testing world. •&#9; Auto Data Filler : Quickly fills forms with test data for faster testing. •&#9; Tool Analyzer : Compares different automation tools to help choose the right one. •&#9; AutoTestData : Generates realistic fake test data for testing purposes. •&#9; XPath Tester for XML : Generates and validates XPath expressions for XML data. •&#9; Clear Cache : Clears browser cache and data instantly in one click. •&#9; CSSViewer : Displays CSS properties of elements directly on the page. •&#9; Request Maker : Allows you to create, send, and modify HTTP requests easily. The following are some of the key features of SelectorsHub: •&#9; One-click selector generation : Quickly generate XPath, CSS, and Playwright selectors with a single click, saving manual effort. •&#9; Smart XPath editor : Built-in editor helps you write and validate XPath and CSS selectors in real-time with instant feedback and clear error messages. •&#9; Dynamic element inspection : Debug dynamic elements like dropdowns, loaders, or hidden elements using a visual debugger that simplifies complex DOM inspections. •&#9; Shadow DOM and nested shadow DOM support : Easily locate elements within Shadow DOMs, even when deeply nested or inside iframes. •&#9; Full iframe handling : Automatically creates selectors for iframes, nested iframes, and even combinations of iframes inside Shadow DOM or vice versa. •&#9; Intelligent error handling : Displays accurate error messages when a selector is invalid, helping users quickly fix mistakes. •&#9; Playwright specific selectors : Supports selector generation and validation specifically for Playwright automation scripts. Advanced features in SelectorsHub: •&#9; XPath healing : Automatically detects broken XPaths in your test scripts and offers smart suggestions to fix them. •&#9; Relative XPath creation : Generate XPath based on the position of another element—perfect for scenarios where absolute paths break easily. •&#9; Attribute filters : Create selectors using specific HTML attributes to ensure uniqueness and stability. •&#9; Context menu integration : Copy XPath or selector directly from the right-click menu—no need to open the plugin. •&#9; Save and manage selectors : Save, edit, and manage your frequently used selectors for quick access. •&#9; Multi-element locator generator : Generate selectors for multiple elements at once—useful during page-level automation. •&#9; Code snippet generator : Instantly get code snippets for Selenium, Cypress, and Playwright, including support for Shadow DOM elements. •&#9; Personalized user interface (UI) : Customize the interface based on your testing preferences and workflow style. SelectorsHub integrates seamlessly with the browser’s DevTools, providing a smart editor to write and verify selectors in real-time. Setting up SelectorsHub Let us see how we can setup the selector hub so that we can use it for inspecting the elements. We will cover how to install the SelectorsHub extension, access it from your browser’s developer tools, and configure basic settings. Once setup, you will be able to quickly identify and generate accurate selectors for web elements during your testing and automation tasks. Follow these steps: 1. Install SelectorsHub : a. Download the extension from the Chrome Web Store, Firefox Add-ons, or Edge Add-ons. b. Once installed, open the browser’s DevTools ( F12 ) and find the SelectorsHub tab, see Figure 3.4 . 2. Using SelectorsHub : a. Navigate to the webpage you are testing. b. In the SelectorsHub tab, hover over or right-click an element to inspect it. c. SelectorsHub will auto-generate selectors (e.g., XPath, CSS, Playwright) and allow you to copy or verify them instantly. d. Use the generated selectors in your Playwright Python scripts. In the following figure, you can see that when we inspect the link Testing it automatically provides auto-generate Playwright and standard selector for the Testing link: Figure 3.5 : Inspect using SelectorsHub In the next section, you will see how we can locate different web elements manually using different selectors. We will explain and provide examples in detail. Finding elements manually using different selectors In this section, you will see how we can find elements using different selectors in Playwright and Python. Playwright is a powerful open-source tool for browser automation, excels at this through its support for various selectors. If you are using Python, you can leverage Playwright to interact with web elements efficiently, whether for testing, scraping, or scripting. We will explore different ways to find elements using selectors like CSS, XPath, text-based, and more. We will explore the practical examples with code snippets, drawing from simple demo pages to keep things hands-on. Selectors are the backbone of element interaction. They tell Playwright exactly which part of the DOM to target. A poor selector can lead to flaky tests, while a robust one ensures reliability across page changes. Playwright supports multiple types, giving you flexibility: CSS for straightforward styling-based picks, XPath for complex traversals, and even text or role-based for semantic searches. Let us break it down with real-world examples using these demo sites: •&#9; Checkbox page : https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/checkbox.php •&#9; Radio button page : https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/radio-button.php •&#9; Form page : https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/form.php •&#9; Web table page : https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/web-table.php •&#9; Dropdown page : https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/dropdown.php Using CSS selectors CSS selectors are intuitive if you are familiar with web design. They target elements by class, ID, attributes, etc. Playwright&apos;s page.locator() method makes this seamless. For instance, on the form demo page, let us find a text input field, e.g., First Name , by its ID. In the following figure, you can see that after right-clicking, we inspect the element, which opens the dev tool where we can see the available locators for the particular field, i.e., First Name : Figure 3.6 : CSS selector In the following code, you can see we have used a CSS ID locator to fill the data in the First Name field: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright import pytest def test_CSS_ID_Selector(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/form.php&quot;) # Locate the name input field using CSS ID selector name_input = page.locator(&quot;#firstname&quot;) name_input.fill(&quot;John Doe&quot;) print(&quot;Filled the name field successfully!&quot;) page.close() Run the command: pytest -s tests/cssSelectors.py In the following figure, you can see we have filled the data in the First Name field using the CSS locator ID: Figure 3.7 : Fill data using CSS selector ID Using XPath selectors XPath is required when you need to navigate the DOM tree deeply, such as finding elements based on position or text content. It is more verbose but powerful for dynamic pages. However, we have to avoid using the XPath selector unless no other option for selecting the elements. Take the web table demo. Suppose you want to grab a specific cell in the table. An XPath like //table//tr/td gets the particular cell data. In the following code, you can see we have used an XPath locator to extract the data from a particular cell: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright import pytest def test_xpath_selector(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/web-table.php&quot;) # Wait for the table cell to be ready page.wait_for_selector(&quot;//table//tr[2]/td[2]&quot;) # Find the cell using XPath and get its text cell = page.locator(&quot;//table//tr[2]/td[2]&quot;) text = cell.inner_text() print(f&quot;Cell content Is ==&gt; : {text}&quot;) browser.close() Using text-based selectors Sometimes, you just need to find an element by its visible text. Playwright&apos;s text selectors are perfect for this, using the :has-text() pseudo-class or direct text matching. In the following code, you can see we have used a text-based locator to find a radio button in the DOM and click on this radio option: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright import pytest def test_testBased_selector(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/radio-button.php&quot;) # Find radio button by text radio_Button_2 = page.locator(&apos;text= Radio Button 2&apos;) radio_Button_2.check() page.wait_for_timeout(2000) # Wait for a second to see the effect print(&quot;Hi !! &apos;Radio Button 2 is selected button!&quot;) browser.close() Combining selectors Playwright lets you mix selectors for precision. For example, on the dropdown demo, combine CSS with text to select an option: In the following code, you can see we have used CSS and a text-based locator to click on the dropdown and finally select a value from the dropdown: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright import pytest def test_combine_selector(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/dropdown.php&quot;) # Combine CSS and text for dropdown selection dropdown = page.locator(&quot;#fruitDropdown&quot;) dropdown.select_option(label=&quot;Banana&quot;) print(&quot;Selected Option 2 : Banana from dropdown!&quot;) browser.close() Finding elements using LocatorLabs LocatorLabs is a modern test automation utility designed to help QA engineers and developers generate reliable element locators for web applications quickly and accurately can be downloaded from chrome extension. It simplifies the process of identifying UI elements and ensures more stable, maintainable automation scripts. It supports multiple popular automation frameworks, including Playwright, Selenium, Cypress, WebDriverIO, and Robot Framework. In the following screenshot, you can see about LocatorLabs with brief introduction: Figure 3.8 : LocatorLabs In the following figure, you can see some of the feature of LocatorLabs: Figure 3.9 : LocatorLabs features Here are some of the amazing features of LocatorLabs : •&#9; Chrome Extension : Easily integrate with DevTools for real-time locator inspection and generation directly in the browser. •&#9; Desktop App : Continue working without restrictions, even in environments where browser extensions are blocked. •&#9; Available Everywhere : LocatorLabs works seamlessly across multiple environments to ensure flexibility and accessibility for testers. •&#9; NPM Package : Instantly run LocatorLabs using npx, making it quick and easy to integrate into your workflow. •&#9; Multi-Framework Support : Generate reliable locators for Playwright, Selenium, Cypress, WebDriverIO, and Robot Framework—all in one tool. •&#9; Smart Locator Generation : Uses intelligent strategies and provides quality ratings (BEST/GOOD/OK) to ensure stable and maintainable tests. •&#9; Advanced DOM Support : Handles complex elements like Shadow DOM and iframes with ease for modern web applications. •&#9; Page Objects Generator : Automatically creates structured page object model ( POM ) classes to improve test organization. •&#9; Flexible Copy Options : Copy locators in different formats, including plain text and variable-based formats. •&#9; Incognito Mode Support : Fully functional in private browsing for secure and isolated testing scenarios. In the following figure, you can how easily we can find the Playwright locator for Add to Cart button which is in first position using LocatorLabs: Figure 3.10 : Finding locator using LocatorLabs In the next section, you will see various waiting strategies to handle dynamic web content. Introduction to waiting strategies Playwright provides several waiting strategies to handle dynamic web content, like elements that load asynchronously or change after user actions, which can make automation tricky. Playwright addresses this through built-in waiting strategies, including auto-waiting, explicit waits, timeouts, and assertions. These strategies help ensure that tests interact with elements only when they are in the desired state (e.g., visible, enabled, or attached). The main waiting mechanisms in Playwright include: •&#9; Auto waiting : Playwright automatically waits for elements to be actionable (e.g., attached, visible, stable) before performing actions like clicking or filling forms. This reduces the need for explicit waits in many cases. •&#9; Explicit waits : Use methods like wait_for_selector , wait_for_timeout , or wait_for_function to wait for specific conditions. •&#9; Network waits : Wait for network events, such as wait_for_request or wait_for_response , to handle API calls or page loads. Auto waiting Auto waiting is an intelligent mechanism in Playwright that ensures elements on a page are fully ready before any action, such as clicking, typing, or selecting, is performed. Instead of forcing developers to add manual delays or write complex wait conditions, Playwright handles this process automatically. Behind the scenes, Playwright runs a series of actionability checks to confirm that an element is visible, enabled, stable, and not obstructed. These checks reduce flakiness in tests and guarantee that the actions being executed behave consistently across different environments and browsers. For example, when you instruct Playwright to click a button, it does not simply trigger the click event immediately. It first verifies that the button is attached to the DOM rendered on the screen and is interactive. Only after all these checks succeed will the action be carried out. If the required conditions are not met within the defined timeout, Playwright raises a TimeoutError . This approach saves time for developers by preventing unnecessary test failures and improving the overall reliability of automation scripts. Let us explain in detail how the auto-waiting mechanism works. Working of auto-waiting Before performing an action, Playwright verifies that the target element meets specific criteria to ensure the action behaves as expected. For example, when using locator.click() , Playwright checks that: •&#9; The locator points to a single element. •&#9; The element is visible on the page. •&#9; The element is stable, meaning it is not undergoing animation or has completed its animation. •&#9; The element can receive events, ensuring it is not blocked by other elements. •&#9; The element is in an enabled state. The following are the key points: •&#9; Visible : Ensures the element is visible on the page. •&#9; Stable : Confirms the element is not animating or has finished animating. •&#9; Receives events : Verifies the element is not obscured and can receive interactions. •&#9; Enabled : Checks that the element is in an enabled state (not disabled). •&#9; Editable : Ensures the element is editable, applicable for input fields. For example, auto-waiting in action. Let us create a simple Python script using Playwright to fill out a search box on a website. This example is original and designed to show how auto-waiting works. Consider a scenario, we will write a script to: •&#9; Open a browser. •&#9; Go to a website (like a search engine, Amazon ). •&#9; Type a search term into a search box. •&#9; Click the search button. The website might take a moment to load the search box and button, but Playwright’s auto-waiting will handle it for us. In the following example, you can see the auto-waiting mechanism happen. Playwright waits for the search box and then waits for the button to be clickable. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_auto_wait(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Go to the website (amazon search engine) page.goto(&quot;https://amazon.in/&quot;) # Auto waiting happens here: Playwright waits for the search box to be ready page.fill(&quot;#twotabsearchtextbox&quot;, &quot;Playwright&quot;) # Click the search button # Auto waiting happens here: Playwright waits for the button to be clickable page.click(&quot;input[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) # Wait a moment to see the results page.wait_for_timeout(3000) # Wait 3 seconds to observe # Close the browser print(&quot;Closing the browser...&quot;) browser.close() Explicit waits Playwright waits automatically for things like buttons to be ready. However, sometimes, you need to tell Playwright to wait for something specific, like waiting for a picture to load or for a message to show up. That is where explicit waits come in. You add them yourself to make sure your test does not rush ahead too soon. Working of explicit waits Explicit waits are like giving Playwright a special instruction: Hey, pause here until this exact thing happens. This is useful when auto-waiting is not enough, such as waiting for a page to fully load after clicking something or for some text to change. Playwright has easy commands for this, and you can set a timeout (like wait up to 10 seconds ) so it does not wait forever. Here are a few common types: •&#9; Wait for an element to appear : Use wait_for_selector() to look for something specific on the page. •&#9; Wait for the page to load : Use wait_for_load_state() after actions like clicking a link. •&#9; Wait for a condition : Use wait_for_function() to check if something is true, like is this number bigger than 5? •&#9; Simple pause : Use wait_for_timeout() for a quick break, but try not to use this too much, it is like a lazy wait. Let us take some examples: •&#9; wait_for_selector : This method tells Playwright to pause execution until a specific element appears in the DOM and becomes visible (by default). In the following code, you can see that after clicking Submit , the script explicitly waits for the search box to load: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_auto_wait(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.amazon.in&quot;) # Step 1: Type a query into the search bar page.fill(&quot;input#twotabsearchtextbox&quot;, &quot;laptop&quot;) # Step 2: Click on the search button page.click(&quot;input#nav-search-submit-button&quot;) # Step 3: Wait for search results to load page.wait_for_selector(&quot;div.s-main-slot div[data-component-type=&apos;s-search-result&apos;]&quot;, timeout=10000) browser.close() •&#9; wait_for_url : In the following example, you can see that after searching for a particular product, you wait for the search result URL to load: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_element_text(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.amazon.in&quot;) # Enter search query and submit page.fill(&quot;input#twotabsearchtextbox&quot;, &quot;laptop&quot;) page.click(&quot;input#nav-search-submit-button&quot;) # Wait for search results URL to load page.wait_for_url(&quot;**/s?k=laptop*&quot;) print(&quot;Navigated to search results for laptop!&quot;) browser.close() •&#9; wait_for_funcation : In the following code, you can see that after navigating to the URL, we will wait until the button is enabled using JS. We can use this method when element becomes enabled/disabled dynamically. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_element_text(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.amazon.in&quot;) # Wait until the button is enabled using JS page.wait_for_function( &quot;&quot;&quot;() =&gt; { const btn = document.querySelector(&quot;input#nav-search-submit-button&quot;); return btn &amp;&amp; !btn.disabled; }&quot;&quot;&quot; ) page.click(&quot;input#nav-search-submit-button&quot;) print(&quot;Clicked search button!&quot;) browser.close() •&#9; wait_for_load_state : This method is used to wait until the page reaches a specific loading state, waits for the load event, when the page and all resources (images, stylesheets, etc.) are fully loaded: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_element_text(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.amazon.in&quot;) # Wait for a lighter state if networkidle times out page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;domcontentloaded&quot;) print(&quot;Page fully loaded and network idle&quot;) browser.close() Network waits So far, we discussed auto-wait and explicit wait. Now we will focus on network waits, which are another important waiting mechanism in Playwright. Network waits allow the script to pause until essential network activities, such as loading resources, API responses, or server calls, are fully completed. This ensures that the page is ready for interaction and prevents actions from being attempted on partially loaded elements. Playwright monitors ongoing network requests and gives the option to wait for them to finish before moving ahead with the next step in the script. This is especially useful when dealing with modern web applications that depend heavily on asynchronous calls to fetch or update content. For example, a page may display a loading spinner while it retrieves data from an API. Using network waits ensures that the script continues only after the data has been received and the UI is ready. In practice, network waits can be applied in scenarios like form submissions, data table loading, and page navigation, where multiple background requests are triggered. By incorporating this strategy, tests become more stable, maintainable, and reflective of real user behavior. Working of network waits Playwright checks the network, that is all the back-and-forth communication between your browser and the internet. It can wait until things quiet down, like no more requests happening for a short time. This is great for tests where you need everything fully loaded, such as after submitting a form that pulls in new data. Here are some common network waits: •&#9; Wait for the network to be idle : Use wait_for_load_state(&apos;networkidle&apos;) to pause until there are no active connections for at least half a second (500 milliseconds). However, Playwright sometimes says to avoid this if your site has ongoing chats or live updates, because it might wait forever. •&#9; Wait for a specific request : Use wait_for_request() to watch for a certain web request, like waiting for a file to download. •&#9; Wait for a response : Use wait_for_response() to hold until the server sends back info, such as after clicking a button that asks for new data. The following are the best practices for waiting in Playwright: •&#9; Prefer auto-waiting and expect : Rely on Playwright’s built-in auto-waiting and expect assertions for most interactions, as they are optimized for reliability. •&#9; Use explicit waits sparingly : Only use methods like wait_for_selector or wait_for_function when auto-waiting is not sufficient (e.g., for elements loaded dynamically). •&#9; Leverage network waits : Use expect_request or expect_response for API-driven applications to ensure tests wait for critical network events. •&#9; Avoid hardcoded delays : Minimize wait_for_timeout to prevent flaky tests; use condition-based waits instead. •&#9; Set reasonable timeouts : Configure timeouts (e.g., timeout=5000 ) to balance test speed and reliability, avoiding overly long waits. •&#9; Handle errors gracefully : Use try-except blocks or check conditions to handle cases where elements or events may not occur. Introduction to various types of assertions Assertions are used to verify the state of web elements or application behavior during automated testing. These assertions ensure that the application behaves as expected by checking conditions like equality, visibility, presence, or absence of elements. Playwright, being a powerful browser automation tool, integrates with Python&apos;s testing frameworks like Pytest to perform these assertions. In the following, we will explain the assertion example mainly around equality, visibility, presence, and absence: •&#9; to_be_checked : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Apply a filter (e.g., Electronics category checkbox) electronics_filter = page.locator(&quot;input[type=&apos;checkbox&apos;][value=&apos;electronics&apos;]&quot;) electronics_filter.click() # Check the checkbox # Assertion: Verify the checkbox is checked expect(electronics_filter).to_be_checked() •&#9; to_be_disabled : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Navigate to a product page (assumed out-of-stock product) page.click(&quot;a[href*=&apos;product/out-of-stock&apos;]&quot;) # Adjust based on actual product link # Assertion: Verify the &quot;Add to Cart&quot; button is disabled add_to_cart_button = page.locator(&quot;button:has-text(&apos;Add to cart&apos;)&quot;) expect(add_to_cart_button).to_be_disabled() •&#9; to_be_editable : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Navigate to the website page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;email&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;password&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.click(&quot;button[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) # Locate the search bar search_bar = page.locator(&quot;input[type=&apos;search&apos;]&quot;) # Assertion: Verify the search bar is editable expect(search_bar).to_be_editable() •&#9; to_be_ enabled : from playwright.sync_api import expect locator = page.locator(&quot;button.submit&quot;) expect(locator).to_be_enabled() •&#9; to_be_visible : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Assertion: Verify the search bar is visible search_bar = page.locator( &quot;input[type=&apos;search&apos;]&quot; ) expect(search_bar).to_be_visible() •&#9; to_have_count : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Navigate to the website and log page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;email&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;password&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.click(&quot;button[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) # Assertion: Verify the products in the product listing products = page.locator(&quot;#productContainer&quot;) expect(products).to_have_count(1) •&#9; to_contain_text : from playwright.sync_api import expect # Navigate to the website and login page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;email&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.fill(&quot;input[type=&apos;password&apos;]&quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) page.click(&quot;button[type=&apos;submit&apos;]&quot;) # Assertion: Verify the page header has the text &quot;Shop&quot; page_header = page.locator(&quot;#navbarCollapse&quot;) expect(page_header).to_contain_text(&quot;Shop&quot;) Table 3.1 is a concise summary of the Playwright assertions discussed ( to_be_visible() , to_have_count() , to_have_text() , to_have_value() , to_be_checked() , to_be_disabled() , to_be_editable() , to_be_empty() , to_be_enabled() , to_be_focused() ) in a tabular format. The table includes the purpose of some of the assertions and a unique example. Assertion Purpose Example use case expect(locator).to_be_visible() Verifies that an element is rendered and visible on the page (not hidden). Ensure the search bar is visible after logging into the shop page. expect(locator).to_have_text() Verifies that an element contains exact text (or matches a pattern). Check that the page header displays exactly, e.g, Shop after login. expect(locator).to_have_value() Confirms that an input element has a specific value. Verify that the search bar contains the value, e.g, laptop, after entering a search term. expect(locator).to_be_checked() Ensures a checkbox or radio button is in the checked state. Confirm that the Electronics checkbox is checked after selecting it. expect(locator).to_be_disabled() Verifies that an element (e.g., button, input) is disabled and non-interactable. Check that the Add to Cart button is disabled for an out-of-stock product. expect(locator).to_be_editable() Confirms that an input element is editable (not disabled or readonly). Ensure the search bar is editable for entering product queries. expect(locator).to_be_enabled() Ensures an element is enabled and interactable (not disabled). Verify that the Proceed to Checkout button is enabled after adding a product. expect(locator).to_be_focused() Checks that an element (e.g., input, button) currently has focus. Ensure the search bar is focused after clicking it for user input. Table 3.1 : Playwright assertions Hard vs. soft assertion Assertions are used to verify that the expected conditions in a test case are met. Playwright supports both soft assertions and hard assertions, which differ in how they handle test execution when an assertion fails. Assertions help validate that your tests meet expected conditions, such as element visibility or page state. There are two main types of assertions, i.e., hard assertions and soft assertions. Hard assertions immediately stop test execution on failure, while soft assertions allow the test to continue, collecting failures to report at the end. This makes soft assertions ideal for scenarios where you want to check multiple conditions without early termination. In the following, you will see both types of assertions, with original examples using the OpenCart demo site (a sample e-commerce page) at the provided URL https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=common/home/ . These examples are written in Python using Playwright&apos;s synchronous API for simplicity, and they focus on verifying basic page elements like the title and visible components to demonstrate the concepts without copying external code. •&#9; Hard assertion : We will navigate to the OpenCart demo site and use hard assertions to check the page title and the visibility of the search input field. If the title assertion fails, the test stops before checking the search field. In the following code, you can see how hard assertion can be implemented in practice: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_hard_assertions_opencart(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) # Run in headed mode for visibility page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=common/home&quot;) # Hard assertion: Check page title (fails if not exact match, stops test) expect(page).to_have_title(&quot;Your Store&quot;) # This should pass # Hard assertion: Check if search input is visible (continues only if previous passes) search_locator = page.locator(&quot;#search input&quot;) expect(search_locator).to_be_visible() # If both pass, the test completes successfully browser.close() In this test, if the title does not match Your Store , execution stops, and you will not see the visibility check or the print statement. This enforces a strict flow for essential validations. •&#9; Soft assertions : Playwright with Python does not support soft assertions by default. To implement soft assertion in Playwright with Python, you have to install the package pytest-check . To install this package, run the command: pip install pytest-check In the following code, you can see an example of soft assertion: import pytest_check as check from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_soft_assertions_opencart(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=common/home&quot;) # Soft Assertion 1: Page title check.equal(page.title(), &quot;Your Store&quot;, &quot;Page title mismatch&quot;) # Soft Assertion 2: Logo visibility logo = page.locator(&quot;img[title=&apos;naveenopencart&apos;]&quot;) check.is_true(logo.is_visible(), &quot;Logo should be visible on homepage&quot;) # Soft Assertion 3: Search box placeholder search_placeholder = page.locator(&quot;input[name=&apos;search&apos;]&quot;).get_attribute(&quot;placeholder&quot;) check.equal(search_placeholder, &quot;Search&quot;, &quot;Search placeholder text mismatch&quot;) # Soft Assertion 4: Search box placeholder enterData_searchBox = page.locator(&quot;input[name=&apos;search&apos;]&quot;) enterData_searchBox.fill(&quot;NaveenAutomation&quot;) value =enterData_searchBox.input_value() check.equal(value, &quot;NaveenAutomation&quot;) page.wait_for_timeout(2000) browser.close() In this test above, it opens the OpenCart demo site, runs four soft assertions (title, logo visibility, search box placeholder with entering data). Now, let us try to fail the third check intentionally. Update the text Search to SearchUpdated . Below code after updating the text: # Soft Assertion 3: Search box placeholder search_placeholder = page.locator(&quot;input[name=&apos;search&apos;]&quot;).get_attribute(&quot;placeholder&quot;) check.equal(search_placeholder, &quot;SearchUpdated&quot;, &quot;Search placeholder text mismatch&quot;) When we execute the above code after updating the test, the complete line of code is executed without stopping at the third assertion. The third assertion failed, which was expected. You can see in the following figure: Figure 3.11 : Soft assertions In the following figure, you can see that the fourth assertion is executed successfully by entering data in the search box: Figure 3.12 : Soft assertions with output Conclusion In this chapter, we have seen how CSS, XPath, text-based, and role methods enable precise element location and interactions like clicking, typing, form filling, and option selection across demos for checkboxes, alerts, tables, and more. Built-in browser developer tools and automation framework capabilities help in generating and validating these selectors, while effective waiting strategies (waits, timeouts) and assertions ensure reliable handling of dynamic content and accurate test validation. The choice of locator generation tools discussed in this chapter depends on the end user’s preferences, project needs, and automation framework. They remove the need for assertions d. They help design selectors visually 4. What is one of the main objectives of this chapter? a. To learn backend API automation b.
