Which of the following is not listed as a key action covered in this chapter? a. Clicking elements b. Filling forms c. Selecting options from dropdowns d. Database query execution Answer key 1. b 2. b 3. b 4. c 5. d Join our Discord space Join our Discord workspace for latest updates, offers, tech happenings around the world, new releases, and sessions with the authors: https://discord.bpbonline.com

  - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 4 Advanced Elements Handling Introduction In the previous chapter, we have learned about selectors, assertions, and waiting strategies. This chapter provides a comprehensive guide to handling a wide range of user interface ( UI ) components, starting with basic elements like input boxes, buttons, and checkboxes, and progressing to more advanced interactions such as dropdowns, menus, radio buttons, and multi-select dropdowns. You will also learn techniques for performing mouse actions, including drag-and-drop, as well as managing file uploads and downloads. The chapter further delves into handling complex scenarios involving inline frames ( iframes ), JavaScript ( JS ) alerts, browser tabs, pop-ups, and Shadow Document Object Model ( DOM ) elements. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Handling elements in automation •&#9; Handling input fields, buttons, and checkboxes •&#9; Working with dropdowns, menus, and radio buttons •&#9; Handling multi-select dropdown, mouse drag-and-drop •&#9; Uploading and downloading file •&#9; Handling iframe and JS alert •&#9; Working with shadow DOM and tabs Objectives The objectives of this chapter are to handle basic UI elements (input boxes, buttons, checkboxes), advanced components (dropdowns, menus, radio buttons, multi-select dropdowns), perform mouse actions (drag-and-drop), manage file uploads or downloads, handle iframes, JS alerts, tabs, pop-ups, and interact with Shadow DOM elements for robust web automation. Handling elements in automation Elements handling in automation testing refers to the process of interacting with and manipulating web elements (such as buttons, text fields, dropdowns, links, checkboxes, etc.) on a web page during automated testing. In the context of software testing, automation tools like Selenium , Cypress , or Playwright are used to simulate user interactions with these elements to validate the functionality, performance, and behavior of a web application. This process ensures that the application works as intended across various scenarios and user interactions. Importance of element handling In automated testing, scripts must accurately find and manipulate elements to perform tasks such as clicking, entering text, or verifying states (e.g., enabled, visible, or selected). Poor handling leads to flaky tests, unreliable results due to timing issues or UI changes, potentially missing bugs, or causing false failures. The following are the key aspects of element handling in automation testing: •&#9; Locating elements : To interact with web elements, automation scripts must first locate them on the page. This is typically done using locators such as: o&#9; ID : Unique identifier for an element. o&#9; Name : This attribute of the element. o&#9; Class name : Cascading Style Sheets ( CSS ) class associated with the element. o&#9; XPath : Path-based locator to navigate the DOM structure. o&#9; CSS selector : Uses CSS syntax to select elements. o&#9; Link text/partial link text : For hyperlinks. o&#9; Tag name : Identifies elements by their Hypertext Markup Language ( HTML ) tag. •&#9; Interacting with elements : Once located, automation scripts perform actions on elements to mimic user behavior. Common interactions include: o&#9; Clicking : Clicking buttons, links, or checkboxes. o&#9; Typing : Entering text into input fields or text areas. o&#9; Selecting : Choosing options from dropdowns or radio buttons. o&#9; Hovering : Simulating mouse hover to trigger events like tooltips. o&#9; Dragging-and-dropping : Moving elements across the page. o&#9; Scrolling : Navigating to elements not in the viewport. •&#9; Validating elements : After interacting with elements, automation scripts verify the expected outcomes, which include: o&#9; Visibility : Checking if an element is visible or hidden. o&#9; State : Verifying if an element is enabled, disabled, selected, or checked. o&#9; Text/Content : Confirming the text or value of an element matches expectations. o&#9; Attributes : Validating properties like class, style, or data attributes. o&#9; Position/Size : Ensuring elements are correctly positioned or sized on the page. •&#9; Handling dynamic elements : Modern web applications often have dynamic elements that change based on user actions or data (e.g., elements loaded via AJAX or JS). Handling these requires: o&#9; Explicit waits : Waiting for elements to appear, become clickable, or meet certain conditions before interacting. o&#9; Implicit waits : Setting a default wait time for elements to load. o&#9; Dynamic locators : Using flexible locators to handle elements with changing attributes (e.g., dynamic IDs). Common challenges in element handling In the following, there are some of the challenges: •&#9; Element not found : Occurs when locators are incorrect or elements are not yet loaded. •&#9; Stale element reference : When an element is no longer attached to the DOM due to page refreshes or updates. •&#9; Dynamic content : Elements that change dynamically require robust wait mechanisms. •&#9; Cross-browser issues : Elements may behave differently across browsers, requiring browser-specific handling. •&#9; Iframe handling : Interacting with elements inside iframes requires switching to the iframe context. •&#9; Pop-ups/Alerts : Handling browser alerts or pop-ups that interrupt automation flow. In the next section, you will see how we can handle basic elements like input boxes, buttons, and checkboxes. Handling input fields, buttons, and checkboxes Managing UI elements like input boxes, buttons, and checkboxes is essential for creating reliable test scripts. These components form the backbone of user interactions in web and mobile applications, and automating them involves locating the elements, performing actions, and verifying outcomes. The following sections outline practical approaches to handle each of them to ensure tests are robust and maintainable. Input box Input text fields allow users to enter data such as usernames, passwords, or search boxes. In automation, the key is to simulate accurate data entry while handling variations like placeholders or validation rules: •&#9; Locating the element : Use reliable selectors like ID, name, or XPath. For example, if an input box has a unique ID like username , target it directly to avoid fragility in dynamic UIs. •&#9; Performing actions : Clear any existing text first to prevent carryover from previous tests, then input new values. •&#9; Verification : After entry, check if the value is correctly set by retrieving the attribute&apos;s content and comparing it to the expected input. Also, test edge cases, such as maximum character limits or invalid entries that trigger error messages. Playwright is a modern automation framework for web applications that allows easy interaction with input elements such as text fields. Let us take an example of how you can handle input boxes, illustrated using the example site https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php and the provided demo credentials. The following are the key methods for input fields: •&#9; page.fill() : This method focuses on the input box and fills it with the desired value, clearing any existing content first: page.fill(&apos;input[name=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo@demo.com&apos;); page.fill(&apos;input[name=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;); •&#9; Assertions : You can also check whether the input box is visible, empty, editable, and enabled before operating on it: expect(email).to_be_visible() expect(email).to_be_empty() expect(email).to_be_editable() expect(email).to_be_enabled() In the following script, we can check that both input fields are visible, empty, editable, and enabled before entering values. These assertion methods ( to_be_visible() , to_be_empty() , to_be_editable() , and to_be_enabled() ) ensure the fields are in the correct state for automation: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_text_box(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) email = page.locator(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;) password = page.locator(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;) # Assertions for email input expect(email).to_be_visible() expect(email).to_be_empty() expect(email).to_be_editable() expect(email).to_be_enabled() # Assertions for password input expect(password).to_be_visible() expect(password).to_be_empty() expect(password).to_be_editable() expect(password).to_be_enabled() # Fill login details email.fill(&quot;demo@demo.com &quot;) password.fill(&quot;demo&quot;) # Login button page.click(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) browser.close() Buttons Handling buttons involves locating the button elements on a web page and performing actions such as clicking them. For example, consider the site https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php , where you might want to automate the login process by clicking the Login button. The following are the ways to handle buttons in Playwright: •&#9; Locating the button : You can locate a button using various selectors such as CSS selectors, text content, or attributes. For example, to find the login button on the demo site, one common way to use the id : login_button = page.click(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) This targets the button element used to submit the login form. •&#9; Clicking the button : Once located, the .click() method simulates a user clicking the button: await login_button.click() •&#9; Asserting button properties : Before interacting, you may check if the button is visible and enabled to avoid errors: expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() •&#9; Handling button actions in workflow : Typically, clicking a button is part of a sequence, such as filling out a form before submitting: page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo@demo.com&apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) login_button.click() •&#9; Code snippet : The following code snippet is for clicking the button, but before clicking, make sure the button is visible and enabled for clicking: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_button(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) # Assert button is visible and enabled expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() # Click the login button login_button.click() # Additional steps or assertions can follow here browser.close() Checkboxes Handling checkboxes in Playwright is straightforward using its powerful API. You can select, deselect, and verify the state of checkbox elements easily. Let us take an example scenario based on the site https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php , where you login using an email and password, then navigate to Men Fashion under Shop Now , and verify the checkbox states. In the following code, you can see we have logged in and navigate to Men Fashion under Shop Now , and verify the checkbox states. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_button(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) # Log in page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) login_button.click() # Click on Shop Now against Men Fashion # Adjust selector based on actual page content page.click(&apos;text=Men Fashion &gt;&gt; xpath=.. &gt;&gt; text=Shop Now&apos;) # Checkboxes for Formal and Footwear inside Filter by Type formal_checkbox = page.locator(&apos;label[for=&quot;gender-1&quot;]&apos;) footwear_checkbox = page.locator(&apos;label[for=&quot;gender-2&quot;]&apos;) # Ensure the checkboxes are visible expect(formal_checkbox).to_be_visible() expect(footwear_checkbox).to_be_visible() formal_checkbox.click() footwear_checkbox.click() # Print whether these checkboxes are checked return True print(&quot;Formal checkbox checked:&quot;, formal_checkbox.is_checked()) print(&quot;Footwear checkbox checked:&quot;, footwear_checkbox.is_checked()) page.wait_for_timeout(3000) formal_checkbox.uncheck() footwear_checkbox.uncheck() # You can add assertions to verify expected states after these actions expect(formal_checkbox).not_to_be_checked() expect(footwear_checkbox).not_to_be_checked() # Print whether these checkboxes are unchecked return False print(&quot;Formal checkbox is checked:&quot;, formal_checkbox.is_checked()) print(&quot;Footwear checkbox is checked:&quot;, footwear_checkbox.is_checked()) browser.close() Above script launches the browser, logs into the site with demo credentials, navigates to Men Fashion , locates the Formal and Footwear checkboxes, verifies they are visible, checks them, confirms they are selected, unchecks them, verifies they are unselected, prints their states, waits briefly, and then closes the browser. In the next section, you will see how we can use dropdowns, menus, and radio buttons. Working with dropdowns, menus, and radio buttons Handling element dropdowns, menus, and radio buttons in automation testing using Playwright for Python is a critical aspect of testing web applications. These UI elements are common in forms and interactive interfaces, and Playwright provides robust methods to interact with them efficiently. The following sections explain how to handle each of these elements in a clear, concise, and original manner, tailored for automation testing with Playwright in Python. Handling dropdown Dropdowns, typically implemented using the &lt;select&gt; HTML tag, allow users to choose one or multiple options from a list. Playwright provides the select_option() method to interact with dropdowns. The following are the key concepts: •&#9; Locating the dropdown : Use Playwright’s locators ( page.locator() ) to find the &lt;select&gt; element by its ID, name, CSS selector, or XPath. •&#9; Selecting options : Options can be selected by their value, label, or index. •&#9; Handling multi-select : Some dropdowns allow multiple selections, which Playwright supports. In the following figure, you can see a fruit dropdown with various options, and the user can select one value from the dropdown: Figure 4.1 : Dropdown The following code selects the value from the dropdown menu using the link https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/dropdown.php . It selects options by value, label, and index, verifies selections, and lists all available options. The following script ensures the dropdown is visible, enabled, and validates interactions with assertions for reliable testing: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_dropdown(): with sync_playwright() as p: # Launch browser in non-headless mode for visibility browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Navigate to the provided URL page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/dropdown.php&quot;) # Click on dropdown by ID dropdown = page.locator(&quot;#fruitDropdown&quot;) # Ensure the dropdown is visible and enabled dropdown.wait_for(state=&quot;visible&quot;) assert dropdown.is_enabled(), &quot;Dropdown is not enabled&quot; # Test Case 1: Select by value dropdown.select_option(value=&quot;Apple&quot;) selected_value = dropdown.input_value() print(f&quot;Selected by value: {selected_value}&quot;) assert selected_value == &quot;Apple&quot;, f&quot;Expected &apos;Apple&apos;, but got {selected_value}&quot; # Test Case 2: Select by label dropdown.select_option(label=&quot;Banana&quot;) selected_value = dropdown.input_value() print(f&quot;Selected by label: {selected_value}&quot;) assert selected_value == &quot;Banana&quot;, f&quot;Expected &apos;Banana&apos;, but got {selected_value}&quot; # Test Case 3: Select by index dropdown.select_option(index=2) # Index 2 corresponds to &quot;Banana&quot; selected_value = dropdown.input_value() print(f&quot;Selected by index: {selected_value}&quot;) assert selected_value == &quot;Banana&quot;, f&quot;Expected &apos;Banana&apos;, but got {selected_value}&quot; # Verify all available options in the dropdown options = dropdown.locator(&quot;option&quot;) count = options.count() print(f&quot;Available options ({count}):&quot;) for i in range(count): option_text = options.nth(i).text_content() option_value = options.nth(i).get_attribute(&quot;value&quot;) print(f&quot;Option {i}: Text={option_text}, Value={option_value}&quot;) # Close the browser browser.close() In the following figure, you can see test cases executed successfully and printed the output with correct values: Figure 4.2 : Dropdown execution Handling menus Handling menus in automation testing with Playwright for Python is essential for interacting with navigation bars, context menus, or custom dropdowns commonly found in web applications. Unlike standard &lt;select&gt; dropdowns, menus are often built with &lt;div&gt; , &lt;ul&gt; , &lt;li&gt; , or other HTML elements, driven by JS or CSS for interactivity. Playwright provides robust methods like click() , hover() , and locators to handle these elements effectively. The following section explains how to automate menu interactions, focusing on a practical, original example tailored for clarity and reliability. The following code launches a browser, navigates to the shop site, reveals the Shop dropdown, selects Womens Wear and Electronics , lists all dropdown items, selects View All , then closes the browser: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_shop_menu_dropdown(): with sync_playwright() as p: # Launch browser in non-headless mode for visibility browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Navigate to the website page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) # Locate the Shop menu toggle shop_menu = page.locator(&apos;[data-toggle=&quot;dropdown&quot;]&apos;) shop_menu.wait_for(state=&quot;visible&quot;) # Test Case 1: Hover to reveal dropdown and select &quot;Womens Wear&quot; shop_menu.hover() page.wait_for_timeout(3000) womens_wear = page.locator(&apos;a.dropdown-item:has-text(&quot;Womens Wear&quot;)&apos;) womens_wear.click() page.wait_for_url(&quot;**/womens-wear.php&quot;, timeout=10000) print(&quot;Navigated to Womens Wear page&quot;) # Test Case 2: Select &quot;Electronics&quot; from dropdown shop_menu.hover() electronics = page.locator(&apos;a.dropdown-item:has-text(&quot;Electronics&quot;)&apos;) electronics.click() page.wait_for_url(&quot;**/electronics.php&quot;) print(&quot;Navigated to Electronics page&quot;) # Test Case 3: List all dropdown items shop_menu.hover() dropdown_menu = page.locator(&quot;.dropdown-menu.show&quot;) dropdown_menu.wait_for(state=&quot;visible&quot;) dropdown_items = dropdown_menu.locator(&quot;a.dropdown-item&quot;) count = dropdown_items.count() print(f&quot;Shop dropdown contains {count} items:&quot;) for i in range(count): item_text = dropdown_items.nth(i).text_content().strip() item_href = dropdown_items.nth(i).get_attribute(&quot;href&quot;) print(f&quot;Item {i + 1}: {item_text} (URL: {item_href})&quot;) # Test Case 4: Select &quot;View All&quot; and verify shop_menu.hover() dropdown_menu.wait_for(state=&quot;visible&quot;) view_all = page.locator(&apos;a.dropdown-item:has-text(&quot;View All&quot;)&apos;) view_all.click() page.wait_for_url(&quot;**/shop.php&quot;) print(&quot;Navigated to View All page&quot;) # Close the browser browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that the test cases executed successfully and printed all menu options in the shop menu: Figure 4.3 : Menus execution Handling radio button Radio buttons are commonly used when users need to select a single option from a group. Playwright provides intuitive methods to interact with radio buttons, making automated testing straightforward. Here is how you can handle radio buttons using the site used for example purposes https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/radio-button.php In the following figure, you can see we have selected the male radio button, the female, and verified the visibility of each selected radio button: Figure 4.4 : Radio button The following script checks each radio button, validates its selection, and prints a true/false status for both Male and Female after each selection step, allowing you to confirm which button is selected in real-time: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_radioButton(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/radio-button.php”) # Locate radio buttons by value male_radio = page.locator(&apos;input[type=&quot;radio&quot;][name=&quot;gender&quot;][value=&quot;Male&quot;]&apos;) female_radio = page.locator(&apos;input[type=&quot;radio&quot;][name=&quot;gender&quot;][value=&quot;Female&quot;]&apos;) # Assert radio buttons are visible and enabled expect(male_radio).to_be_visible() expect(male_radio).to_be_enabled() expect(female_radio).to_be_visible() expect(female_radio).to_be_enabled() # Select Male radio button male_radio.check() expect(male_radio).to_be_checked() expect(female_radio).not_to_be_checked() # Print status of radio buttons print(&quot;Male radio selected:&quot;, male_radio.is_checked()) # True print(&quot;Female radio selected:&quot;, female_radio.is_checked()) # False # Click the Show Selected Gender button page.click(&apos;button:has-text(&quot;Show Selected Gender&quot;)&apos;) # Verify result text displays &quot;You selected: Male&quot; result = page.locator(&apos;#result&apos;) expect(result).to_contain_text(&quot;You selected: Male&quot;) # Select Female radio button, check status, and click female_radio.check() expect(female_radio).to_be_checked() expect(male_radio).not_to_be_checked() page.click(&apos;button:has-text(&quot;Show Selected Gender&quot;)&apos;) expect(result).to_contain_text(&quot;You selected: Female&quot;) # Print status of radio buttons print(&quot;Male radio selected:&quot;, male_radio.is_checked()) # False print(&quot;Female radio selected:&quot;, female_radio.is_checked()) # True browser.close() Handling multi-select dropdown, mouse drag-and-drop During automation, interacting with complex UI elements like multi-select dropdowns and mouse drag-and-drop functionalities requires precise handling to ensure accurate test coverage. The following sections explain how to effectively automate these interactions, focusing on common tools like Playwright with Python. Handling multi-select dropdowns Multi-select dropdowns allow users to choose multiple options from a list, presenting unique challenges when automating tests for such elements. Multi-select dropdowns allow users to select more than one option simultaneously. Playwright provides a straightforward method called select_option() to handle this. The following are the steps to interact with a multi-select dropdown: 1. Use the Playwright locator to target the dropdown element. 2. Use the select_option() method, which can take a list of option values, labels, or indexes. 3. This method works with native &lt;select multiple&gt; HTML elements, like the example dropdown ID countryDropdown . In the following figure, you can see we have a multi-select dropdown from which we can select multiple values: Figure 4.5 : Multi-select dropdown In the following code, select_option() is given a list of values matching the value attributes from the dropdown. You can also use a label or an index in the option dictionary if you prefer. The method performs all selections in one call, efficiently handling multi-select dropdowns. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_button(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/dropdown.php”) # Select multiple countries by value page.locator(&quot;#countryDropdown&quot;).select_option([&quot;India&quot;, &quot;UK&quot;, &quot;Canada&quot;]) page.wait_for_timeout(3000) # Optionally verify the selection selected = page.locator(&quot;#countryDropdown&quot;).evaluate(&quot;el =&gt; Array.from(el.selectedOptions).map(o =&gt; o.value)&quot;) print(&quot;Selected options:&quot;, selected) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that the code is executed successfully and the three options from the county multi-selected dropdown are selected: Figure 4.6 : Multi-selected values Handling drag-and-drop Drag-and-drop demo at the provided link demonstrates a sortable task list, and Playwright can be used for automating interactions with this UI for robust end-to-end ( E2E ) testing. Let us take an example of https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/drag-and-drop.php for drag-and-drop. On the example site given above, the user can reorder a list of items ( Inbox , Work , Personal , etc.) by dragging and dropping them in a new order. In Playwright, page.drag_and_drop() is a built-in method that simulates a drag-and-drop action: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/drag-and-drop.php”) # Selectors for the list items source_selector = &quot;ul#sortableList li:has-text(&apos;Item 1&apos;)&quot; target_selector = &quot;ul#sortableList li:has-text(&apos;Item 5&apos;)&quot; # Perform drag-and-drop page.drag_and_drop(source_selector, target_selector) # Optionally: Wait or check result page.wait_for_timeout(5000) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see Item 1 is dragged and dropped just above Item 5 : Figure 4.7 : Drag-and-drop Uploading and downloading file In automation testing, handling file uploads and downloads is a common requirement when testing web applications that involve file interactions, such as uploading documents or downloading generated reports. Playwright, a powerful browser automation library, provides robust APIs to manage these tasks efficiently. In the following sections, you will see the handling of upload and download files. Handling upload file File upload functionality is a critical feature in web applications, allowing users to submit files like documents, images, or PDFs through an &lt;input type=&quot;file&quot;&gt; element. Playwright provides a straightforward and efficient way to automate file uploads using its robust APIs. In the following code, you can see we have uploaded the pwpy.txt file. This Playwright Python code demonstrates file upload. It launches a Chromium browser, navigates to a file upload page, and uses the method set_input_files() to upload a text file ( pwpy.txt ) into the input element with ID fileInput : from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_uploadFile(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/file-upload.php”) # Upload file to input element with id &apos;fileInput&apos; page.set_input_files(&quot;#fileInput&quot;, &quot;/Users/abc/Documents/CHAPTER_4_PW_PY/pwpy.txt&quot;) page.wait_for_timeout(3000) # You can add validation code here, e.g., wait for the file name display to update file_info = page.inner_text(&quot;#fileInfo&quot;) print(&quot;Uploaded file info:&quot;, file_info) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that the document was uploaded successfully: Figure 4.8 : Upload file Handling download file To handle file downloads in Playwright using Python, you can use the expect_download() context manager. This allows Playwright to listen for the download event when the user triggers a file download. For example, on the site https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/file-download.php , you can enter text in the text area, click the download button, and Playwright will capture the download. The downloaded file can then be saved to a custom location using download.save_as() . The following code automates all the steps mentioned above: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright import os def test_file_download(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Navigate to file download demo site page.goto(&quot;https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/file-download.php&quot;) # Enter some text in the text area text_to_write = &quot;Hello, this is a Playwright download test!&quot; page.fill(&quot;#textInput&quot;, text_to_write) # Step 1: Click &quot;Generate File&quot; button page.click(&quot;button:has-text(&apos;Generate File&apos;)&quot;) # Step 2: Wait for and click &quot;Download File&quot; link with page.expect_download() as download_info: page.click(&quot;a#downloadLink&quot;) download = download_info.value # Step 3: Save the downloaded file under downloads folder os.makedirs(&quot;downloads&quot;, exist_ok=True) file_path = os.path.join(&quot;downloads&quot;, &quot;playwright_test_file.txt&quot;) download.save_as(file_path) print(f&quot;Downloaded file name: {download.suggested_filename}&quot;) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that data is filled in the text area. Click Generate File button wait for the download file link to appear and finally click Download File link to download the file. File is downloaded under the Downloads folder: Figure 4.9 : Download File Handling iframe and JS alert Handling iframes, JS alerts, tabs, and pop-ups in automation testing using Playwright involves specific techniques to interact with these elements. In the following sections, you will see how we can handle iframe, JS alert, and tab. Handling iframe An iframe is an HTML element ( &lt;iframe&gt; ) used to embed another HTML document inside the current webpage. Think of it like a small browser window inside your main page. The following are the uses of iframes: •&#9; Embedding external content : Websites use iframes to show YouTube videos, maps, advertisements, or third-party widgets without rebuilding them. •&#9; Keeping content separate : An iframe has its own HTML, CSS, and JS environment. That means the embedded page will not easily interfere with the parent page. •&#9; Code reuse : If you have a page or tool you want to display in multiple places, you can embed it with an iframe instead of duplicating the code. Let us take an example of the site https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/iframe-demo/ , where we have a text box where we can type some text inside the text area. In the following code, you can see that we can easily type inside the text area, which is under the iframe. Here page.frame_locator(&quot;#iFrame1&quot;) allows you to target elements inside a specific iframe: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_iframe(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Navigate to the page page.goto(&quot;https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/iframe-demo/&quot;) # Locate the iframe by its id frame = page.frame_locator(&quot;#iFrame1&quot;) # Type into the contenteditable div inside the iframe frame.locator(&quot;div[contenteditable=&apos;true&apos;]&quot;).fill(&quot;Hello from Playwright &lt; Python !&quot;) # Keep browser open for a few seconds to see result able to type or no inside iframe page.wait_for_timeout(3000) browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that we can successfully type inside the text area, which is inside the iframe: Figure 4.10 : iframe Handling JS alert Alert box, confirm box, and prompt box are types of dialog boxes in web browsers used to interact with the user: •&#9; Alert box (alert) : Displays a message to the user. Used for notifications, warnings, or debugging. The user must click OK to proceed. •&#9; Confirm box (confirm) : Displays a message with OK and Cancel options. Returns a Boolean value: true if the user clicks OK , false if the user clicks Cancel . •&#9; Prompt box (prompt) : Displays a message asking the user to input some text. Returns the entered value (string) or null if cancelled. Useful for quick user inputs. Let us take an example of the site https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/javaScript-alert.php to understand the three types of alerts: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_jsAlert(): with sync_playwright() as p: # Launch browser in non-headless mode browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://testing.qaautomationlabs.com/javaScript-alert.php”) # Handle Alert def handle_alert(dialog): print(„Alert text:“, dialog.message) dialog.accept() output_alert = page.locator(&quot;#output&quot;).text_content() print(&quot;After Alert:&quot;, output_alert) page.once(„dialog“, handle_alert) page.click(&quot;button:has-text(&apos;Show Alert&apos;)&quot;) # Handle Confirm def handle_confirm(dialog): print(&quot;Confirm text:&quot;, dialog.message) dialog.accept() # Use dialog.dismiss() to cancel page.once(&quot;dialog&quot;, handle_confirm) page.click(&quot;button:has-text(&apos;Show Confirm&apos;)&quot;) output_alert = page.locator(&quot;#output&quot;).text_content() print(&quot;Confirm Alert:&quot;, output_alert) # Handle Prompt def handle_prompt(dialog): print(&quot;Prompt text:&quot;, dialog.message) dialog.accept(&quot;Playwright input&quot;) # Sends input then accepts page.wait_for_timeout(5000) # Wait to observe the prompt page.once(&quot;dialog&quot;, handle_prompt) page.click(&quot;button:has-text(&apos;Show Prompt&apos;)&quot;) output_alert = page.locator(&quot;#output&quot;).text_content() print(&quot;Prompt Alert:&quot;, output_alert) # Close the browser browser.close() In the following figure, you can see all three alerts run successfully in the above script, which launches a browser, navigates to a page, and handles the alert, confirm, and prompt dialogs: Figure 4.11 : JS alert Working with shadow DOM and tabs Shadow DOM is a web technology that enables developers to create encapsulated, isolated DOM trees within a web page . It is a key part of the web components standard, designed to improve modularity, maintainability, and style isolation in web development. Shadow DOM is a separate, hidden DOM tree attached to a regular DOM element. Unlike the main DOM, the Shadow DOM is encapsulated, meaning its styles, structure, and behavior are isolated from the rest of the page. This isolation prevents external CSS or JS from unintentionally affecting the component and vice versa. Key concepts include: •&#9; Shadow host : The regular DOM node to which the shadow DOM is attached. •&#9; Shadow tree : The internal DOM subtree inside the shadow DOM. •&#9; Shadow boundary : The boundary where shadow DOM ends and the regular DOM begins. •&#9; Shadow root : The root node of the shadow tree. To understand Shadow DOM, let us take an example of the site https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/shadow-dom . The sign-up form on this site is encapsulated within a shadow DOM, so we will need to use Playwright&apos;s shadow root handling to access and fill the form fields. In the following figure you can see sign-up form fields are under Shadow DOM and can be easily seen when we inspect those fields: Figure 4.12 : Shadow DOM In the following code, you can see how can handle Shadow DOM for fields of a sign-up form: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_shadow_dom_signup_form(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/shadow-dom&quot;, wait_until=&quot;domcontentloaded&quot;) # Shadow host for the form form_host = page.locator(&quot;.signup-form&quot;) expect(form_host).to_be_visible() # Fill the fields inside the Shadow DOM form_host.locator(&apos;[name=&quot;username&quot;]&apos;).fill(&quot;play_user&quot;) form_host.locator(&apos;[type=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;).fill(&quot;play_user@test.com &quot;) form_host.locator(&apos;[name=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;).fill(&quot;P@ssw0rd!&quot;) form_host.locator(&apos;[name=&quot;confirm_password&quot;]&apos;).fill(&quot;P@ssw0rd!&quot;) # Click Submit inside the shadow root) form_host.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Submit&quot;).click() # Optional: small pause to observe page.wait_for_timeout(2000) browser.close() Working with tabs In Playwright, a tab is represented as a Page object within a BrowserContext . Each browser context can host multiple pages (tabs), and you can create, switch, and interact with these tabs programmatically. To work with tabs, you typically: 1. Create a new browser context. 2. Open new tabs using context.new_page() . 3. Handle dynamically opened tabs (e.g., via clicking a link that opens a new tab) using context.expect_page() . 4. Switch between tabs by referencing the Page objects stored in the context&apos;s pages list. 5. Close tabs or the browser context as needed. In the following code, you can see we have created the browser context. Now, navigate to the different site by opening them in new tab: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_multiple_tabs(): # Initialize Playwright with sync_playwright() as p: # Launch browser browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False, slow_mo=500) # slow_mo for demo purposes context = browser.new_context() # Create first tab and navigate to a website page1 = context.new_page() page1.goto(“https://qaautomationlabs.com/”) print(f&quot;Tab 1 Title: {page1.title()}&quot;) # print the title of the page # Create second tab and navigate to another website page2 = context.new_page() page2.goto(“https://www.python.org”) print(f&quot;Tab 2 Title: {page2.title()}&quot;) # Simulate clicking a link that opens a new tab # Using a demo site that opens a new tab on click page1.goto(&quot;https://the-internet.herokuapp.com/windows&quot;) with context.expect_page() as new_page_info: page1.locator(&quot;text=Click Here&quot;).click() # Opens a new tab page3 = new_page_info.value print(f&quot;Tab 3 Title: {page3.title()}&quot;) # Switch back to the first tab page1.bring_to_front() print(f&quot;Switched back to Tab 1: {page1.title()}&quot;) # Interact with the second tab page2.bring_to_front() page2.fill(&quot;#id-search-field&quot;, &quot;Playwright Python&quot;) page2.press(&quot;#id-search-field&quot;, &quot;Enter&quot;) print(f&quot;Performed search in Tab 2: {page2.title()}&quot;) # List all open tabs print(&quot;\nAll open tabs:&quot;) for i, page in enumerate(context.pages): print(f&quot;Tab {i + 1}: {page.title()}&quot;) # Close specific tab (e.g., the third tab) page3.close() print(&quot;\nClosed Tab 3&quot;) # Verify remaining tabs print(&quot;\nRemaining tabs:&quot;) for i, page in enumerate(context.pages): print(f&quot;Tab {i + 1}: {page.title()}&quot;) # Close browser browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that test cases are executed successfully by opening the URL in a different tab, closing it, and performing a search operation by switching to the tab: Figure 4.13 : Multi-tab Conclusion In this chapter, we gained practical insights into handling a wide range of web elements with Playwright, from basic components like input boxes, buttons, and checkboxes to advanced interactions with dropdowns, menus, radio buttons, multi-select lists, and drag-and-drop actions. We also explored managing file uploads and downloads, along with handling complex scenarios such as iframes, JS alerts, browser tabs, pop-ups, and Shadow DOM elements. In the next chapter, we will shift focus to reporting, assertions, annotations, and hooks, learning how to validate test outcomes, generate meaningful reports, and structure automation workflows more efficiently. Menus and dropdowns are usually automated by: a. Using alert handling&#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003;b. Downloading files c. Selecting visible text or index values&#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003;d.   - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 6 Debugging with Playwright and AI Tools Introduction In the previous chapter, we focused on reporting and assertions in Playwright. We began with reports in Playwright, showing how built-in options summarize test results clearly, making it easy to track successes and failures. We then explored Allure Report integration, which provides rich, interactive reports with execution timelines, screenshots, and logs, ideal for deeper analysis and team collaboration. Next, we covered assertions in Playwright, the foundation of validating application behavior. We discussed the different types, such as visibility checks, text matching, state validations, and the difference between hard and soft assertions, ensuring accurate verification. Finally, we introduced annotations with detailed examples, demonstrating how to label, group, or skip tests, improving test organization and scalability for larger suites. In this chapter, we will focus on strengthening your debugging workflow by combining proven tools with modern techniques. The journey begins with codegen for script generation, which helps you quickly generate test scripts, reducing manual effort and ensuring accuracy from the start. Once scripts are ready, the Playwright Inspector allows you to pause execution, explore selectors, and interact with the application in real-time, giving you greater visibility into what is happening under the hood. Next, we introduce the Trace Viewer, a powerful tool for replaying test runs step-by-step. With it, you can analyze network activity, screenshots, console logs, and DOM snapshots, all in one place. Beyond these tools, we will explore various debugging techniques that help isolate root causes efficiently. To push your workflow further, this chapter covers debugging with AI, showing how intelligent assistants can suggest fixes, highlight flaky patterns, and guide you toward solutions more quickly. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Codegen for script generation &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Debugging with Playwright Inspector •&#9; Various debugging techniques &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Trace Viewer •&#9; Debugging with AI Objectives The primary objective of this chapter is to help you build a solid foundation in debugging Playwright tests using modern tools and structured approaches. Debugging is one of the most critical skills for ensuring stable and reliable test automation, and this chapter will guide you through each step of the process with clarity and hands-on techniques. We will begin by exploring codegen for script generation, which accelerates script writing by automatically capturing interactions and reducing the chances of human error. From there, you will learn how to use the Playwright Inspector to pause tests, explore selectors, and interactively analyze test execution. This enables deeper insights into the flow of your scripts. The chapter also covers the Trace Viewer, a powerful utility that provides a timeline of events, DOM snapshots, logs, and screenshots, helping you diagnose issues with precision. Alongside these tools, we introduce various debugging techniques, such as effective logging, conditional retries, and network mocking, which are vital for isolating and resolving complex issues. Additionally, you will see how AI can assist in debugging, offering suggestions and insights for faster troubleshooting. Codegen for script generation Codegen for script generation in Playwright is a powerful feature that simplifies the process of creating automated test scripts for web applications. Playwright, a robust open-source automation library developed by Microsoft, supports multiple browsers like Chromium , Firefox , and WebKit , offering a unified application programming interface ( API ) for cross-browser testing. Its codegen tool, accessible via the playwright codegen command, enables developers and testers to generate scripts by recording user interactions with a web application in real-time, streamlining the creation of reliable and maintainable test scripts. The codegen feature works by launching a browser instance and a simple user interface ( UI ) where users can interact with a target website. As actions like clicking buttons, filling forms, or navigating pages are performed, Playwright records these interactions and translates them into executable code in languages like JavaScript, TypeScript, Python, or C#. For instance, running the command npx playwright codegen example. com opens a browser and a codegen window, capturing actions as a script. This is particularly beneficial for those new to automation or when dealing with complex web applications, as it reduces the need to manually write intricate selector logic or handle dynamic elements. Pros and cons of using codegen Codegen in Playwright offers significant advantages for creating automated test scripts, enhancing efficiency and reliability in web testing. The following figure shows the pros and cons of using codegen: Figure 6.1 : Pros and cons of using codegen Now, let us discuss the pros of Playwright codegen in detail: •&#9; Accelerated script creation : Playwright’s codegen tool records user interactions in real-time, instantly generating scripts in JavaScript, TypeScript, Python, or C#. This eliminates manual coding of repetitive actions, saving time for developers and testers. •&#9; Beginner-friendly : Codegen is ideal for those new to automation. By translating clicks, form inputs, and navigation into code, it lowers the learning curve, enabling non-programmers to create functional test scripts. •&#9; Robust selector generation : The tool prioritizes reliable selectors, such as Accessible Rich Internet Applications ( ARIA ) attributes or text-based locators, over fragile CSS paths. This reduces test flakiness and maintenance effort, especially for dynamic web applications. •&#9; Interactive debugging : As codegen shows scripts in real-time, users can immediately spot errors or optimize their flows while recording. •&#9; Cross-browser compatibility : Codegen scripts work seamlessly across Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit, ensuring consistent test behavior without requiring browser-specific adjustments. Now, let us discuss the cons of Playwright codegen: •&#9; Limited customization : Generated scripts may lack complex logic, such as conditional flows or data-driven testing, requiring manual edits for advanced use cases. •&#9; Over-reliance on risk : Beginners might depend solely on codegen, neglecting to learn Playwright’s APIs, which are crucial for robust test suites. •&#9; Not ideal for large suites : Codegen is better suited for small-scale tests or prototyping, as large test suites need structured frameworks and reusable components. •&#9; Limited logic handling : Codegen captures user actions but cannot incorporate advanced test logic such as loops, conditions, or data-driven tests. These must be added manually. •&#9; Dynamic content challenges : It may struggle with highly dynamic elements, requiring manual selector adjustments for stability. •&#9; Learning curve for refinement : Generated code often needs tweaking for edge cases, which can be challenging for users unfamiliar with Playwright’s ecosystem. •&#9; Maintenance required : As applications evolve, selectors or flows may change. Recorded scripts must still be updated manually to keep tests relevant and reliable. Script generation Now, let us see how we can generate the script using codegen tool. Launching codegen is as simple as a single command. Open your Terminal, navigate to your project directory, and type: playwright codegen https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php Now, let us see how this command actually works. Here is a step-by-step breakdown of the command: 1. CLI invocation (playwright codegen) : a. The playwright prefix invokes the Playwright CLI, which must be installed via pip (e.g., pip install playwright followed by playwright install to download browser binaries). b. The codegen subcommand activates the code generator mode. This tool runs a headless or headed browser instance and injects a recorder script that monitors DOM interactions. 2. URL parameter ( https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php ) : a. This argument specifies the starting webpage. Upon execution, Playwright opens the browser directly to this shop page, which appears to be a demo e-commerce site for testing automation workflows (based on its domain, likely featuring product listings, carts, and checkout flows). b. If no URL is provided, the tool opens a blank page, but here it preloads the shop interface for immediate interaction. 3. Interactive recording process : a. Browser launch : The command starts a visible (headed) browser window by default. You can customize this with flags like --headed (explicitly visible) or --device=&quot;iPhone 12&quot; to simulate mobile viewports. b. Action capture : As you perform actions—such as hovering over products, clicking Add to Cart buttons, typing into search fields, or scrolling—the tool records these in real-time. It uses Playwright&apos;s selector engine (supporting CSS, XPath, text-based, and role-based locators) to identify elements robustly, avoiding brittle attributes like IDs that might change. c. Multi-browser support : By default, it records for Chromium, but you can switch tabs to record for other browsers simultaneously, ensuring cross-compatibility. d. Output generation : The recorded actions are displayed in a sidebar pane within the browser, showing code in your chosen language (Python in this case). It auto-generates a script that you can copy-paste into a .py file. https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php In the UI, when we run the command playwright codegen https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/shop.php , it launches the browser with the provided URL and opens the Playwright Inspector. In the following figure, you can see the browser launch with shopping site and Playwright Inspector on the right side: Figure 6.2 : Playwright Inspector Let us explain each action item of Playwright Inspector: Figure 6.3 : Playwright record •&#9; Record button : This toggles the recording of actions. When enabled, any interactions performed in the opened browser window (like clicking a link or typing text) are automatically translated into Playwright test code in the Inspector. This helps quickly generate scripts by mimicking user activity. •&#9; Pick locator : This lets testers hover over UI elements in the browser and instantly receive the required selector code for targeting those elements with Playwright (e.g., &apos;locator(&apos;.row&apos;).first()&apos;) . This streamline writing automated tests by letting users fetch robust and reliable selectors for any page element. •&#9; Assert visibility : Enables testers to confirm if a particular element (such as the Login button or email input) is visible on the page. This validates that the UI is rendering components correctly and the user can interact with them as expected. •&#9; Assert text : This allows verifying the correctness of displayed text (e.g., Login , button text, field labels). You can use Inspector to check that expected labels are present, ensuring both UI accuracy and accessibility. •&#9; Assert value : Used especially for input fields, this action checks whether a field (like Email or Password ) contains the correct value after input or after an action is performed, which is vital for form-field validation in login workflows. •&#9; Assert snapshot : Takes a visual snapshot of the selected element or the whole page, enabling pixel-to-pixel validation or regression checks. This is crucial for catching unexpected UI changes and visual defects that are not covered by traditional functional assertions. Other tab In the following figure, you can see we have some other tabs of Playwright Inspector that are really helpful from testing perspective: Figure 6.4 : Playwright other tab The following is the detail of other tabs of Playwright Inspector: •&#9; Code generation pane : The pane displays auto-generated test code from recording or locator picking actions. Testers can edit, copy, or run code from here, seeing immediate effects in the browser. •&#9; Target dropdown : The dropdown in the top right corner is used to select the programming language and test framework for code generation. Options shown include: o&#9; Node.js (Test Runner, Library) o&#9; Python (Pytest, Library, Library Async) o&#9; .NET C# (MSTest, NUnit, Library) o&#9; Java (JUnit, Library) o&#9; Debugger (JSONL) This is crucial for generating test scripts compatible with the desired stack, and for seamless integration into CI pipelines or local projects. •&#9; Locator, Log, Aria tabs are below the code pane : o&#9; Locator : To type, inspect, and validate selectors for UI elements. o&#9; Log : To view logs generated during script execution, useful for debugging test runs. o&#9; Aria : To inspect accessibility properties and ARIA roles, supporting inclusive design practices. Example of codegen Now, let us generate the code for the scenario with the help of codegen: 1. Login into the site https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ . 2. Click on My Account | Register . 3. Enter Personal Details . 4. Enter Password ( Password , Password Confirm ). 5. Accept Privacy Policy by clicking on checkbox. 6. Click on Continue . 7. Verify the message Your Account Has Been Created . Run the command : playwright codegen https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/ As we run the above command, it will open the browser on the left side and Playwright Inspector on the right side. As we click on various actions, they will capture right side in Playwright Inspector in the right side: Figure 6.5 : Playwright Inspector without record script Script starts generating on the right side as we perform the action. You can see in the following figure that the script is generated for a few steps: Figure 6.6 : Playwright Inspector with record script Complete script for the mentioned scenario above is generated, as shown in the following figure: Figure 6.7 : Playwright final generated script Now, let us copy and paste the above generated script and execute the script. In the following figure, you can see the test script is executed successfully: Figure 6.8 : Codegen generated code So, this way we can leverage codegen to generate the code. The next question is: can we automate iframes and Shadow DOM using Codegen? The answer is yes. When you interact with elements inside an iframe or a Shadow DOM while codegen is running, Playwright automatically understands the context and generates the correct locators and actions. In the next section, you will see some of the examples of iframe and Shadow DOM Handel iframe and Shadow DOM using codegen Let us see how we can handle iframe and Shadow DOM using Codegen. iframe handling For example, let us take an example https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/iframe-demo/ Run the command: playwright codegen https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/iframe-demo/ In the following figure, you can use codegen to capture the field that is inside an iframe: Figure 6.9 : Codegen iframe capture field In the following figure, you can see that the text we entered is captured: Figure 6.10 : Codegen iframe entered data Shadow DOM Now, let us see how we can handle Shadow DOM. For example, let us take the example: https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/shadow-dom Run the command: playwright codegen https://www.lambdatest.com/selenium-playground/shadow-dom In the following figure, you can see that using codegen, we can enter the data into the fields Username , Email Address , Password , and Confirm Password , which are inside the Shadow DOM: Figure 6.11 : Codegen Shadow DOM Debugging with Playwright Inspector When a UI test fails, screenshots and logs only tell part of the story. Playwright’s Inspector gives you a live, visual timeline of what your test is doing, lets you step through actions, and helps you fine-tune locators, all from a GUI. In Python, you can launch the Inspector in seconds and combine it with tracing for a full black box recorder of your run. Unlike traditional debuggers that rely solely on console logs or breakpoints, the Playwright Inspector offers a visual bridge between your Python code and the browser&apos;s live state. It lets you pause execution, inspect DOM elements in real-time, and even auto-generate code snippets, all while keeping your tests in headed mode for easy observation. What the Inspector actually gives you: •&#9; Step controls : Run, pause at breakpoints, and step over actions one by one. •&#9; Actionability and logs : See why an action waited (e.g., element not visible, detached). •&#9; Locator tools : Pick elements from the page and experiment with locators interactively. •&#9; Headed browser by default : You watch the browser do exactly what your test does Method 1: Launch the Inspector with PWDEBUG Set the PWDEBUG environment variable when running your pytest suite. This switches Playwright into debug mode, opens the Inspector, runs browsers in headed mode, and disables default timeouts (so nothing fails while you poke around). Run the command, as follows: PWDEBUG=1 pytest –s D etails about the flag pass in command during execution: •&#9; The -s flag ensures your test output streams directly to the console, avoiding buffering issues. •&#9; For a single file: PWDEBUG=1 pytest -s tests/my_test.py . •&#9; Target a specific test: PWDEBUG=1 pytest -s -k &quot;test_user_login&quot; . Once launched, two windows pop open: your browser and the Inspector panel. The toolbar at the top features play, pause, and step buttons, think of it as a media player for your test script. Hit play to run from the beginning, or step to advance one action at a time, with highlights syncing between your code view and the browser. Run the command, as follows: PWDEBUG=1 pytest -s tests/loginVerification.py In Figure 6.12 , you can see above command runs the loginVerification.py test with Playwright’s interactive debugger enabled, showing real-time browser actions and full console output—ideal for debugging flaky or failing tests: Figure 6.12 : PWDEBUG Method 2: Precision breakpoints with page.pause() Sometimes you do not want to step from the very beginning, you want to freeze the run at a specific moment. Drop a page.pause() in your test; execution will halt there, and the Inspector opens. From that pause you can tweak locators, run the next action, or jump around as needed. In the following code, you can see we have put pause just before clicking on login button: from playwright.sync_api import Page def test_adds_item_to_cart(page: Page): from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_Login(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) #expect(page.get_by_placeholder(&quot;Email&quot;)).not_to_have_value(&quot;wrong@demo.com&quot;) # Assert button is visible and enabled expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() page.pause() # Click the login button login_button.click() # Additional steps or assertions can follow here browser.close() This works great alongside PWDEBUG=1 or on its own during a normal run. In the following screenshot, you can see we can freeze the run at a specific moment: Figure 6.13 : Pause the command The page.pause() line is a debugging tool in Playwright that pauses the test execution and opens the Playwright Inspector . Let us discuss what it does: When the test reaches page.pause() , it: 1. Freezes execution : The test stops running at that exact point 2. Opens Playwright Inspector : A visual debugging window appears showing: a. The current state of the browser. &#9;b. The DOM structure. c. A step-by-step control panel. 3. Allows manual interaction : You can: a. Step through code line-by-line. b. Inspect elements on the page. c. Test locators manually. d. Resume, step over, or step into the next actions. e. Examine what the page looks like at that moment. Various debugging techniques Debugging in Playwright with Python offers several approaches to diagnose, analyze, and fix issues during test automation. Here are comprehensive, unique, and effective debugging methods with Playwright and Python: •&#9; Headed Mode (headless=False) : Running tests with a visible browser window allows you to observe actions as they happen, making it easier to spot visual mismatches or navigation errors. This is ideal for initial troubleshooting. o&#9; Set headless=False in your browser launch options. o&#9; Run the browser visibly to watch what is happening. Example : from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://example.com&quot;) # Your test logic here browser.close() •&#9; Slowing down execution with slow motion : Introduce deliberate delays between actions to mimic human-like interactions, revealing timing-related bugs that might occur in fast automated runs. Use the slow_mo parameter (in milliseconds) when launching the browser. Example : browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False, slow_mo=250) # 250ms delay per action This will help in headed mode for a more controlled debugging session. •&#9; Leverage browser developer tools : Inject Playwright commands directly into the browser&apos;s console for on-the-fly queries, element inspection, and selector generation while paused. 1. Run with PWDEBUG=console pytest -s your_test.py , then add page.pause() to enter the console. 2. In the DevTools console, use helpers like: a. playwright.$(selector) to query a single element (e.g., playwright.$(&apos;text=Save) ). b. playwright.inspect(&apos;button&apos;) to highlight in the Elements panel. c. playwright.selector($0) to generate a robust selector from a selected DOM node ( $0 is the last clicked element). 3. Timeouts are disabled (set to 0) for unlimited interaction time. In the following figure, you can see we have injected Playwright commands directly into the browser&apos;s console: Figure 6.14 : Browser developer tools •&#9; Log network activity : Capture and log all network requests/responses to identify API failures or slow resources. The following code helps trace authentication issues, 404s, or unexpected redirects. It can be extended to assert specific API behaviors in tests. resources: def log_request(request): print(f&quot;→ {request.method} {request.url}&quot;) def log_response(response): print(f&quot;← {response.status} {response.url}&quot;) page.on(&quot;request&quot;, log_request) page.on(&quot;response&quot;, log_response) •&#9; Take screenshots and record videos : Capture page state at specific points: # Full page screenshot page.screenshot(path=&quot;debug_screenshot.png&quot;) # Element screenshot element =page.locator(&quot;#specific-element&quot;) element.screenshot(path=&quot;element_screenshot.png&quot;) o&#9; page.screenshot() captures the entire page as an image. o&#9; path=&quot;debug_screenshot.png&quot; specifies the filename where the screenshot will be saved. Record entire test execution, using the following code: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) context = browser.new_context( record_video_dir=&quot;videos/&quot;,record_video_size={&quot;width&quot;: 1280, &quot;height&quot;: 720} ) page = context.new_page() # Your test code context.close() # Video is saved when context closes Let us look at the explanation now: o&#9; Launches a Chromium browser. o&#9; headless=False runs the browser in visible mode (you see the browser window). o&#9; If headless=True (default), the browser runs in the background without showing the UI. o&#9; Creates a new browser context (like a fresh user profile). o&#9; record_video_dir=&quot;videos/&quot; tells Playwright to record a video of all page interactions and store it in the videos/ folder. o&#9; record_video_size={&quot;width&quot;: 1280, &quot;height&quot;: 720 } sets the video resolution. •&#9; Enabling Verbose API logging for granular output : When you need a text-based audit trail, crank up Playwright&apos;s logging to capture every API call, error, and parameter. It is lightweight and scriptable, perfect for continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) environments or log parsing. The following are the steps on how to set it up: 1. Prefix your test command with a debug flag to stream detailed entries to the Terminal. 2. Filter logs by prefix (e.g., pw:api ) for focus. The following are it key features: o&#9; Logs include timings, arguments, and stack traces, helping pinpoint slow operations or invalid states. o&#9; For WebKit users, note that inspector pop-ups may interrupt flow—handle with care. Example code : Run with logs: DEBUG=pw:api pytest your_test_file.py -s In the following figure, you can see we are debugging a Playwright + Pytest test. DEBUG=pw:api is a Playwright environment variable that enables detailed internal logs of every action your script performs: Figure 6.15 : Internal logs Trace Viewer Trace Viewer is a built-in Playwright GUI utility that generates a detailed, interactive report of a test execution. Unlike traditional logging or screenshots, it records a full-fidelity trace of the browser session, including user interactions, JavaScript errors, network activity, and visual snapshots at each step. This trace can be viewed offline using Playwright’s CLI, making it invaluable for debugging flaky or failing tests without re-running them. Setup Trace Viewer Add the following lines in the Python code: context.tracing.start(screenshots=True, snapshots=True, sources=True) context.tracing.stop(path = &quot;trace.zip&quot;) context.tracing.start(screenshots=True, snapshots=True, sources=True) This tells Playwright to start recording a trace for the given browser context: •&#9; screenshots=True takes screenshots at each action step. •&#9; snapshots=True captures DOM snapshots (the state of the page’s HTML and elements). •&#9; sources=True saves the source code of scripts loaded by the page (useful for debugging). context.tracing.stop(path=&quot; test results/trace.zip &quot;) This tells Playwright to stop tracing and save everything it recorded into a file named trace.zip . The following is the code to login into the application: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_Login(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) context = browser.new_context() context.tracing.start(screenshots=True, snapshots=True, sources=True) page = context.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) # Assert button is visible and enabled expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() #page.pause() # Click the login button login_button.click() # Additional steps or assertions can follow here context.tracing.stop(path = &quot; test-results /trace.zip&quot;) browser.close() Let us execute the preceding code. Command : Run the following command: first command executes the test case, and the second command opens the trace view: pytest tests/loginVerification.py playwright show-trace trace.zip When we run the command: pytest tests/loginVerification.py it will create trace.zip file under the folder test results . In the following figure, you can see the generated .zip file: Figure 6.16 : Trace .zip file When we run the command: playwright show-trace trace.zip it will open Trace Viewer. In the following figure, you can see Trace Viewer is generated: Figure 6.17 : Playwright Trace Viewer Let us explain the above Trace View screenshot with its core features, which are very useful from a testing perspective. Key features of Trace Viewer The following are some of the key features of Trace Viewer: •&#9; Timeline (top bar) : o&#9; Shows the execution flow over time with thumbnails of page states, from 0ms to ~2.8s. Example : In the screenshot above, you can see the transition from the login page (email/password form) to the shop page after login. o&#9; If a step took longer (like navigation or waiting for an element), you would see a longer gap in the timeline, making performance issues easier to catch. •&#9; Actions panel (left side) : o&#9; Displays a step-by-step breakdown of Playwright actions executed in your test. Example : It shows Navigate to ./index.php , Fill demo@demo.com , fill demo , Click Login button. o&#9; By clicking each action, you can see what the page looked like at that exact moment, making it easy to debug failures step-by-step. •&#9; Action preview (middle) : o&#9; Shows a snapshot of the web page at the chosen action. Example : You selected the step where it was filling email/password. The preview shows the login form with email filled as demo@demo.com and password as demo . o&#9; This is powerful for debugging because you can verify if the element was really visible/clickable when the script attempted the action. •&#9; Tabs (Action | Before | After) : o&#9; Let us you toggle between Before , Action , and After states for that step. Example : If you pick the click Login button step: &#9; Before : Page just before click ( Login form visible). &#9; Action : The click happening. &#9; After : The navigation to Shop page (if successful). o&#9; This is great for debugging flaky issues where the page did not change as expected after an action. •&#9; Metadata tab (beside actions) : o&#9; Give details like the browser name (e.g., &quot;chromium&quot; here, implied by the Chrome-like rendering). o&#9; Width and height of the browser window (e.g., 1280x720 if default), plus any emulated device metrics like mobile user-agent or pixel ratio. o&#9; Overall test duration (e.g., 2.8 seconds total), start/end timestamps, and sometimes per-phase breakdowns (load time, action time). •&#9; Bottom panel (DevTools-like tabs) : This section provides deep debugging tools. o&#9; Network : Shows all HTTP requests during the session. Example : When you navigated to index.php , CSS, JS, and image files were loaded. You can check if any failed (e.g., 404 or 500 errors). o&#9; Console : Captures browser logs and JS errors. Example : If the login button had a JavaScript error handler failing, it would show up here. o&#9; Source : Displays scripts, HTML, and other loaded resources. Example : Lets you verify if the login form markup had the loginBtn ID or if it changed dynamically. o&#9; Attachments : Stores artifacts like screenshots, HTTP Archive ( HAR ) files, or videos captured during the test run. Example : If you exported the test trace, you could replay the video of the login attempt in CI/CD. Debugging with AI Debugging is one of the most time-consuming parts of test automation. We often spend hours figuring out why a Playwright test failed, whether it is a flaky locator, network delay, or an environment mismatch, but with the rise of AI-assisted debugging, this process is becoming faster and smarter. Debugging Playwright tests in Python can be streamlined with AI assistants like GitHub Copilot , Claude , DeepSeek and Grok which integrates seamlessly into IDEs such as VS Code. By analyzing code context, error messages, Copilot suggests fixes, generates debugging snippets, and explains failures in natural language, reducing manual trial-and-error. This is especially useful for common issues like flaky locators, timing problems, or assertion mismatches. The traditional debugging challenge Before discussing AI-powered solutions, let us acknowledge the common debugging challenges we face with Playwright automation: •&#9; Flaky tests : Tests that pass sometimes and fail others, often due to timing issues or dynamic content. •&#9; Unclear error messages : Generic timeout errors that do not reveal the root cause. •&#9; Complex async operations : Debugging promise chains and async/await patterns. •&#9; Cross-browser inconsistencies : Tests working in Chrome but failing in Firefox or Safari. •&#9; Dynamic selectors : Elements that change between test runs. •&#9; Network-related issues : API calls failing or returning unexpected responses. How AI-powered debugging works AI debugging tools do not understand code in the human sense. Instead, they use sophisticated models trained on massive datasets of public code (from repositories like GitHub ), documentation, and bug-fix commits. This training allows them to: •&#9; Pattern recognition : They recognize patterns of errors, common bug types (e.g., null pointer exceptions, off-by-one errors), and their corresponding fixes. •&#9; Contextual analysis : They analyze the code you are working on, including the surrounding functions, variable names, and comments, to provide context-aware suggestions. •&#9; Natural language processing (NLP) : They can understand your error descriptions in plain English (or other languages), like I am getting a &apos;list index out of range&apos; error in this loop . AI-powered solution In the following table, you can see the category and problems we normally face when we write the code. In the table, we have provided some AI-based solutions: Category The problem AI-powered solution Error message analysis with AI Your test fails with a vague error message. Feed the error to AI for instant analysis and solutions. Visual debugging—screenshot analysis Test passes locally but fails in CI/CD. AI analyzes screenshots to identify visual differences. Selector generation and healing Selectors break when UI changes. AI generates multiple robust selector strategies. Pattern Recognition for flaky tests Tests randomly fail at different steps. AI analyzes failure patterns to identify the root cause. Network and API debugging Test fails due to API errors or network issues. AI analyzes network logs to pinpoint API problems. Console error analysis Test passes but JavaScript errors occur. AI interprets console errors and suggests fixes. Test data issues Test fails because test data is invalid or missing. AI validates and generates appropriate test data. Smart assertion validation Assertions fail with unclear reasons. AI provides context-aware assertion messages. Table 6.1 : AI-powered solution for different error categories The following are some live examples of how we can do the debugging of the code: •&#9; Using AI assistants for code analysis : Modern AI assistants like Claude , ChatGPT , or GitHub Copilot can analyze your Playwright test code and provide debugging insights. Paste a Playwright error (e.g., from a failed test run) into any LLM model for instant analysis and code suggestions. When this test fails, you can provide the error message and code to an AI assistant, which might identify: o&#9; The selector might be wrong or dynamic. o&#9; A wait condition might be needed. o&#9; The element might be inside an iframe. o&#9; The page might not have fully loaded. Example : Let us take an example where button name locator is wrong: page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) # Fill email and password page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) # Locate the login button login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtnSubmit&quot;]&apos;) Let us run the above code.
