  - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 7 Page Object Model, Pytest Fixtures, and AI Integrations Introduction In the previous chapter, we focused on strengthening your debugging workflow by combining proven tools with modern techniques. The journey began with codegen for script generation, a feature designed to help you quickly generate test scripts, reducing manual effort and ensuring accuracy from the start. Once your scripts were in place, the Playwright Inspector allowed you to pause execution, explore selectors, and interact with your application in real-time, giving you deeper visibility into what is happening under the hood. Next, we explored the Trace Viewer, a powerful tool for replaying test runs step-by-step. With it, you can examine network activity, screenshots, console logs, and DOM snapshots, all within a single, unified interface. Beyond these tools, we also introduced a variety of debugging techniques to help you isolate root causes efficiently and build more stable tests. Finally, we explored how we can leverage artificial intelligence ( AI ) in debugging. In this chapter, we will explore one of the most widely used design patterns in test automation the page object model ( POM ) and understand how it contributes to building clean, maintainable, and scalable test frameworks. You will learn how POM helps separate the test logic from the user interface ( UI ) structure, making automation frameworks more organized and easier to maintain. We will begin by understanding what the POM Pattern is and discuss the benefits it provides, such as improved readability, reusability, and reduced maintenance effort. However, no pattern is perfect, so we will also examine the disadvantages of POM design pattern. Next, we will move on to the practical implementation of the POM for end-to-end ( E2E ) testing, where you will see how to design and structure page classes, create base utilities, and write test cases using the Pytest framework. This section will guide you through the process of building a robust, modular, and reusable automation architecture. To support our test structure, we will introduce Pytest fixtures, a powerful feature that simplifies test setup and teardown. Finally, we will look at how modern automation frameworks are evolving by integrating AI into the POM. With AI integration, tests can become smarter through features like self-healing locators, intelligent element identification, and visual validation, allowing automation frameworks to adapt dynamically to UI changes without manual updates. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; About POM Pattern &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Benefit of Using POM Pattern •&#9; Downsides of POM Pattern &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Understanding Pytest fixtures •&#9; Creating POM for E2E &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; POM with AI integration Objectives By the end of this chapter, you will learn the core concepts of POM, explaining how it simplifies automation design and promotes reusability through modular architecture. We will also discuss the key advantages and limitations of using POM, highlighting its strengths in maintaining test consistency while acknowledging potential challenges such as increased initial setup effort. Next, you will move on to the practical implementation of POM for E2E testing using the Pytest framework. This includes designing and structuring page classes, developing reusable base utilities, and writing efficient test cases that adhere to clean coding principles. To enhance your test architecture further, you will learn how to use Pytest fixtures, a powerful feature that simplifies test setup and teardown processes. Finally, you will learn in very brief detail how AI is reshaping the way automation frameworks are designed and maintained. About POM pattern In software testing, automation frameworks are designed to improve the efficiency, accuracy, and speed of test execution. However, as applications become more complex, automation code can quickly become difficult to maintain, especially when UI elements change frequently. To overcome these challenges, testers and developers follow specific design patterns that promote modularity, reusability, and maintainability in test scripts. One of the most widely used design patterns in test automation is the POM. POM is a design pattern used in test automation that helps make your test scripts more structured, maintainable, and reusable. It is primarily used when automating web applications using tools like Selenium , Playwright , or Cypress . The following figure depicts POM pattern: Figure 7.1 : POM pattern Core principles of POM The POM is based on a few key principles: •&#9; Encapsulation of page elements : Each page or component of the web application is encapsulated within a dedicated class, making it self-contained and modular. •&#9; Abstraction of UI interactions : The implementation details (locators and interaction logic) are hidden from the test scripts. The test case only calls high-level methods such as login() or searchProduct() , without knowing the internal details. •&#9; Single source of truth : All locators and actions related to a particular page exist in a single class. This reduces duplication and ensures consistency across tests. •&#9; Reusability : Since each page class is independent, it can be reused across multiple test scenarios, saving time and effort. Structure of the POM A typical POM-based automation framework follows this logical structure: •&#9; Page classes : Each class represents a single-page or component. It includes: o&#9; Variables (locators for web elements). o&#9; Methods (actions to interact with those elements). •&#9; Test classes : These classes contain the actual test logic, they use methods from the page classes to perform business-level actions and validations. •&#9; Utility or helper classes (optional) : Contain reusable functions such as configuration loading, browser setup, and reporting. This modular design separates the what to test (test logic) from the how to test (page details). Explaining the POM with an analogy The POM is one of the most popular design patterns in test automation. It is widely used with tools like Selenium, Playwright, and Cypress to make automation scripts more organized, maintainable, and readable. For many beginners, the term POM sounds abstract until you look at it through a simple analogy. Let us take an analogy to explain POM. •&#9; Restaurant analogy : Think of your application as a restaurant, your automation script as the customer, and the waiter as the page object. When you want food, you do not walk into the kitchen and cook it yourself. Instead, you: o&#9; Tell the waiter what you want. o&#9; The waiter communicates with the kitchen (the application’s internals). o&#9; You simply get your food served (the outcome). In the above case: o&#9; The menu is like your test script; it defines what actions (tests) you can perform. o&#9; The waiter is the page object; it knows how to talk to the kitchen (UI elements) and get things done. o&#9; The kitchen is the application, the underlying functionality you are testing. So, when something changes in the kitchen (for example, they change the stove or ingredients), you do not have to rewrite the menu; only the waiter (page object) adjusts how it communicates. •&#9; Library analogy : Think of a library. You want to borrow a book. Instead of going into the storage room and searching through thousands of shelves yourself (which represents messy, direct UI automation), you follow the following steps: 1. &#9; Go to the librarian (the page object). 2 . &#9; Tell them which book you want (the action). 3 . &#9; The librarian knows where to look and brings it to you (the locator logic). So, your interaction remains simple, while the librarian handles the complexity. That is exactly how the POM works; it hides complexity behind a clean, user-friendly interface. So, if we sum up all the above analogies together, each of these analogies points to the core idea behind. POM : Separate the what (test logic) from the how (UI interaction). Benefit of using POM pattern Using the POM pattern in test automation offers significant benefits for maintaining, scaling, and improving the quality of automated test suites. Here are some of the benefits of using POM. Let us discuss it using the following figure: Figure 7.2 : Benefit of using POM •&#9; Improved code reusability : Page objects encapsulate all interactions with a UI page. These can be reused across multiple test scripts, eliminating code duplication and making tests shorter and cleaner. •&#9; Enhanced maintainability : When the UI changes (e.g., a button&apos;s ID is modified), you only need to update the code in one place, the corresponding page object. All tests using that object will automatically pick up the change, drastically reducing maintenance effort. •&#9; Reduced code duplication : Test logic (e.g., navigation, form filling) is stored within page object methods. Tests simply call these methods, leading to Don&apos;t Repeat Yourself ( DRY ) and more efficient code. •&#9; Increased readability and clarity : Tests are written in a business-level, user-centric language (e.g., loginPage.enterCredentials(&quot;user&quot;, &quot;pass&quot;) ). This makes them easier to read, understand, and review for non-technical stakeholders like product managers. •&#9; Stronger separation of concerns : POM creates a clear separation between the test code (the what to test) and the page-specific code (the how to interact with the page). This makes the architecture cleaner and more modular. •&#9; Improved collaboration : Quality assurance ( QA ) engineers can work on building and maintaining the page objects and their methods, while Software Development Engineer in Test ( SDETs ) or developers can focus on writing the core test logic and framework, enabling parallel workstreams. •&#9; Higher reliability of tests : By centralizing element locators and interaction methods, the chances of using inconsistent or outdated locators in tests are minimized, leading to more stable and reliable automated suites. •&#9; Easier refactoring : As the application evolves, refactoring becomes less risky and time-consuming because UI change impacts are isolated to the page objects, not scattered throughout thousands of test lines. •&#9; Tool independence (to some extent) : While not fully tool-agnostic, the pattern abstracts the UI interactions. If you need to change a selector or interaction type, it is confined to the page object, making the test logic itself less dependent on the specific automation tool&apos;s implementation details. We have many advantages of using POM, but there are some downsides to this pattern as well. In the next section, you will see the Downsides of POM pattern . Downsides of POM pattern While the POM is widely adopted, it has several significant downsides that can become apparent as a test suite grows in size and complexity: •&#9; Unmaintainable page classes : As an application grows, a single-page can have dozens of elements and interactions. The corresponding Page Object can become a massive with hundreds of lines of code, making it difficult to navigate and maintain, defeating its original purpose. •&#9; Increased code complexity : POM introduces an additional layer of abstraction. For small projects or simple applications, this can be overkill, leading to more code to write and manage without significant benefit. The initial setup requires more thought and structure. •&#9; Propagation of UI changes : While POM localizes the impact of a UI change, it does not eliminate it. A change to a common UI component (like a navigation bar or header present on multiple pages) still requires updates in every page object that uses it, leading to significant refactoring. •&#9; Poorly structured test code : The pattern does not enforce how you write your test logic. It is still easy to write procedural, non-declarative tests that are fragile and hard to understand, even if they use page objects. The pattern is a tool, not a guarantee of good tests. •&#9; Tight coupling to the UI : POM is fundamentally tied to the UI structure. If the UI is unstable and changes frequently, the page objects require constant maintenance. The tests are also not truly black box, as their structure mirrors the UI. •&#9; Learning curve for beginners : New automation engineers must understand both the testing framework and the POM abstraction. Concepts like returning other page objects from methods can be confusing initially. •&#9; Overhead for simple actions : Performing a simple, one-off action might require creating a method in a page object, which can feel like unnecessary overhead compared to a simple scripted line in a test. Understanding Pytest fixtures In Pytest, a fixture is a powerful feature that allows you to setup and tear down reusable test resources. They help you prepare the test environment before running tests and clean up afterward if needed. Think of a fixture as a function that prepares something your test needs, like: •&#9; A database connection •&#9; A logged-in user session •&#9; A temporary file •&#9; A mock API response •&#9; A configured browser instance (e.g., in Playwright or Cypress or Selenium) Fixtures in Pytest are primarily categorized by their scope, which determines how long the fixture lives and when it is recreated. This helps balance efficiency (reusing resources) with test isolation (avoiding interference). There are four main scopes: function, class, module, and session. Additionally, fixtures can have special behaviors like being autouse (running automatically without explicit requests) or parameterized (running multiple times with different inputs). Overview of fixture scopes In the following table, you can see the scope of each fixture in detail. The explanation of each example is given in detail in later sections. Scope Description When it is created/teardown Best for Function Runs once per test function. Most isolated, but can be resource-intensive if setups are heavy. Default scope if unspecified. Before/After each def test_... () call Quick, independent tests needing a fresh state (e.g., per-user login). Class Runs once per test class. Shared within the class but isolated across classes. Before/After the entire @pytest.mark.usefixtures class or all tests in it Grouped tests for the same object (e.g., multiple checks on one user profile). Module Runs once per Python module (file). Shared across all tests in that file. Before/After all tests in the module Tests in a single file that reuse moderate resources (e.g., database connection per test suite). Session Runs once for the entire test run (all files). Most efficient for global setups. Before/After the whole Pytest invocation Heavy, shared resources like servers or configs (e.g., starting a test database). Table 7.1 : Fixture scopes The following are some other notable fixture types or modifiers: •&#9; Autouse fixtures : These run automatically for all tests in their scope without needing to be requested (via autouse=True ). Useful for global logging or mocks. •&#9; Parameterized fixtures : Use @pytest.fixture(params=[...]) to run the fixture multiple times with different values, generating multiple test invocations. •&#9; Yield fixtures : Use yield to define teardown code that runs after the test, even if it fails (like a finally block). Now, let us see some examples of each scope. You can control how often a fixture runs using the scope parameter: @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;function&quot;) Here is the example of fixture with function scope: # test_function_scope.py import pytest @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;function&quot;) def user(): print(&quot;\n[SETUP] Creating a fresh product&quot;) yield {&quot;id&quot;: 1, &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Mango&quot;} print(&quot;[TEARDOWN] Deleting product&quot;) def test_user_name(user): assert user[&quot;name&quot;] == &quot;Mango&quot; def test_user_id(user): assert user[&quot;id&quot;] == 1 This code uses a @pytest.fixture with scope=&quot;function&quot; (default), meaning it runs once per test. Let us see how. •&#9; The user fixture : o&#9; Prints a setup message. o&#9; Provides a product dictionary {&quot;id&quot;: 1, &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Mango&quot;} to the test. o&#9; After each test, prints a teardown message (using yield ). •&#9; Two tests use this fixture : o&#9; One checks that the product name is &quot;Mango&quot; . o&#9; The other checks the ID is 1 . Since the scope is functional, the setup and teardown happen twice, once for each test, ensuring clean, isolated tests. Output : The following is the output. You can see the setup and teardown happen twice, once for each test: Figure 7.3 : Function fixture Fixture that is created once per test class @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;class&quot;) Here is the example of fixture with class scope: import pytest @pytest.fixture(scope=”class”) def database(): print(&quot;\n[SETUP] Connecting to DB (once per class)&quot;) yield &quot;db_connection&quot; print(&quot;[TEARDOWN] Closing DB connection&quot;) class TestUserQueries: def test_fetch_user(self, database): assert database == &quot;db_connection&quot; def test_update_user(self, database): assert database == &quot;db_connection&quot; The preceding code uses a @pytest.fixture with scope=&quot;class&quot; , meaning it runs once per test class. •&#9; The database fixture : o&#9; Sets up a DB connection (prints a setup message). o&#9; Provides the string db_connection to tests. o&#9; Cleans up after all tests in the class finish (prints a teardown message). •&#9; The TestUserQueries class contains two tests that both use the database fixture. It is because the scope is class , the setup and teardown happen only once, even though there are two tests, improving efficiency while keeping tests grouped logically. Output : The following is the output. The setup and teardown happen only once even though there are two tests: Figure 7.4 : Class fixture Fixture that runs once per Python test module @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;module&quot;) Here is the example of fixture with module scope: import pytest @pytest.fixture(scope=”module”) def browser(): print(&quot;\n[SETUP] Launching browser (once per file)&quot;) browser_instance = &quot;Chromium&quot; yield browser_instance print(&quot;[TEARDOWN] Closing browser&quot;) def test_homepage(browser): assert browser == &quot;Chromium&quot; def test_login_page(browser): assert browser == &quot;Chromium&quot; The preceding code uses a @pytest.fixture with scope=&quot;module&quot; , meaning it runs once per test file. •&#9; The browser fixture: o&#9; Launches a browser (prints setup message). o&#9; Provides the string &quot;Chromium&quot; to tests. o&#9; Closes the browser (prints teardown message) after all tests in the file finish. •&#9; Two tests ( test_homepage and test_login_page ) both use this shared browser instance. Setup and teardown happen only once for the entire file—ideal for expensive resources like browsers—while keeping tests fast and clean. Output : Figure 7.5 shows the output. The setup and teardown happen only once: Figure 7.5 : Module fixture @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot; session &quot;) Below the example of fixture with session scope The following code can be kept in conftest.py file that initializes shared configuration once for the entire test run and makes it available to multiple test cases: # conftest.py (or in any test file) import pytest @pytest.fixture(scope=”session”) def global_config(): print(&quot;\n[SETUP] Loading global config (once per session)&quot;) config = {&quot;env&quot;: &quot;test&quot;, &quot;timeout&quot;: 30} yield config print(&quot;[TEARDOWN] Cleaning up global config&quot;) # test_session_scope.py def test_env(global_config): assert global_config[&quot;env&quot;] == &quot;test&quot; def test_timeout(global_config): assert global_config[&quot;timeout&quot;] == 30 The preceding code uses a session-scoped @pytest.fixture called global_config : •&#9; It runs once for the entire test session (all test files). •&#9; Setup : Loads a config dictionary {&quot;env&quot;: &quot;test&quot;, &quot;timeout&quot;: 30} and prints a setup message. •&#9; Teardown : Prints a cleanup message after all tests finish. •&#9; Both tests ( test_env and test_timeout ) share the same config instance. Ideal for global, read-only data, efficient and initialized only once. Output : In the following output, you can see test case runs once for the entire test session, perfect for global resources like API clients or shared test data: Figure 7.6 : Session fixture Now, let us use scope session in an actual using some example: import pytest from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect # Session scoped - fixture: Launch browser once for the entire test session @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;session&quot;) def browser(): print(&quot;=== SESSION: Launching browser ===&quot;) with sync_playwright() as p: browser_instance = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) # Visible for demo yield browser_instance print(&quot;=== SESSION: Closing browser ===&quot;) browser_instance.close() # Module scoped - fixture: Create a browser context once per module (file) @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;module&quot;) def context(browser): print(&quot;=== MODULE: Creating browser context ===&quot;) context_instance = browser.new_context() yield context_instance print(&quot;=== MODULE: Closing browser context ===&quot;) context_instance.close() # Function-scoped - fixture: Runs per test function (e.g., for isolated actions) @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;function&quot;) def take_screenshot(context): def _screenshot(page, name=&quot;screenshot&quot;): print(f&quot;=== FUNCTION: Taking screenshot for {name} ===&quot;) page.screenshot(path=f&quot;{name}.png&quot;) yield _screenshot # Standalone function tests (no class) to demo function/module/session scopes def test_home_page_title(context, take_screenshot): page = context.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/”) assert &quot;Your Store&quot; in page.title() # Fixed: Actual title is &quot;Your Store&quot; take_screenshot(page, &quot;home_page&quot;) page.close() def test_search_functionality(context, take_screenshot): page = context.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/”) page.fill(&apos;input[name=&quot;search&quot;]&apos;, &quot;iPhone&quot;) page.click(&apos;.input-group-btn&apos;) assert page.locator(&apos;h1&apos;).inner_text() == &quot;Search - iPhone&quot; take_screenshot(page, &quot;search_results&quot;) page.close() # Class-based tests to demo class scope alongside others # class TestProductPage: # Class-scoped fixture: Runs once per class (setup for all methods) @pytest.fixture(scope=”class”) def setup_page(context): print(&quot;=== CLASS: Navigating to products page ===&quot;) page = context.new_page() page.goto(“https://naveenautomationlabs.com/opencart/index.php?route=product/category&amp;path=20”) yield page print(&quot;=== CLASS: Closing class page ===&quot;) page.close() def test_product_count(setup_page, take_screenshot): setup_page.wait_for_selector(&quot;h4 a&quot;, state=&quot;visible&quot;, timeout=15000) count = setup_page.locator(&quot;h4 a&quot;).count() assert count &gt; 0 take_screenshot(setup_page, &quot;product_count&quot;) def test_first_product_link(setup_page, take_screenshot): first_link = setup_page.locator(&quot;h4 a&quot;).first first_link.click() assert &quot;Apple Cinema 30&quot; in setup_page.title() take_screenshot(setup_page, &quot;first_product&quot;) Let us understand the preceding code: •&#9; test_home_page_title : o&#9; Opens the homepage. o&#9; Checks that the page title contains Your Store . o&#9; Takes a screenshot named home_page.png . •&#9; test_search_functionality : o&#9; Opens the homepage. o&#9; Searches for iPhone using the search bar. o&#9; Verifies the header text is Search - iPhone . o&#9; Takes a screenshot search_results.png . •&#9; test_product_count : o&#9; Opens the products page (via class fixture). o&#9; Waits for product links to appear. o&#9; Ensures at least one product is listed. o&#9; Takes a screenshot product_count.png . •&#9; test_first_product_link : o&#9; Clicks the first product link on the page. o&#9; Checks the page title includes Apple Cinema 30 . o&#9; Takes a screenshot first_product.png . Let us see the scope that we used in the preceding code: •&#9; browser ()—session scoped : o&#9; Runs once per test session. o&#9; Launches a Chromium browser ( headless=False |visible). •&#9; context(browser)—module scoped : o&#9; Runs once per test file (module). o&#9; Creates a new browser context (like an isolated incognito window). o&#9; Ensures tests in the same file share cookies/storage. •&#9; take_screenshot(context)—function scoped: o&#9; Runs for each individual test function. o&#9; Provides a helper function _screenshot() that takes a screenshot of the page. •&#9; setup_page(context)—class scoped: o&#9; Runs once per test class. o&#9; Opens a specific Products page before all class tests. o&#9; Closes the page when the class tests are done. Creating POM for E2E Creating a POM for E2E testing helps structure test automation by separating test logic from UI interactions. In the below use case let us implement POM using the example of the site: https://opensource-demo.orangehrmlive.com/web/index.php/auth/login . 1. Open the URL https://opensource-demo.orangehrmlive.com/web/index.php/auth/login . 2. Enter username: Admin and password: admin123 . 3. Click on Login button. 4. Verify user logged in with verifying Upgrade button. 5. Click on Dashboard link. 6. Click on Performance link. 7. Click on Recruitment link. Let us implement the steps without POM. The following is the code without POM: import re from playwright.sync_api import Page, expect def test_example(page: Page) -&gt; None: page.goto(&quot;https://opensource-demo.orangehrmlive.com/web/index.php/auth/login&quot;) expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;img&quot;, name=&quot;company-branding&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.get_by_role(&quot;textbox&quot;, name=&quot;Username&quot;).click() page.get_by_role(&quot;textbox&quot;, name=&quot;Username&quot;).fill(&quot;Admin&quot;) page.get_by_role(&quot;textbox&quot;, name=&quot;Password&quot;).fill(&quot;admin123&quot;) page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;).click() expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;,name =&quot;Upgrade&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Dashboard&quot;).click() expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;navigation&quot;, name=&quot;Sidepanel&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Performance&quot;).click() expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;navigation&quot;, name=&quot;Sidepanel&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;, name=&quot;Recruitment&quot;).click() expect(page.get_by_role(&quot;navigation&quot;, name=&quot;Sidepanel&quot;)).to_be_visible() In the following code, you see that without POM, your test works but is fragile and repetitive. Using accurate locators and proper waits fixes reliability, and POM later improves maintainability. Issue without POM The following are some of the issues that normally come up when we do not use proper design patterns like POM: Problem Description Unreliable locators Using get_by_role() with the wrong role/name — does not match real elements. No synchronization (timing issues) No explicit wait after login or navigation assertions may run too early. Repeated/Duplicate code Same locators and actions are used multiple times. Hard to maintain If UI changes (IDs, labels), must update every test manually. No reusability Login, navigation, and verification logic are not reusable in other tests. Poor readability All steps are inline no clear separation of what vs. how . Tight coupling Tests are tightly coupled with UI elements instead of business actions. No data separation Test data (username, password, URLs) hardcoded. No error handling or validation If one step fails (like login), test continues or crashes abruptly. No data separation Test data (username, password) hardcoded inside the test. Difficult debugging No structured logs or clear failure point. No test scalability One long test script does not scale for multiple pages/features. Table 7.2 : List of issues without POM Let us solve the preceding problem by implementing POM. Let us start with creating a folder structure: Create folder POM | under POM create two sub folders with name pages and tests : Figure 7.7 : Folder structure of POM For the orange app, let us create a pages class first, so we need two pages classes to cover the above scenario: one is the login page, and another is the homepage. Figure 7.8 : Pages, tests class In the following code page, qaautomationlabs_login_page.py handles login actions, navigating to the site, entering credentials, and verifying success. Page qaautomationlabs_home_page.py manages post-login interactions, verifying login success and clicking dashboard, performance, or recruitment links. qaautomationlabs_login_page.py : from playwright.sync_api import Page from playwright.sync_api import expect class LoginPage: def __init__(self, page:Page): self.page = page self.username_textbox = page.get_by_role(&quot;textbox&quot;, name=&quot;Username&quot;) self.password_textbox = page.get_by_role(&quot;textbox&quot;, name=&quot;Password&quot;) self.login_button = page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;) def goto(self,url): self.page.goto(url) def enterUsername(self, username): self.username_textbox.click() self.username_textbox.fill(username) def enterPassword(self, password): self.password_textbox.fill(password) def clickLogin(self): self.page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;, name=&quot;Login&quot;).click() def verifyLoginSuccess(self): expect(self.page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;,name =&quot;Upgrade&quot;)).to_be_visible() qaautomationlabs_home_page.py : from playwright.sync_api import Page from playwright.sync_api import expect class HomePage: def __init__(self, page:Page): self.page = page self.upgrade_button = page.get_by_role(&quot;button&quot;,name =&quot;Upgrade&quot;) self.perormance_link = page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;,name =&quot;Performance&quot;) self.dashboard_link = page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;,name =&quot;Dashboard&quot;) self.recruitment_link = page.get_by_role(&quot;link&quot;,name =&quot;Dashboard&quot;) def verifyLoginSuccess(self): expect(self.upgrade_button).to_be_visible() def clickDashboard(self): self.dashboard_link.click() def clickPerformance(self): self.perormance_link.click() def clickRecruitment(self): self.recruitment_link.click() Now, let us create the test class that reads the URL, username, and password from a config.json file (refer to Figure 7.8 ), launches a Chromium browser, opens a login page, enters credentials, and clicks the login button. It uses for cleaner, reusable code structure. qaautomationlabslogin_test.py : import os import json from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect,Page from pages.qaautomationlabs_login_page import LoginPage from pages.qaautomationlabs_home_page import HomePage def test_Login(): with open(&quot;config.json&quot;) as f: config = json.load(f) username = config[&quot;username&quot;] password = config[&quot;password&quot;] url = config[&quot;url&quot;] with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) context = browser.new_context() page = context.new_page() login_page = LoginPage(page) home_page = HomePage(page) login_page.goto(url) login_page.enterUsername(username) login_page.enterPassword(password) login_page.clickLogin() home_page.verifyLoginSuccess() home_page.clickDashboard() home_page.clickPerformance() home_page.clickRecruitment() browser.close() Let us execute the test case using the command: pytest -s POM/tests/qaautomationlabslogin_test.py In the following figure, you can see that the browser is open, and the test cases are executed successfully: Figure 7.9 : Test case execution POM with AI integration In traditional automation frameworks, the POM ensures scalability and maintainability by separating page logic from test logic. However, as modern web applications evolve rapidly, maintaining locators and writing repetitive test cases becomes time-consuming. This is where AI, especially LLM’s models transforms the automation process by combining Playwright (for browser automation) with AI-assisted design (for code generation and healing), testers can create self-adapting, self-healing, and intelligently generated test frameworks. AI integration typically involves using LLMs like GitHub Copilot to generate or optimize POM code from natural language prompts. For instance, you describe a page&apos;s functionality (e.g., Create a login page with username, password fields, and submit button ), and Copilot auto-generates the class structure, locators, and methods. This can be paired with tools like Playwright&apos;s Model Context Protocol ( MCP ) for AI-driven browser control, enabling dynamic, robust automation. Brief example Consider automating a login flow on a sample e-commerce site using Playwright and Copilot-generated POM. Prompt to Copilot : Generate a Playwright POM class for a login page with username input ( #email ), password input ( #password ), and login button ( #loginBtn ) for the site https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ using Playwright and Python. In the following figure, you can see we have provided the prompt to GitHub Copilot and its generated page and test class: Figure 7.10 : POM with AI The following code defines a POM class for handling the login page of a web application using Playwright. Its purpose is to encapsulate all login-related actions and elements in one place so that test cases remain clean and easy to maintain. from playwright.sync_api import Page class LoginPage: def __init__(self, page: Page): self.page = page self.email_input = page.locator(&quot;#email&quot;) self.password_input = page.locator(&quot;#password&quot;) self.login_button = page.locator(&quot;#loginBtn&quot;) self.base_url = &quot; https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com &quot; async def navigate(self): &quot;&quot;&quot;Navigate to the login page&quot;&quot;&quot; await self.page.goto(f&quot;{self.base_url}/login&quot;) async def login(self, email: str, password: str): &quot;&quot;&quot; Login with the given credentials Args: email: User&apos;s email password: User&apos;s password &quot;&quot;&quot; await self.email_input.fill(email) await self.password_input.fill(password) await self.login_button.click() async def get_error_message(self) -&gt; str: &quot;&quot;&quot;Get the error message if login fails&quot;&quot;&quot; error_message = self.page.locator(&quot;.error-message&quot;) return await error_message.text_content() async def is_logged_in(self) -&gt; bool: &quot;&quot;&quot;Check if user is logged in successfully&quot;&quot;&quot; return await self.page.url == f&quot;{self.base_url}/dashboard&quot; The following code verifies a successful login scenario by importing required module and pages: import pytest from pages.login_page import LoginPage async def test_successful_login(page): login_page = LoginPage(page) await login_page.navigate() await login_page.login(&quot;demo@demo.com &quot;, &quot;demo&quot;) assert await login_page.is_logged_in() When we run the preceding code, it should execute and pass successfully. In case something breaks, we can ask GitHub Copilot to help fix the issue. GitHub Copilot can analyze the reason for the failure and suggest or apply the necessary corrections, effectively performing self-healing of the failed test cases to ensure the code runs successfully. So, this demonstrates how we can leverage AI to generate code. This is just a glimpse of what is possible with AI-assisted development. In the upcoming chapters, we will discuss in detail how we can further utilize AI for automation. Conclusion In this chapter, we learned how POM enhances test automation by separating test logic from the UI, leading to cleaner, reusable, and maintainable frameworks. Using Pytest fixtures, we simplified test setup and teardown, improving modularity and resource management. We also explored how AI integration transforms automation through self-healing locators, intelligent element identification, and visual validation, enabling tests to adapt automatically to UI changes. The chapter will guide you through setting up and structuring a Cucumber project with Playwright, helping you implement effective BDD-based automation frameworks. You will learn how to write feature files, create step definitions, and leverage Cucumber hooks, tags, backgrounds, and data tables to build flexible and reusable test scenarios. Additionally, we will cover how to generate Cucumber HTML reports for clear, visual test insights and improved communication of test outcomes. Multiple choice questions 1. It disables parallel testing 3. What role do Pytest fixtures play in the proposed framework? a. Auto-healing element locators b. Managing setup/teardown like browser sessions and configs c. Generating screenshots from video d. Performing visual diffing by default l 4. Which AI capability is highlighted for integration with POM? a. Automatic unit test generation for backend services b.   - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 12 CI/CD Integration and Serial, Parallel Test Execution with Playwright Introduction In Chapter 11 , Visual Testing with Playwright and Generative AI , we introduced visual testing as a critical pillar of modern quality assurance, highlighting its importance in detecting user interface ( UI ) regressions that functional and accessibility tests often overlook. The chapter established core visual testing concepts, explaining how screenshots are captured, compared, and analyzed to identify unintended visual changes. Readers learned how to implement visual validation using Playwright, covering both region-level and full-page layout testing with practical, hands-on examples. We also explored the growing impact of generative AI ( GenAI ) powered visual testing tools such as Percy , demonstrating how AI helps reduce visual noise, minimize maintenance overhead, and improve overall test coverage. Together, these approaches enable teams to deliver visually consistent, reliable applications with greater confidence. This chapter focuses on integrating Playwright-based test automation into modern continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) pipelines to achieve scalable and reliable test execution. You will be introduced to GitHub Actions and learn how to design and implement workflows that automatically run Playwright tests as part of the continuous integration process. You will explore executing Playwright tests seamlessly within CI/CD pipelines to enable faster feedback and consistent validation across environments. The chapter also covers cross-browser testing with Playwright, demonstrating how to validate applications across multiple browsers to ensure compatibility. In addition, you will learn about browser contexts and how they enable independent, isolated browser sessions for parallel and secure test execution. The chapter also introduces sharding techniques for parallel test execution, helping you significantly reduce execution time and optimize overall test performance for large test suites. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Playwright tests into your CI/CD pipeline •&#9; Introduction to GitHub Actions for CI/CD •&#9; Execute Playwright tests in CI/CD pipeline •&#9; Cross-browser testing with Playwright •&#9; Sharding for parallel test case execution •&#9; Browser context for independent sessions Objectives The objective of this chapter is to introduce visual testing as a key practice in modern quality assurance, building on the accessibility foundations covered in the previous chapter. It aims to help readers understand how visual testing complements functional and accessibility testing by detecting UI regressions that automated checks often miss. This chapter focuses on core visual testing concepts, screenshot comparison techniques, and practical implementation using Playwright for both component-level and full-page validations. Additionally, it highlights the role of GenAI-powered visual testing tools in improving accuracy, reducing maintenance, and enhancing overall test coverage. Playwright tests into your CI/CD pipeline Playwright is a powerful, modern tool for end-to-end ( E2E ) browser automation testing that supports Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit across multiple platforms. Integrating Playwright tests into your CI/CD pipeline is a crucial step toward building a reliable, scalable, and high-quality test automation strategy. Designed for speed and stability, Playwright delivers consistent results across all major browser engines and operating systems. When Playwright is added to a CI/CD workflow, UI tests are automatically triggered on every code change, pull request, or scheduled build. This ensures that new features, bug fixes, and refactoring efforts are continuously validated in real browser environments before they are merged or released. By shifting browser testing earlier in the development lifecycle, teams can detect UI regressions, broken flows, and cross-browser inconsistencies much sooner. From an automation perspective, Playwright fits naturally into CI systems because it runs headless by default, supports parallel execution, and integrates smoothly with popular CI tools such as GitHub Actions , GitLab CI , Jenkins , and Azure DevOps . Its built-in auto-wait mechanisms reduce flaky tests, making CI runs more stable and trustworthy. Additionally, Playwright’s rich reporting, screenshots, videos, and trace files provide clear visibility into failures, helping teams debug issues quickly without rerunning tests locally. Understanding the CI/CD pipeline CI/CD pipeline is an automated process that streamlines how code changes move from development to production. Instead of relying on manual steps, the pipeline orchestrates building, testing, and releasing software in a consistent and repeatable way, reducing human error and speeding up delivery. The following figure illustrates how the CI/CD pipeline works: Figure 12.1 : CI/CD pipeline Let us understand what CI/CD is: •&#9; CI : It focuses on integrating code changes frequently into a central repository. Every time a developer pushes new code, the pipeline automatically triggers builds and runs tests. This immediate feedback helps teams identify bugs, integration issues, or broken functionality at an early stage, when fixes are faster and less expensive. •&#9; CD : In continuous delivery, once the code passes all quality checks, it is packaged and kept in a deployable state, ready to be released at any time. CD goes one step further by automatically pushing those validated changes directly to production without manual intervention. Benefits of integrating Playwright tests into a CI/CD pipeline Automating Playwright E2E tests in CI/CD delivers significant advantages for development teams. The details are as follows: •&#9; Early bug detection : Issues are identified immediately after code commits, reducing debugging time and preventing bugs from reaching production. •&#9; Improved code quality and reliability : Automated browser tests ensure consistent application behavior across environments, minimizing manual verification. •&#9; Faster feedback loops : Developers receive quick results on code changes, accelerating iterations and collaboration. •&#9; Parallel test execution : Playwright runs tests concurrently by default, and CI/CD tools can scale this further to shorten run times dramatically. •&#9; Reduced flakiness : Features like auto-waiting, robust selectors, and trace viewer make tests stable in automated environments. •&#9; Higher deployment confidence : Teams deploy more frequently with fewer issues, leading to faster releases and better user experiences ( UXs ). •&#9; Resource efficiency : Headless execution and sharding optimize CI costs and speed. Introduction to GitHub Actions for CI/CD GitHub Actions is a powerful, built-in automation platform that lets developers automate software workflows directly within their GitHub repositories. Modern software development demands speed, reliability, and automation. Teams need a way to build, test, and deliver code continuously without manual intervention. This is where GitHub Actions plays a crucial role in enabling efficient CI/CD workflows directly within your repository. Understanding GitHub Actions GitHub Actions are a built-in automation platform that allows you to define workflows triggered by events in your GitHub repository. These workflows can compile code, run tests, perform security scans, build containers, or deploy applications, without leaving GitHub. Workflows are defined using Yet Another Markup Language ( YAML ) configuration files stored directly in the repository, typically within the .github/workflows directory. This approach enables teams to version control their automation pipelines alongside their application code. GitHub Actions also supports a wide range of pre-built actions and integrations, allowing developers to reuse community or official components to streamline their CI/CD pipelines. Additionally, workflows can run on GitHub-hosted runners or self-hosted environments, providing flexibility for different infrastructure and security requirements. Understanding how GitHub Actions work Each step illustrated in the following figure explains how these components work together to create a complete CI/CD workflow, from triggering events to executing jobs and steps on runners: Figure 12.2 : GitHub Actions working Let us explain each component in detail: •&#9; Workflows : A workflow is an automated process defined in a YAML file stored inside your repository under .github/workflows . Each workflow represents a CI/CD pipeline. •&#9; Events : Workflows run in response to events such as: o&#9; Code push. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; Pull request creation. o&#9; Scheduled (cron jobs). &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; Manual triggers. •&#9; Jobs : A workflow consists of one or more jobs. Jobs run independently and can execute in parallel or in sequence. •&#9; Steps : Each job contains steps, which are individual tasks like checking out code, installing dependencies, or running tests. •&#9; Runners : Jobs run on runners, which are virtual machines provided by GitHub or self-hosted by your team. GitHub Actions for efficient CI/CD automation Using GitHub Actions for CI/CD is a natural choice for teams that build and collaborate on code within GitHub. It brings automation directly into your repository and removes much of the friction traditionally associated with setting up pipelines. The following list outlines why GitHub Actions are best for CI/CD: •&#9; CI/CD where your code lives : GitHub Actions is tightly integrated with your source code. You do not need external tools or complex connectors, your pipeline configuration lives alongside your application, versioned just like your code. •&#9; Event-driven automation : Workflows run automatically based on real development events such as code pushes, pull requests, scheduled jobs, or manual triggers. This ensures every change is validated at the right moment, without human intervention. •&#9; Simple and readable configuration : Pipelines are defined using YAML files that are easy to read and maintain. Even beginners can quickly understand what is happening, while advanced users can design complex workflows with conditional logic and parallel execution. •&#9; Rich ecosystem of reusable actions : Instead of reinventing the wheel, you can reuse thousands of communities and official actions for testing, building, security scanning, deployments, and notifications—saving time and effort. •&#9; Scales from small projects to enterprises : Whether you are running a single test job or orchestrating multi-stage deployments, GitHub actions scale effortlessly. You can run jobs in parallel, split workloads, and use cloud-hosted or self-hosted runners. •&#9; Built-in security and access control : Secrets, tokens, and environment variables are securely managed. Permissions can be tightly controlled at workflow and job levels, making CI/CD safer by design. •&#9; Cost-effective and developer-friendly : For many teams, GitHub Actions are either free or significantly cheaper than maintaining separate CI/CD infrastructure. Developers stay productive because everything happens in one familiar platform. Implement GitHub Actions workflow Implementing a workflow in GitHub Actions is a practical step toward building a reliable CI/CD pipeline. A workflow defines what should run, when it should run, and where it should run—all as code stored directly in your repository. This approach makes automation transparent, repeatable, and easy to maintain. Real-world CI workflow example using GitHub Actions To understand CI/CD in practice, let us walk through a real-world CI workflow implemented using GitHub Actions. This example reflects how teams validate code changes before merging them into the main branch. The scenario is CI for a web application. The following are the details: •&#9; Goal : Automatically validate every pull request by running builds, lint checks, and automated tests. If anything fails, the pull request is blocked. This ensures : o&#9; Only tested code gets merged. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; Bugs are caught early. o&#9; Code quality remains consistent. •&#9; Workflow trigger : In real projects, CI is usually triggered when: o&#9; A developer pushes code. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; A pull request is opened or updated. This guarantees that every change is verified automatically. •&#9; Real-world CI workflow (YAML example) : The following is a practical and commonly used CI workflow: name: CI Pipeline on: pull_request: branches: [ &quot;main&quot; ] push: branches: [ &quot;main&quot; ] jobs: build-and-test: runs-on: ubuntu-latest steps: - name: Checkout source code uses: actions/checkout@v4 - name: Setup Node.js uses: actions/setup-node@v4 with: node-version: &quot;18&quot; - name: Install dependencies run: npm ci - name: Run lint checks run: npm run lint - name: Run unit tests run: npm test The following list outlines a step-by-step explanation: 1. Workflow name : The workflow is named CI pipeline, making it easy to identify in the GitHub Actions dashboard. 2. Trigger events : The workflow runs when: a. Code is pushed to the main branch. b. A pull request targets the main branch. This setup protects the main branch from unstable code. 3. Job definition : The job build-and-test: a. Runs on a Linux runner ( ubuntu-latest ). b. Represents the core CI validation stage. In real projects, additional jobs (security scans, UI tests) may be added later. 4. Checkout code : The repository source code is fetched so the runner can access it. This is required in almost every CI workflow. 5. Environment setup : Node.js is installed with a fixed version to ensure: a. Consistent builds. b. Not works on my machine issues. 6. Dependency installation : Dependencies are installed in a clean and repeatable way using npm ci . This improves reliability compared to regular installs. 7. Linting : Lint checks validate: a. Code formatting &#9;b. Best practices &#9;c. Potential errors Failing early here saves time and effort later. 8. Automated tests : Unit tests verify application behavior. If any test fails: a. The workflow stops b. The pull request is marked as failed Execute Playwright tests in CI/CD pipeline Modern applications demand fast feedback, reliable releases, and consistent quality. Executing Playwright tests in a CI/CD pipeline ensures that every code change is automatically validated across browsers before it reaches production. Why run Playwright in CI? Running Playwright, Python tests locally is useful, but CI/CD execution provides much higher confidence: •&#9; Catch UI regressions early, before they reach production. •&#9; Enforce quality gates on pull requests and merges. •&#9; Produce reproducible artifacts [ JUnit Extensible Markup Language ( JUnit XML ), Hypertext Markup Language ( HTML ) for dashboards and audits]. The prerequisites are as follows: •&#9; A Python project using Playwright and Pytest. •&#9; A requirements.txt containing at least: o&#9; playwright&#9; o&#9; pytest o&#9; pytest-playwright&#9; o&#9; pytest-html •&#9; Tests following Pytest discovery rules: tests/test_*.py . •&#9; A GitHub repository with actions enabled. Refer to the given example for automating the login flow and validating the login button before clicking it. In the following figure, you can also see the required dependencies: Figure 12.3 : Login testcase The following is the code for login flow and validating the login button: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_Login(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() login_button.click() browser.close() Now, let us create .yml file in .github/workflows/playwright-tests.yml to execute the preceding test cases in CI/CD: Figure 12.4 : .yml file The following is .yml file to execute the preceding test cases in CI/CD: name: Playwright Tests on: push: branches: [ main, master ] pull_request: branches: [ main, master ] jobs: test: runs-on: ubuntu-latest steps: - name: Checkout code uses: actions/checkout@v4 - name: Setup Python uses: actions/setup-python@v5 with: python-version: &apos;3.11&apos; - name: Install dependencies run: | python -m pip install --upgrade pip python -m pip install -r requirements.txt - name: Install Playwright browsers run: python -m playwright install --with-deps chromium - name: Run tests run: | mkdir -p test-results pytest -q --junitxml=test-results/results.xml --html=test-results/report.html --self-contained-html tests/ - name: Upload test results if: always() uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4 with: name: test-results path: test-results/ retention-days: 7 The following list outlines the .yaml walkthrough, here the name is Playwright tests: •&#9; It defines the workflow name shown in the GitHub Actions UI: on: push: branches: [ main, master ] pull_request: branches: [ main, master ] •&#9; Runs the workflow on: o&#9; Code push to main or master. o&#9; Pull requests targeting main or master. o&#9; Ensures tests run for both new commits and PR validations. jobs: – Job Definition jobs: test: runs-on: ubuntu-latest •&#9; Defines a job named test. •&#9; Uses a fresh Ubuntu Linux runner. •&#9; Provides a clean, reproducible CI environment. The following is the .yaml walkthrough for checkout the code and how to configure: 1. Checkout code : - name: Checkout code uses: actions/checkout@v4 a. Clones the repository into the runner. b. Makes source code available for testing. 2. Setup Python : - name: Setup Python uses: actions/setup-python@v5 with: python-version: &apos;3.11&apos; a. Installs and configures Python 3.11. b. Ensures consistent Python version across all CI runs. 3. Install dependencies : - name: Install dependencies run: | python -m pip install --upgrade pip python -m pip install -r requirements.txt a. Upgrades pip . b. Installs all project dependencies (Playwright, Pytest plugins). c. Keeps dependency management centralized. 4. Install Playwright browsers : - name: Install Playwright browsers run: python -m playwright install --with-deps chromium a. Installs Chromium browser. b. --with-deps installs required operating system libraries for Linux. c. Ensures Playwright can run in CI. 5. Run tests : - name: Run tests run: | mkdir -p test-results pytest -q --junitxml=test-results/results.xml \ --html=test-results/report.html --self-contained-html tests/ a. Creates a directory for test outputs. b. Executes all pytest Playwright tests in tests/ . c. Generates : i. JUnit XML : CI-friendly, machine-readable. ii. HTML report : Human-readable, self-contained. d. -q runs pytest in quiet mode. 6. Upload test results : - name: Upload test results if: always() uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4 a. Uploads reports as CI artifacts. b. if: always() ensures upload even if tests fail. with: name: test-results path: test-results/ retention-days: 7 •&#9; Stores test reports for 7 days. •&#9; Enables easy debugging and result sharing. Now, let us execute the test case in CI/CD by pushing the code. In the following figure, you can see that test cases are executed in CI/CD and pass successfully: Figure 12.5 : Execution in CI/CD In the following figure, you can see the .html report is generated: Figure 12.6 : .html report Cross-browser testing with Playwright In this section, you will see how Playwright and Python test cases are executed serially and in parallel in local and CI/CD. Run test cases serially across cross-browser locally, to run Playwright Python test cases serially across multiple browsers locally, you primarily use the Playwright Pytest plugin command line interface ( CLI ). By default, Pytest runs tests sequentially (one after another), so as long as you do not enable parallel plugins like pytest-xdist , your tests will run serially. The prerequisites are that you have to ensure that the Playwright Pytest plugin and the necessary browser binaries are installed: pip install pytest-playwright playwright install Running serially across multiple browsers To execute your tests across Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit (Safari) one after the other, use the --browser flag multiple times in your command. Let us take the following example, which opens the application login page, enters a valid email, and password. Verifies the login button is visible and enabled. Clicking the Login button repeats the same flow on Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit (one-by-one). In the following figure, you can see in serial execution processes, we can run test one browser at a time: Figure 12.7 : Serial test case execution Use @pytest.mark.parametrize to run the same test logic across browsers: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect import pytest @pytest.mark.parametrize(&quot;pw_browser&quot;, [&quot;chromium&quot;, &quot;firefox&quot;, &quot;webkit&quot;]) def test_Login(pw_browser): print(f&quot;\n Running test on browser: {pw_browser.upper()}&quot;) with sync_playwright() as p: browser = getattr(p, pw_browser).launch(headless=False, slow_mo=50) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) login_button = page.locator(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) expect(login_button).to_be_visible() expect(login_button).to_be_enabled() login_button.click() browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; Imports Playwright’s synchronous API for browser automation and expects for assertions, along with Pytest for test execution. •&#9; Uses @pytest.mark.parametrize to run the same login test on Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit. •&#9; Starts Playwright and dynamically launches the selected browser in headed mode with slow motion for better visibility. •&#9; Opens a new page and navigates to the application’s login URL. •&#9; Enters the email and password into the login form fields. •&#9; Locates the Login button and validates that it is visible and enabled. •&#9; Clicks the Login button to perform the login action. •&#9; Closes the browser after the test completes. Execute test case: R un the following command to execute the test case serially. Run the same login test serially on browsers Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit: pytest -s tests/test_test_case_1.py The following figure shows the code for serial test cases execution: Figure 12.8 : Single test cases serial execution result Run test cases in parallel across cross-browser locally Modern web applications must be have consistently across different browsers. Running cross-browser tests serially increases execution time, especially as the test suite grows. Parallel execution solves this problem by running multiple browser tests at the same time on your local machine. Parallel cross-browser testing Parallel cross-browser testing focuses on validating an application across multiple browsers and environments at the same time. Instead of executing tests sequentially on each browser, tests run concurrently, which significantly reduces the overall testing time and speeds up feedback during development cycles. The following list outlines the key challenges with serial execution: •&#9; Each browser runs one after another. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Execution time increases linearly. •&#9; Slower feedback for developers. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Inefficient use of CPU cores. The following list outlines the key benefits of parallel execution: •&#9; Faster test completion. •&#9; Optimal CPU utilization. •&#9; Early detection of browser-specific defects. •&#9; Improved developer productivity. The following package must be installed to run test cases in parallel: •&#9; Install the package : We need to install the following package to execute the test case in parallel: pip install pytest-xdist pytest-xdist is a Pytest plugin that enables parallel execution of test cases across multiple CPU cores, significantly reducing test execution time. Example : Let us take three test cases that we are planning to run in parallel. In the following figure, you can see three test cases for parallel execution: Figure 12.9 : Test cases for execution •&#9; To execute the test cases in parallel, we have the following commands: o&#9; Table, pytest command : The following table outlines the different types of pytest commands to execute the test cases in parallel: Command Workers created How workers are decided CPU usage Best use case Notes pytest tests/ -n auto Dynamic (≈ CPU cores) Automatically detected by pytest-xdist High Fast execution on strong machines, API/unit tests. May overload the system for heavy UI/browser tests. pytest tests/ --numprocesses 2 Fixed (2) Manually specified Low–moderate Playwright/Selenium browser tests, limited resources. Long-form version of - n 2 . pytest tests/ -n 4 Fixed (4) Manually specified Moderate–high Balanced parallel execution for regression suites. Needs tuning based on machine capacity. Table 12.1 : Commands to run test cases in parallel •&#9; Execution : Let us execute the same 9 Playwright test cases locally using pytest-xdist with different parallel configurations. In the following figure, you can see that we use more workers to speed up the execution: Figure 12.10 : Multiple test cases serial execution result So far, we have seen how test cases are executed in local now let us see how we can execute the test cases in parallel in CI/CD. Sharding for parallel test case execution So far, we have seen how test cases are executed locally. Now, let us understand how we can execute test cases in parallel within a CI/CD pipeline. For parallel execution in CI/CD, we use the concept of sharding. Shard-based execution means splitting the entire test suite into multiple independent parts, called shards, and running them simultaneously across different runners or jobs. Each shard executes only a subset of test cases, which significantly reduces the overall execution time. This approach is especially effective for large Playwright test suites, where running everything sequentially would be slow and inefficient. By leveraging sharding in CI/CD, we achieve: •&#9; Faster feedback cycles. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Better resource utilization. •&#9; Scalable and reliable test execution. In the following figure, you can see how we can split the test cases among different shades to execute the test cases in parallel: Figure 12.11 : Sharding with multiple browser executions Refer to the following list to build an understanding of Figure 12.11 : •&#9; Large test suite (left side) : The figure starts with a large Playwright test suite containing many test cases. Running all of these tests sequentially in CI/CD would take a long time. •&#9; Split into shards : The test suite is split into multiple independent shards (Shard 1, Shard 2, Shard 3). o&#9; Each shard contains a subset of test cases. o&#9; Shards are independent and do not share state. o&#9; Example : &#9; Shard 1 : Test A running in three browsers. &#9; Shard 2 : Test B running in three browsers. &#9; Shard 3 : Test C running in three browsers. Let us take an example of executing test cases in a CI/CD pipeline in parallel using the sharding concept. We create a YAML file that defines a GitHub Actions CI/CD workflow to run Playwright E2E tests (using pytest-playwright ) in a sharded (parallelized) manner across multiple jobs. This approach speeds up execution by generating per-shard reports and then merging them into a single combined report for easier review. Pipeline triggers on pushes or pull requests to the main branch, making tests run automatically. The sharding balances test execution time across parallel runners, and the merge step ensures a unified view of results (especially useful when some shards might fail). The following is the code for .yml file for parallel test cases execution using the sharding concept: name: Playwright Python Tests - Sharded with Combined Report on: push: branches: [main] pull_request: branches: [main] jobs: test-shards: timeout-minutes: 60 runs-on: ubuntu-latest strategy: fail-fast: false matrix: shard: [1, 2, 3] # Increase this number for more parallel shards total_shards: [3] # Must match the number of shards above steps: - uses: actions/checkout@v4 - name: Setup Python uses: actions/setup-python@v5 with: python-version: &quot;3.12&quot; - name: Install dependencies run: | python -m pip install --upgrade pip pip install pytest pytest-playwright pytest-xdist pytest-split pytest-html junitparser - name: Install Playwright browsers run: playwright install --with-deps - name: Run sharded tests run: | pytest --splits ${{ matrix.total_shards }} --group ${{ matrix.shard }} \ -n auto \ --junitxml=shard-xml-${{ matrix.shard }}.xml \ --html=shard-html-${{ matrix.shard }}.html --self-contained-html - name: Upload shard reports as artifacts if: always() uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4 with: name: shard-${{ matrix.shard }}-reports path: | shard-xml-*.xml shard-html-*.html test-results/ playwright-report/ merge-reports: needs: test-shards runs-on: ubuntu-latest if: always() # Run even if some shards fail steps: - uses: actions/checkout@v4 - name: Setup Python uses: actions/setup-python@v5 with: python-version: &quot;3.12&quot; - name: Install required tools run: | python -m pip install --upgrade pip pip install junitparser junit2html - name: Download all shard artifacts uses: actions/download-artifact@v4 with: pattern: shard-*-reports merge-multiple: true path: reports - name: Merge JUnit XML reports run: | cd reports python -m junitparser merge shard-xml-*.xml merged-results.xml - name: Generate combined HTML report from merged JUnit XML run: | cd reports junit2html merged-results.xml final-report.html - name: Upload combined report uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4 with: name: combined-playwright-report path: | reports/final-report.html reports/merged-results.xml reports/test-results/ reports/playwright-report/ Execution: Let us execute the preceding code. In the following figure, you can see how GitHub Actions workflow runs Playwright Python tests using sharding. Three shard jobs execute tests in parallel, generate individual shard reports, and a final job merges them into a single combined HTML report artifact. Figure 12.12 : GitHub Actions summary The following figure shows a merged Pytest HTML report generated after sharded CI execution, combining results from multiple browsers. It summarizes test duration, pass status, and detailed outcomes for Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit runs: Figure 12.13 : Html pytest report One more example of sharding is illustrated in the following figure, where you can see shard-based parallel test execution in CI/CD, where a large test suite (Test A, Test B,..., Test N) is split into multiple shards. Each shard runs a subset of tests in parallel on separate CI/CD runners using Chrome, enabling faster, scalable feedback: Figure 12.14 : Sharding with single browser execution Browser context for independent sessions In Playwright, browser context is one of the most important concepts for building reliable, scalable, and efficient test automation. It allows testers to simulate multiple independent users within a single browser instance while keeping each user’s data completely isolated. Think of a browser context as a clean, private session, similar to opening a new incognito window—without the overhead of launching a brand-new browser every time. Understanding browser context A browser context is an isolated execution environment created inside a browser instance. Each context has its own: •&#9; Cookies &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Local storage •&#9; Session storage &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Cache •&#9; Authentication state Pages created inside the same context share data with each other, but never share data with pages from another context. In simple terms , one browser | multiple independent contexts | multiple pages per context. Importance of browser context Without browser contexts, you would need to launch a separate browser for every user or test scenario. That approach is: •&#9; Slower &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Memory-intensive •&#9; Harder to scale Browser contexts solve this problem by allowing true isolation with minimal resource usage. The following list outlines how browser context works internally: •&#9; Playwright launches a single browser process (Chromium, Firefox, or WebKit). •&#9; Inside that browser, one or more contexts are created. •&#9; Each context behaves like a fresh browser profile. •&#9; Pages opened inside a context inherit that context’s state. Let us take an example to understand browser context. In the following code, Playwright test launches a single Chromium browser and creates two independent browser contexts to simulate separate user sessions. In the first context, it logs into the QA Automation Labs Shop and captures a screenshot after successful authentication. In the second context, it logs into the Sauce Demo site using different credentials and takes another screenshot. Each context maintains its own cookies, storage, and login state, providing session isolation within the same browser. Finally, the test waits briefly for observation and then cleanly closes both contexts and the browser. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_browser_contexts_independent_sessions(): with sync_playwright() as p: # Launch a single browser instance browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) # Use headless=True in CI # -------- Context 1: QA Automation Labs Shop -------- context1 = browser.new_context() page1 = context1.new_page() page1.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php&quot;) page1.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;email&quot;]&apos;, &apos; demo@demo.com &apos;) page1.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;demo&apos;) page1.click(&apos;button[id=&quot;loginBtn&quot;]&apos;) page1.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) page1.screenshot(path=&quot;screenshot_shop_logged_in.png&quot;) # -------- Context 2: SauceDemo -------- context2 = browser.new_context() page2 = context2.new_page() page2.goto(&quot;https://www.saucedemo.com/&quot;) page2.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;user-name&quot;]&apos;, &apos;standard_user&apos;) page2.fill(&apos;input[id=&quot;password&quot;]&apos;, &apos;secret_sauce&apos;) page2.click(&apos;input[id=&quot;login-button&quot;]&apos;) page2.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) page2.screenshot(path=&quot;screenshot_saucedemo_logged_in.png&quot;) # Observation wait page1.wait_for_timeout(5000) page2.wait_for_timeout(5000) # Cleanup context1.close() context2.close() browser.close() In the following figure, you can see that under a single Chromium browser, two independent browser contexts are created: Figure 12.15 : Browser contexts Conclusion In this chapter, we explored how to integrate Playwright-based test automation into modern CI/CD pipelines for scalable and reliable testing. It introduces GitHub Actions and demonstrates how to design workflows that automatically execute Playwright tests during CI. The chapter covers running tests in CI/CD environments, enabling faster feedback and consistent validation. It also explains cross-browser testing with Playwright to ensure application compatibility. Additionally, you learned about browser contexts for isolated sessions and sharding techniques to run tests in parallel, significantly reducing execution time for large test suites. In the next chapter, we explore integrating Playwright with leading cloud providers. This chapter explains how to connect Playwright to cloud platforms to enable parallel execution, reliable cross-browser testing, and scalable test infrastructure for faster and more efficient automation.
