  - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 9 API Testing with Playwright and Al-powered Tool Introduction In the previous chapter, we explored the core concepts and practical implementation of behavior-driven development ( BDD ) using Cucumber. You learned how BDD bridges the communication gap between business stakeholders and technical teams, promoting collaboration through easily understandable test scenarios. We learned about BDD and Cucumber, understanding how they work together to describe application behavior in plain language. You learned how to setup and structure a Cucumber project effectively for better maintainability and scalability. This chapter introduces the fundamentals of application programming interface ( API ) testing, beginning with how to setup API tests using Playwright. It explains how to send API requests and validate responses, and then explores how to integrate API tests with frontend tests using AI-powered tool Loadmill. The chapter also covers techniques for mocking API data in Playwright, as well as implementing authentication and managing session storage to create reliable and secure automated test workflows. Structure The chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Introduction to API testing •&#9; Setting up API tests with Playwright •&#9; Sending API requests and validating responses • &#9; Setting up Loadmill •&#9; Integrating Playwright with Loadmill for UI automation •&#9; Mocking API data with Playwright •&#9; Authentication, and session storage in Playwright Objectives This chapter aims to help learners understand how to test APIs easily and effectively using Playwright. It explains how to setup API tests, send requests, and check the accuracy of responses. Learners will also learn how to connect API tests with frontend tests using a generative AI ( GenAI ) tool Loadmill for smoother workflows. The chapter further covers how to mock API data and handle authentication and session storage. By the end, readers will be able to create reliable and efficient API tests that support strong end-to-end automation. Introduction to API testing API testing, short for API testing, is a specialized form of software testing that focuses on verifying the functionality, reliability, performance, and security of APIs, the set of protocols, routines, and tools that allow different software applications to communicate with each other. Unlike traditional user interface ( UI ) testing, which checks how an app looks and behaves from a user&apos;s perspective, API Testing operates at a deeper, backend level by directly interacting with the API endpoints to simulate real-world data exchanges. Whether you are using a mobile app, making an online payment, or logging into a website, APIs are working behind the scenes to transmit data quickly and securely. Unlike graphical UI ( GUI ) testing, which focuses on the look and feel of an application, API testing concentrates on the business logic, data responses, security, performance, and reliability of the API itself. Understanding API through the restaurant analogy Before we test, we must understand what we are testing. Think of an API as a waiter in a restaurant: •&#9; The customer is the UI the website or app you see. •&#9; The kitchen is the server, where all the data and business logic cooks. •&#9; The waiter is the API. You, the customer, do not go into the kitchen to order your food. You tell the waiter your order (the request). The waiter takes it to the kitchen, the kitchen prepares it, and the waiter brings back your meal (the response). In the following figure, you can see how restaurant analogy fit in API automation: Figure 9.1 : API flow with analogy So, API testing is the process of verifying that this waiter and the kitchen work correctly without involving the customer (the UI). We send requests directly to the API and check the responses to ensure they are correct, fast, and secure. We are testing the business logic, data responses, and performance of the application&apos;s core. The following is why API testing matters: •&#9; Early bug detection : Since APIs sit between the UI and the database, testing them allows teams to detect issues early in the development cycle,often before the UI is even built. •&#9; Language-independent : APIs typically exchange data in standard formats like JSON or XML over HTTP/HTTPS, making them independent of the programming language used. •&#9; Improved test coverage : APIs expose more granular functionality than UIs, enabling deeper and more thorough testing. •&#9; Faster execution : API tests are generally faster to execute than UI tests because they bypass the rendering layer. •&#9; Critical for microservices and modern architectures : With the rise of microservices, cloud-native apps, and headless systems, APIs are the primary integration point—making their reliability essential. The following are the common types of API tests: •&#9; Functional testing : Validates that the API works as expected (correct responses, status codes, error handling). •&#9; Validation testing : Ensures data is correctly formatted and validated (e.g., required fields, data types). •&#9; Security testing : Checks for vulnerabilities such as authentication flaws, injection attacks, or data exposure. •&#9; Performance testing : Measures response times, throughput, and stability under load. •&#9; Contract testing : Verifies that the API adheres to a defined contract (e.g., OpenAPI/Swagger spec). •&#9; Interoperability testing : Confirms that the API works well with different systems or clients. The following are the key components of API testing. It typically covers several layers: •&#9; Functional testing : Checks if the API performs its intended actions. For example: o&#9; Does a GET request retrieve the correct user data? o&#9; Does a POST request successfully create a new record without duplicates? •&#9; Validation testing : Ensures responses adhere to predefined schemas, including status codes (e.g., 200 OK vs. 404 Not Found), headers, and payload structures. •&#9; Performance testing : Measures how the API handles load, such as response times under high traffic or concurrent requests. •&#9; Security testing : Probes for threats like SQL injection, broken authentication, or sensitive data leaks (often aligned with standards like OWASP API Security). Setting up API tests with Playwright Setting up Playwright for API automation with Pytest on both Mac and Windows involves similar steps, primarily focusing on Python environment setup and package installation. Here is a brief, step-by-step guide for both operating systems. Prerequisites (for both Mac and Windows) : •&#9; Python : Ensure you have Python 3.8 or higher installed. You can download it from the official Python website ( https://www.python.org/ ). Verify installation by running python3 --version (Mac/Linux) or python --version (Windows) in your Terminal/command prompt. •&#9; pip : Python&apos;s package installer, usually comes with Python. Verify by running pip3 --version (Mac/Linux) or pip --version (Windows). Setting up on Mac : 1. Open Terminal : Launch your Terminal application. 2. Create a project directory : mkdir CHAPTER_9_PW_PY cd CHAPTER_9_PW_PY 3. Create and activate a virtual environment (recommended) : python3 -m venv venv source venv/bin/activate 4. Install pytest-playwright : pip install pytest-playwright #this command will install both pytest and playwright. 5. Install Playwright browsers : playwright install The preceding command will download the necessary browser binaries (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) that Playwright uses. Setting up on Windows : 1. Open Command Prompt or PowerShell : Search for cmd or powershell in your Start menu. 2. Open Terminal : Launch your Terminal application. 3. Create a Project directory : mkdir CHAPTER_9_PW_PY cd CHAPTER_9_PW_PY 4. Create and activate a virtual environment (recommended) : python -m venv venv .\venv\Scripts\activate 5. Install pytest-playwright : pip install pytest-playwright This command will install both pytest and playwright. 6. Install Playwright browsers : playwright install This command downloads the necessary browser binaries (Chromium, Firefox, WebKit) that Playwright uses. pip list To display a list of all python packages installed in the current environment: Figure 9.2 : Pip list Leveraging Playwright for efficient API testing The following are some of the points that explain why we use Playwright for API testing: •&#9; Single framework for UI + API automation : o&#9; Playwright unifies test automation under one roof. Your UI tests and API tests live in the same repo, follow the same architecture, and use the same test runner. This reduces the overhead of managing multiple frameworks like Postman , Newman , or REST Assured . •&#9; Built-in APIRequestContext : o&#9; It provides a native API testing module ( APIRequestContext ) that supports GET, POST, PUT, DELETE, file uploads, authentication, and more without needing extra libraries. •&#9; End-to-end flow validation : o&#9; You can send an API request, validate the response, and immediately verify the results in the UI during the same test—ideal for true end-to-end scenarios. •&#9; Fast and lightweight execution : o&#9; API tests run faster than UI tests and can be executed headless, making Playwright a strong choice for quick feedback loops in continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) pipelines. •&#9; Automatic handling of authentication : o&#9; Playwright can store and reuse authentication states, generate tokens, and handle sessions across API and UI layers effortlessly. •&#9; Consistent cross-browser and cross-platform setup : o&#9; You can combine API assertions with browser-level checks (Chrome, Firefox, Safari) within one environment, ensuring consistency across platforms. •&#9; Rich debugging and tracing tools : o&#9; Playwright’s trace viewer, network logs, and detailed error reporting make it easier to debug failing API calls than traditional API-only tools. •&#9; Ideal for contract and integration testing : o&#9; Playwright makes it easy to validate schemas, headers, status codes, and data structures when testing microservices or integrated systems. •&#9; Reduces maintenance overhead : o&#9; Using one framework for UI and API reduces dependencies, minimizes context switching, and simplifies long-term maintenance. •&#9; Strong community and CI : o&#9; Backed by Microsoft and a rapidly growing open-source community, ensuring frequent updates, stability, and modern features. In the next section, you will how we can use Playwright for API request validation. Sending API requests and validating responses Playwright is best known for interacting with web pages in browsers like Chromium, Firefox, and WebKit; it also excels in API testing. Playwright&apos;s Python bindings allow you to send HTTP requests directly, intercept network traffic, and validate responses without spinning up a full browser context. You can send HTTP requests, validate responses, and integrate API testing into your end-to-end test suites. Let us take an example of operations Create, Read, Update, and Delete ( CRUD ). For example, purpose we are using the URL https://api.restful-api.dev/ . GET request : from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect BASE_URL = “ https://api.restful-api.dev ” def test_get_all_objects(): &quot;&quot;&quot;Test GET request to retrieve all objects.&quot;&quot;&quot; with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() context = browser.new_context() request = context.request response = request.get(f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects&quot;) # Assertions assert response.status == 200 response_body = response.json() print(&quot;the response body&quot;,response_body) assert isinstance(response_body, list) assert len(response_body) &gt; 0 # At least one object exists browser.close() Code walkthrough : •&#9; This Playwright test validates a GET API request to retrieve objects from a REST API. •&#9; It launches a browser, creates a request context, sends a GET request to the /objects endpoint. •&#9; Then verifies the response has status 200, returns a JSON list, and contains at least one object before closing the browser. POST request : import json from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright BASE_URL = “ https://api.restful-api.dev ” def test_post_create_object(): payload = { &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16&quot;, &quot;data&quot;: { &quot;year&quot;: 2019, &quot;price&quot;: 1849.99, &quot;CPU model&quot;: &quot;Intel Core i9&quot;, &quot;Hard disk size&quot;: &quot;1 TB&quot; } } with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() context = browser.new_context() request = context.request # Send POST request response = request.post( f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects&quot;, data=json.dumps(payload), headers={&quot;Content-Type&quot;: &quot;application/json&quot;} ) # Assertions assert response.status == 200 response_body = response.json() assert &quot;id&quot; in response_body assert response_body[&quot;name&quot;] == &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16&quot; assert response_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;year&quot;] == 2019 assert response_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;price&quot;] == 1849.99 assert response_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;CPU model&quot;] == &quot;Intel Core i9&quot; assert response_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;Hard disk size&quot;] == &quot;1 TB&quot; created_id = response_body[&quot;id&quot;] print(f&quot;Created object ID: {created_id}&quot;) browser.close() Code walkthrough : •&#9; This Playwright test creates a new object via POST request to a REST API. •&#9; It sends MacBook Pro details as JSON payload, validates the response status is 200. •&#9; Verifies the returned data matches the sent payload including all nested properties, confirms an ID was generated, prints it, then closes the browser. PUT request : import json from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright BASE_URL = “ https://api.restful-api.dev ” def test_put_update_object(): create_payload = { &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16&quot;, &quot;data&quot;: { &quot;year&quot;: 2019, &quot;price&quot;: 1849.99, &quot;CPU model&quot;: &quot;Intel Core i9&quot;, &quot;Hard disk size&quot;: &quot;1 TB&quot; } } update_payload = { &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16 Updated&quot;, &quot;data&quot;: { &quot;year&quot;: 2020, &quot;price&quot;: 2049.99, &quot;CPU model&quot;: &quot;Intel Core i9&quot;, &quot;Hard disk size&quot;: &quot;2 TB&quot;, &quot;color&quot;: &quot;silver&quot; } } with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() context = browser.new_context() request = context.request # First, create the object with POST post_response = request.post( f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects&quot;, data=json.dumps(create_payload), headers={&quot;Content-Type&quot;: &quot;application/json&quot;} ) assert post_response.status == 200 post_body = post_response.json() object_id = post_body[&quot;id&quot;] # Now, update the object with PUT put_response = request.put( f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects/{object_id}&quot;, data=json.dumps(update_payload), headers={&quot;Content-Type&quot;: &quot;application/json&quot;} ) assert put_response.status == 200 put_body = put_response.json() assert put_body[&quot;id&quot;] == object_id assert put_body[&quot;name&quot;] == &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16 Updated&quot; assert put_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;price&quot;] == 2049.99 # Updated price assert put_body[&quot;data&quot;][&quot;color&quot;] == &quot;silver&quot; # New field added browser.close() Code walkthrough : •&#9; Playwright test demonstrates PUT request functionality by first creating a MacBook object via POST, capturing its ID, then updating it completely with PUT using modified data (new price, storage, color). •&#9; It validates both responses return status 200. •&#9; Verifies all updated fields match the new payload before closing the browser. DELETE request : import json from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright BASE_URL = “ https://api.restful-api.dev ” def test_delete_object(): create_payload = { &quot;name&quot;: &quot;Apple MacBook Pro 16&quot;, &quot;data&quot;: { &quot;year&quot;: 2019, &quot;price&quot;: 1849.99, &quot;CPU model&quot;: &quot;Intel Core i9&quot;, &quot;Hard disk size&quot;: &quot;1 TB&quot; } } with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() context = browser.new_context() request = context.request # First, create the object with POST post_response = request.post( f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects&quot;, data=json.dumps(create_payload), headers={&quot;Content-Type&quot;: &quot;application/json&quot;} ) assert post_response.status == 200 post_body = post_response.json() object_id = post_body[&quot;id&quot;] print(&quot;the created object id= = =&gt; &quot;,object_id) # Now, delete the object with DELETE delete_response = request.delete(f&quot;{BASE_URL}/objects/{object_id}&quot;) print(&quot;the delete response body&quot;,delete_response.json()) assert delete_response.status == 200 delete_body = delete_response.json() assert &quot;message&quot; in delete_body browser.close() Code walkthrough : •&#9; Playwright test demonstrates DELETE request functionality by first creating a MacBook object via POST, capturing and printing its ID, then deleting it using DELETE request. •&#9; It validates the delete response returns status 200 and contains a message field, prints the response body, then closes the browser. Integrating API tests with frontend tests •&#9; In modern software delivery, using separate tools for API and UI testing leads to silos, inconsistencies, and unnecessary maintenance. Loadmill, an AI-powered testing platform, brings both layers together in one unified, intelligent environment streamlining automation, boosting reliability, and accelerating release cycles. By combining end-to-end validation with AI-driven insights, Loadmill empowers teams to test faster, smarter, and with greater confidence. The following are the key benefits of integrating API test with frontend: •&#9; Unified testing platform: Loadmill provides a single interface for creating, managing, and executing both API and UI tests, eliminating the need for multiple tools and reducing maintenance overhead. •&#9; Consistent test data and environments : With shared environments, secrets, and datasets, Loadmill ensures that API and UI validations use the same configuration, avoiding mismatches and improving test reliability. •&#9; AI-driven test creation : Loadmill’s AI can automatically generate test cases from API specifications, recorded user flows, or production traffic, accelerating test authoring for both API and UI layers. Once verified, you are ready to start recording both UI interactions and underlying API calls. Select From and To account and transfer the fund. i. Logout from the application. j. Pause the recording. Mocking API data with Playwright Mocking API responses is an essential testing technique it makes tests fast, deterministic, and able to exercise edge cases that are hard to produce from the real backend. Playwright provides a robust, low-overhead network interception API ( route/route.fulfill /route.continue/route.abort ) that you can use to stub responses, inject faults, shape latency, and assert what the app requested. Benefits of mocking API data Mocking API data provides a stable, predictable, and flexible foundation for testing and development. Instead of depending on a real backend which may be slow, unstable, or incomplete, you simulate responses and control every aspect of the data. This leads to faster workflows, more reliable tests, and greater freedom when building features. Let us try to understand with an analogy: API mocking is like training a pilot in a flight simulator. The simulator mimics real conditions—weather, turbulence, engine failures without ever putting the pilot at risk. Similarly, mocked APIs let you test normal flows and failures safely without touching real systems. The following are some of the benefits of Mocking API Data: •&#9; Faster and more reliable tests : Real API calls introduce delays, network bottlenecks, and unpredictable response times. Mocked responses return instantly and consistently. The following is why these matters: o&#9; No waiting for backend latency. o&#9; No tests failing due to network instability. o&#9; Consistent run times across pipelines and machines. •&#9; Independence from backend availability: Sometimes the backend is under development, undergoing changes, or offline. Mocking removes this dependency completely. The following is why these matters: o&#9; Frontend teams can build UI before the backend exists. o&#9; Development continues even if backend services crash. o&#9; Tests do not break when backend data changes unexpectedly. •&#9; Predictable, controlled data : With mocks, you decide exactly what the API returns—shape, values, errors, or delays. The following is why these matters: o&#9; Easier to test complex edge cases. o&#9; Perfect for implementing pagination, empty states, or special conditions. o&#9; Eliminates randomness or data drift from real servers. •&#9; Enhanced developer productivity : Mocking tools let developers create fake backends early in the development cycle. The following is why these matters: o&#9; Frontend-first development. o&#9; Quickly exploring UI states. o&#9; Building prototypes without waiting for backend APIs. Different ways of mocking API The following are the different ways of mocking the API data. We will explain mainly two in this section: •&#9; Mock API requests &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Mock API responses Mock API requests In mocking API request, intercepting a network call, and replacing the real server’s response with your own custom data. The following is an example of how to mock the API request: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, Page, Route, expect def test_mock_the_books_api(page: Page): def handle(route: Route): json = { &quot;id&quot;: 1, &quot;title&quot;: &quot;Web Automation with Playwright&quot;, &quot;author&quot;: &quot;Kailash Pathak&quot;, &quot;genre&quot;: &quot;QA Automation&quot;, &quot;price&quot;: &quot;10.95&quot;, &quot;rating&quot;: &quot;★★★★☆&quot;, &quot;stock&quot;: &quot;22&quot; } route.fulfill(json=json) # Intercept the route to the books API (broader pattern) page.route(&quot;**/api/books/*&quot;, handle) # Go to the page page.goto(&quot;https://danube-webshop.herokuapp.com/books/1&quot;) expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;QA Automation&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;Left in stock: 22&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;by Kailash Pathak&quot;)).to_be_visible() page.wait_for_timeout(10000) The following is the stepwise explanation of the code: 1. Create handle(route) : Write a function that builds the fake book JSON. 2. Return the mock : Inside handle , call route.fulfill(json=...) to send that fake response. 3. Register interceptor : Call page.route(&quot;**/api/books/*&quot;, handle) so Playwright catches matching requests. 4. Open the page : page.goto(...) loads the book page and triggers the API call. 5. Playwright intercepts : The network request is caught, and handle supplies the mock instead of the real server. 6. Page renders mock data : The page shows title , author , genre , price , and stock from your fake JSON. 7. Assert UI content : expect(...) checks the mocked values are visible on the page. In the following figure, you can see that data is updated in UI: Figure 9.11 : Mock API requests Mock API responses In mocking API response, we replace the real server’s reply with a fake one instead of waiting for a backend to respond, you intercept the request and return your own controlled data. The following is an example of how to mock the API responses: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, Page, Route, expect import json def test_mock_the_books_api(page: Page): def handle(route: Route): new_book = { &quot;id&quot;: 199, &quot;title&quot;: &quot;Web Automation with Playwright and Type Script&quot;, &quot;author&quot;: &quot;Kailash Pathak&quot;, &quot;genre&quot;: &quot;QA Automation Engineer&quot;, &quot;price&quot;: &quot;13.95&quot;, &quot;rating&quot;: &quot;★★★☆&quot;, &quot;stock&quot;: &quot;34&quot; } route.fulfill( status=200, content_type=&quot;application/json&quot;, body=json.dumps(new_book) ) page.route(&quot;**/api/books/199&quot;, handle) page.goto(&quot;https://danube-webshop.herokuapp.com/books/199&quot;) expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;QA Automation&quot;)).to_be_visible() expect(page.get_by_text(&quot;by Kailash Pathak&quot;)).to_be_visible() In the following figure, you can see how to mock the API responses: Figure 9.12 : Mock API responses The following is a stepwise explanation of the code: •&#9; Import Playwright types and the json module. •&#9; Define the test function test_mock_the_books_api(page: Page) . •&#9; Create an inner request handler handle(route: Route) to run on interception. •&#9; Construct the new_book mock dictionary with id , title , author , genre , price , rating , stock . •&#9; Use route.fulfill(...) to return a 200 JSON response containing new_book . •&#9; Register the interception route **/api/books/199 so Playwright uses the handler. •&#9; Navigate the browser to the book details URL ( page.goto(...) ) •&#9; Assert the mocked genre and author text are visible on the page. Authentication, and session storage in Playwright In this section, you will see how to implement authentication and session storage. Session storage and authentication serve different purposes in a web application. Authentication verifies a user’s identity, usually through a username and password. Once the user is authenticated, the website knows who they are and grants access to protected pages. Session storage, on the other hand, is a temporary browser storage area used to save small pieces of data for the current tab. It disappears when the tab is closed: •&#9; Authentication : Proving who you are. Like showing a ticket to enter a theme park. •&#9; Session storage : Short-term memory for the website. Like when you open a game and it remember, your level and your points but when you close the tab, it forgets everything. The following are the steps to implement the authentication: 1. Create .auth/state.json directory and file at root level: playwright/.auth/state.json 2. Create conftest.py : The following code defines @pytest.fixture that configure Playwright for all tests in the session: import pytest from playwright.sync_api import Browser @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;session&quot;) def browser_type_launch_args(browser_type_launch_args): return { **browser_type_launch_args, &quot;headless&quot;: False } @pytest.fixture(scope=&quot;session&quot;) def browser_context_args(browser_context_args): return { **browser_context_args, &quot;storage_state&quot;: &quot;playwright/.auth/state.json&quot; } What this does: •&#9; Launches the browser in headed mode. •&#9; Loads cookies , localStorage , and session data from state.json . 3. Create auth.py to login once and save state : In following code, we saved the login state under /.auth/state.json : import os from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright os.makedirs(&quot;playwright/.auth&quot;, exist_ok=True) with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() context = browser.new_context() page = context.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.saucedemo.com&quot;) page.locator(&quot;#user-name&quot;).fill(&quot;standard_user&quot;) page.locator(&quot;#password&quot;).fill(&quot;secret_sauce&quot;) page.locator(&quot;#login-button&quot;).click() page.wait_for_url(&quot;**/inventory.html&quot;) context.storage_state(path=&quot;playwright/.auth/state.json&quot;) browser.close() print(&quot;Auth saved!&quot;) The following is what this script does: •&#9; Login into SauceDemo . •&#9; Waits until the login is successful. •&#9; Saves session cookies playwright/.auth/state.json . 4. Run the script using the following command: python auth.py After running the preceding command, you will see the authentication data is saved in the state.json file. 5. Create your actual test : authentication.py : import pytest from playwright.sync_api import Page, BrowserContext def test_with_authenticated_state(page: Page): # Navigate directly to inventory page (already authenticated) page.goto(&apos; https://www.saucedemo.com/inventory.html&apos; ) # Verify authentication - check if products are visible page.wait_for_selector(&quot;.inventory_item&quot;, timeout=10000) assert page.locator(&quot;.inventory_item&quot;).count() &gt; 0 # Continue test actions page.locator(&quot;[data-test=&apos;add-to-cart-sauce-labs-backpack&apos;]&quot;).click() # Verify item added to cart assert page.locator(&quot;.shopping_cart_badge&quot;).inner_text() == &quot;1&quot; In the following code, you can see: •&#9; Application used the saved login state. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Opens the products page. •&#9; Confirms user is logged in. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Adds an item to the cart. •&#9; Confirms the cart is updated with 1 item. In the following code, you can see how we implemented: from playwright.sync_api import Page import pytest @pytest.fixture(scope=”module”) def shared_page(browser): context = browser.new_context() page = context.new_page() yield page context.close() def test_save_session_storage(shared_page: Page): shared_page.goto(&quot;https://www.saucedemo.com/v1/index.html&quot;) shared_page.fill(&quot;#user-name&quot;, &quot;standard_user&quot;), shared_page.fill(&quot;#password&quot;, &quot;secret_sauce&quot;) shared_page.evaluate(&quot;() =&gt; { sessionStorage.setItem(&apos;username&apos;, &apos;standard_user&apos;); sessionStorage.setItem(&apos;password&apos;, &apos;secret_sauce&apos;); }&quot;) shared_page.click(&quot;#login-button&quot;), shared_page.wait_for_selector(&quot;.inventory_list&quot;) print(&quot;\n✓ Credentials saved to session storage&quot;) def test_reuse_session_storage(shared_page: Page): shared_page.goto(&quot;https://www.saucedemo.com/v1/index.html&quot;) username, password = shared_page.evaluate(&quot;() =&gt; sessionStorage.getItem(&apos;username&apos;)&quot;), shared_page.evaluate(&quot;() =&gt; sessionStorage.getItem(&apos;password&apos;)&quot;) if username and password: shared_page.fill(&quot;#user-name&quot;, username), shared_page.fill(&quot;#password&quot;, password) shared_page.click(&quot;#login-button&quot;), shared_page.wait_for_selector(&quot;.inventory_list&quot;) print(f&quot;\n Auto-login successful using stored credentials\nUsername: {username}&quot;) else: print(&quot;\n No credentials found in session storage&quot;) Code walkthrough : •&#9; A @pytest.fixture creates a browser context and a shared page for all tests. •&#9; test_save_session_storage opens Saucedemo’s login page. •&#9; It fills the #user-name and #password fields with standard_user and secret_sauce . •&#9; It manually saves these values into sessionStorage using JavaScript. •&#9; It clicks Login and waits until the product page loads. •&#9; test_reuse_session_storage again opens the login page. •&#9; It reads the stored username and password from sessionStorage . •&#9; If found, it auto-fills the login form, logs in, and confirms success; otherwise prints an error. Conclusion This chapter introduced the essentials of API testing with Playwright, guiding learners from setup to validating API responses with confidence. It demonstrated how to integrate API tests with frontend tests using a GenAI tool, enabling smoother and more efficient workflows. Readers also explored techniques for mocking API data, along with managing authentication and session storage to support secure and realistic test scenarios.   - Web Automation with Playwright and Python using AI and MCP C HAPTER 11 Visual Testing with Playwright and Generative AI Introduction In the preceding chapter, we covered the importance of accessibility testing, the Web Content Accessibility Guidelines ( WCAG ), writing accessibility tests in Python using axe-playwright, and essential tools and best practices for accessibility. It explores why accessibility testing is critical for inclusive digital experiences. The chapter also provides an overview of WCAG principles and success criteria, demonstrates how to automate accessibility checks with Python and axe-playwright, and highlights practical tools and industry best practices to ensure websites and applications are usable by everyone. This chapter introduces visual testing as a critical component of modern quality assurance ( QA ), focusing on how teams can detect user interface ( UI ) regressions that functional tests often miss. Readers will gain a solid understanding of visual testing concepts, learn how screenshots are captured and compared, and explore practical strategies for integrating visual testing into real-world development workflows. The chapter provides hands-on guidance using Playwright to implement visual tests for region and full-page layouts. It also explores the emerging role of generative AI ( GenAI ) powered visual testing tools, demonstrating how AI can enhance accuracy, reduce maintenance effort, and improve test coverage. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: •&#9; Understanding visual testing &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; •&#9; Visual testing setup with Playwright •&#9; Visual testing with GenAI tool Objectives The objective of this chapter is to introduce visual testing as a key practice in modern QA, building on the accessibility foundations covered in the previous chapter. It aims to help readers understand how visual testing complements functional and accessibility testing by detecting UI regressions that automated checks often miss. This chapter focuses on core Visual testing concepts, screenshot comparison techniques, and practical implementation using Playwright for both component-level and full-page validations. Additionally, it highlights the role of GenAI–powered visual testing tools in improving accuracy, reducing maintenance, and enhancing overall test coverage. Understanding visual testing Visual testing is a QA technique focused on verifying the visual appearance of an application rather than its functional behavior. While functional tests confirm that an application works as expected, visual tests ensure that it looks correct to the end user. This includes checking layout structure, colors, fonts, spacing, alignment, images, and overall UI consistency. In modern web applications, UIs are often built with complex frameworks, dynamic content, and responsive designs. Small UI changes, such as a misplaced button, broken layout, or incorrect font, can negatively impact user experience ( UX ) even when all functional tests pass. Visual testing addresses this gap by detecting visual regressions that traditional automated tests may overlook. Understanding how visual testing works Visual testing typically involves capturing a screenshot of an application’s UI and comparing it against a previously approved baseline image. The comparison highlights visual differences, allowing teams to identify unintended UI changes. These differences may include layout shifts, missing elements, incorrect styling, or unexpected visual artifacts. Unlike pixel-perfect manual reviews, modern visual testing tools apply intelligent comparison techniques that can ignore acceptable variations, such as dynamic content or animations, while focusing on meaningful UI changes. Importance of visual testing As applications evolve, frequent UI updates and rapid release cycles increase the risk of visual defects. Manual visual checks are time-consuming, inconsistent, and difficult to scale. Visual testing provides a reliable and repeatable way to validate UI changes across browsers, devices, and screen sizes. By incorporating visual testing into automated test suites, teams can: •&#9; Detect UI regressions early in the development lifecycle. •&#9; Maintain consistent UX across releases. •&#9; Reduce reliance on manual visual inspections. •&#9; Improve confidence in UI changes during refactoring or redesigns. Visual testing vs. functional testing Visual testing and functional testing serve different but complementary roles in ensuring application quality. While both aim to validate software behavior, they focus on distinct aspects of the UX. The details are as follows: •&#9; Functional testing : It verifies that an application behaves according to its requirements. It focuses on user actions and system responses, ensuring that features work as intended. For example, functional tests validate whether a button performs the correct action, a form submits data successfully, or an application programming interface ( API ) returns the expected response. These tests are typically written using assertions that check values, states, or outcomes. If the expected result matches the actual result, the test passes, regardless of how the interface looks. •&#9; Visual testing : On the other hand, it focuses on the appearance of the UI. It validates that UI elements are displayed correctly after user interactions. This includes checking layout structure, alignment, colors, fonts, spacing, icons, and overall visual consistency. Instead of validating logic or data, visual tests compare screenshots of the application against approved baseline images to detect unintended visual changes. The following table provides a summary of functional and visual testing: Aspect Functional testing Visual testing Primary focus Application behavior UI appearance Validates Logic, workflows, data handling Layout, styling, visual consistency Test method Assertions on values or states Screenshot comparison Detects Broken functionality Visual regressions User perspective System correctness UX Table 11.1 : Comparison table Benefits of visual testing Visual testing plays a crucial role in modern QA automation by addressing limitations that traditional automated tests cannot fully cover. As applications become more visually complex and UI-driven, visual testing enhances automation effectiveness by validating what users see. The following list outlines some essential benefits of using visual testing: •&#9; Detects UI regressions miss : Automated functional tests validate logic and behavior, but do not verify visual correctness. Visual testing identifies unintended changes in layout, alignment, styling, fonts, icons, and colors that may occur due to Cascading Style Sheets ( CSS ) updates, framework upgrades, or minor code changes. This ensures UI defects are caught early before reaching production. •&#9; Reduces manual visual verification effort : Manual UI reviews are time-consuming and prone to human error. Visual testing automates the process of visual comparison, eliminating repetitive manual checks during regression cycles. This allows QA engineers to focus on test strategy, edge cases, and exploratory testing instead of routine UI inspections. •&#9; Builds confidence during UI refactoring : When redesigning or refactoring UI components, QA teams often worry about unintended side effects. Visual testing provides clear evidence of what changed and what remained the same, enabling teams to approve intentional updates while quickly spotting accidental regressions. •&#9; Improves automation test coverage : Traditional automation scripts validate only what is explicitly asserted. Visual tests capture the entire screen or page, validating multiple UI elements at once. This broad coverage increases confidence in UI quality without writing numerous individual assertions for each visual element. •&#9; Supports cross-browser and responsive testing : Visual testing makes it easier to validate UI consistency across different browsers, devices, and screen sizes. Instead of writing separate assertions for each configuration, teams can rely on visual snapshots to ensure layouts render correctly under varying conditions. Visual testing setup with Playwright Playwright is an open-source test automation framework, designed to support stable and efficient end-to-end testing of modern web applications. A key capability of Playwright is its native support for visual regression testing, which allows teams to identify unexpected changes in the UI by comparing current screenshots with approved baseline images. This approach helps maintain visual consistency across application updates, browsers, and device types, and makes it easier to spot issues such as layout misalignment, incorrect colors, or font rendering inconsistencies that are not typically detected through functional testing alone. Visual testing with Playwright focuses on validating the visual appearance of a web application by capturing screenshots and comparing them against approved baseline images. This approach helps ensure that the UI remains visually consistent as the application evolves, even when underlying code changes. Playwright simplifies Visual testing by offering built-in APIs that support screenshot capture and image comparison without requiring third-party tools. These capabilities make it easier for QA automation teams to detect visual regressions early in the development lifecycle. At its heart, Playwright’s visual testing is a Spot the Difference engine. It works on a simple three-step lifecycle: •&#9; Baseline : The first time you run the test, Playwright takes a screenshot and saves it as the Golden Image . •&#9; Comparison : On subsequent runs, it takes a new screenshot and compares it pixel-by-pixel against the Golden Image. •&#9; Diff : If pixels do not match (beyond a set threshold), the test fails and generates a Diff image highlighting the changes in red. Setup visual testing with Playwright Refer to the following steps to integrate visual testing with Python into Playwright: 1. Install dependencies : You need the core Playwright library, the Pytest plugin, and the visual comparison plugin. a. Install the Pytest plugin for visual testing with Playwright: pip install pytest-playwright-visual 2. Write your first visual test : The plugin provides a fixture called assert_snapshot . You can use this to capture and compare the state of your page. Let us take an example, let us create a file named test_visual.py : from playwright.sync_api import Page def test_homepage_visual(page: Page, assert_snapshot): # 1. Navigate to the site page.goto(&quot;https://example.com&quot;) # 2. Wait for content to settle (optional but recommended) page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) # 3. Assert the snapshot # This takes a screenshot and compares it to the baseline assert_snapshot(page.screenshot(full_page=True)) 3. Run and generate baselines : The first time you run the test, there is no baseline image to compare against. The test will fail, but it will automatically create the reference image for you. What happens next: a. First run : You will see a failure message: Failed: --&gt; New snapshot(s) created. Please review images . b. Folder structure : A new directory named snapshots/ will be created in your project. It stores images organized by test name and browser. 4. Handling UI changes and failures : If the UI changes, the test will fail. The plugin generates a snapshot_tests_failures folder containing three images for every failure—actual, expected, and diff. a. If the change is a bug : Fix your code and run pytest again. The test should pass once the UI matches the original baseline. b. If the change is intentional (update baseline) : If you updated your website&apos;s design and want the new look to be the new baseline, run the command: pytest --update-snapshots Note: The test will still show as failed during this command to ensure you manually review that the newly generated images are correct. Visual testing with Playwright Visual tests are used to verify the visual appearance of an application by comparing screenshots against a baseline image. In single-page visual testing, screenshots are taken of specific UI elements or the visible viewport to detect localized changes such as color, font, or alignment issues. This approach is useful when validating critical components without testing the entire page. Full-page visual testing captures the complete page, including content beyond the visible screen, ensuring that layouts, spacing, and overall design remain consistent. Together, these methods help identify visual regressions early and improve UI quality and UX. Let us discuss some examples to understand how to do visual testing using the plugin pytest-playwright-visual . Use case one is to capture a full-page screenshot Take the example of the site https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php to capture a full-page screenshot. When the page is executed again, if any discrepancy occurs, the plugin can easily detect it by highlighting the differences between the images. In the following code, you can see how a full-page screenshot is captured using Playwright and validated using a visual snapshot. The test launches a Chromium browser, navigates to the application Uniform Resource Locator ( URL ), captures a full-page screenshot, and compares it against a baseline image with an allowed pixel difference threshold to detect visual discrepancies. from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_page_level(assert_snapshot): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/index.php”) assert_snapshot( page.screenshot(full_page=True), name=&quot;homepage-full.png&quot;, # Custom name (optional) threshold=0.1) # Pixel difference tolerance (0-1) browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright : It imports Playwright’s synchronous API. •&#9; def test_page_level(assert_snapshot) : It defines a test function with snapshot assertion support. •&#9; with sync_playwright() as p : It starts Playwright context. •&#9; browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) : It launches Chromium browser in visible mode. •&#9; page = browser.new_page() : It opens a new browser page. •&#9; page.goto(...) : It navigates to the specified URL. •&#9; assert_snapshot(...) : It captures and compares a screenshot with the baseline. •&#9; browser.close() : It closes the browser. In first run, you will see a failure message: Failed: --&gt; New snapshot(s) created. Please review images , as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 11.1 : Full-page screenshot—failed In the next run, test cases are passed, and since there are no discrepancies, test cases are passed, as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.2 : Full-page screenshot—pass Let us take another example where there is a discrepancy in the image on the given site: https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff1 In the following example, we will cover how to handle discrepancy in the image: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright,expect def test_diff1_version(assert_snapshot): &quot;&quot;&quot;Test with ?diff1 parameter to detect visual differences&quot;&quot;&quot; with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff1”) page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) page.wait_for_timeout(1000) assert_snapshot( page.screenshot(full_page=True), name=&quot;helloworld-full-page-diff.png&quot;, threshold=0.0 ) browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect : It imports Playwright synchronous API and assertion utilities. •&#9; def test_diff1_version(assert_snapshot) : It defines a visual regression test function. •&#9; Docstring : It describes the purpose of testing visual differences. •&#9; with sync_playwright() as p : It initializes Playwright session. •&#9; browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) : It launches Chromium in UI mode. •&#9; page = browser.new_page() : It opens a new browser tab. •&#9; page.goto(... ): It navigates to the test URL with ?diff1 parameter. •&#9; page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) : It waits for network requests to finish. •&#9; page.wait_for_timeout(1000) : It adds a short delay for stability. •&#9; assert_snapshot(...) : It takes a full-page screenshot and compares it with the baseline. •&#9; threshold=0.0 : It allows no pixel difference. •&#9; browser.close() : It closes the browser. In the first run, the test may fail with the message: Failed: → New snapshot(s) created. Please review the images , as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 11.3 : Snapshot—failed In the second run, because there are UI changes, the test fails. The plugin generates a snapshot_tests_failures folder containing three images for each failure: actual, expected, and diff. As shown in the following figure, all three images are generated. The discrepancy is clearly visible—the number displayed on the page has changed in the second run, causing the test case to fail: Figure 11.4 : Snapshot—discrepancy Use case two is handling dynamic value Take the example of the site https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff2 to handle the dynamic value. This test performs visual regression testing by loading a webpage, waiting for it to stabilize, and hiding a dynamic random number element to avoid false failures. It then captures a full-page screenshot and compares it with a baseline image using a defined threshold before closing the browser. In the following code, you can see how to handle dynamic value: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect def test_diff1_version(assert_snapshot): &quot;&quot;&quot;Test with ?diff1 parameter - hiding dynamic random number&quot;&quot;&quot; with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff2”) page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) page.wait_for_timeout(1500) # Hide the dynamic number element using CSS page.evaluate(&quot;&quot;&quot; const dynamicElement = document.querySelector(&apos;.diff1.diff2.random-number&apos;); if (dynamicElement) { dynamicElement.style.visibility = &apos;hidden&apos;; } &quot;&quot;&quot;) page.wait_for_timeout(500) assert_snapshot( page.screenshot(full_page=True), name=&quot;helloworld-diff1.png&quot;, threshold=0.2 ) browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect : Imports Playwright synchronous APIs. •&#9; def test_diff1_version(assert_snapshot) : Defines the visual regression test function. •&#9; with sync_playwright() as p : Starts Playwright context. •&#9; browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) : Launches Chromium in visible mode. •&#9; page = browser.new_page() : Opens a new browser page. •&#9; page.goto(...) : Navigates to the test URL. •&#9; page.wait_for_load_state(&quot;networkidle&quot;) : Waits for network activity to finish. •&#9; page.wait_for_timeout(...) : Adds wait for UI stability. •&#9; page.evaluate(...) : Hides the dynamic random number element. •&#9; assert_snapshot(...) : Takes and compares a full-page screenshot. •&#9; browser.close() : Closes the browser. In first run, you will see a failure message: Failed: --&gt; New snapshot(s) created. Please review images , as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.5 : Handling a dynamic value failed Since we hide the dynamic random number element. In the next run, test cases are passed. You can see in the following figure that the test case passes: Figure 11.6 : Handling dynamic value pass Use case three is handling dynamic value with masking Take the example of the site https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff1 to handle the dynamic value. The following code handles the dynamic value using the masking concept: from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright def test_visual_hide_dynamic_text(assert_snapshot): &quot;&quot;&quot;Hide elements with dynamic text (timestamps, user names, etc.)&quot;&quot;&quot; with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() page.goto(“https://applitools.com/helloworld/?diff1”) # Hide the dynamic number element before taking the screenshot assert_snapshot( page.screenshot(mask=[page.locator(&apos;.diff1.diff2.random-number&apos;), # Mask the dynamic number ]), name=&quot;homepage-no-year.png&quot;, threshold=0.8 ) browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright : Imports Playwright’s synchronous API. •&#9; def test_visual_hide_dynamic_text(assert_snapshot) : Defines a visual test that hides dynamic content. •&#9; Docstring : Explains the purpose of masking dynamic text. •&#9; with sync_playwright() as p : Starts the Playwright session. •&#9; browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) : Launches Chromium in visible mode. •&#9; page = browser.new_page() : Opens a new browser tab. •&#9; page.goto(...) : Navigates to the test URL. •&#9; page.screenshot(mask=[...]) : Takes a screenshot while masking the dynamic number element. •&#9; assert_snapshot(...) : Compares the screenshot with the baseline image. •&#9; threshold=0.8 : Allows a defined level of visual difference. •&#9; browser.close() : Closes the browser. During the first run, you may see the following failure message: Failed: → New snapshot(s) created . Review the images shown in Figure 11.7 . It shows a visual snapshot generated after masking a dynamic element during visual testing. The highlighted number on the page changes randomly on each run, so it is masked to prevent false test failures. Figure 11.7 : Dynamic value with masking In the second run, the test case passes because the dynamic number is masked during visual testing. Although the value changes on each execution, masking excludes this area from the screenshot comparison. As a result, only stable and consistent UI elements are validated against the baseline image. Since no visual differences are detected in the unmasked regions, the visual comparison is successful, and the test passes. Use case four is the visual testing of a specific region Testing at the specific region-level involves validating a defined section of a webpage instead of the full-page or a single field. In this approach, a locator is used to capture a particular UI region, such as a form, banner, or container, and a screenshot is taken only for that area. The captured image is then compared with a baseline to detect visual changes. Region-level visual testing helps identify layout or styling issues within a section while avoiding noise from unrelated page changes. In the following code, we are capturing the login region with Email and Password field using the locator page.locator(&apos;.contact-form.bg-light&apos;) : from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright,expect def test_page_level(assert_snapshot): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) page = browser.new_page() # Set fixed viewport size for consistent screenshots page.set_viewport_size({&quot;width&quot;: 1280, &quot;height&quot;: 720}) page.goto(&quot;https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/&quot;) # Use .first to select the first matching element login_page_continer = page.locator(&apos;.contact-form.bg-light&apos;).first expect(login_page_continer).to_be_visible() # Compare only a specific region of the page assert_snapshot(login_page_continer.screenshot(),name=&quot;contact-form.png&quot;,threshold=0.1) browser.close() The following list outlines the code walkthrough: •&#9; from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright, expect : Imports Playwright’s synchronous API and assertion utilities. •&#9; def test_page_level(assert_snapshot) : Defines a visual regression test function. •&#9; with sync_playwright() as p : Starts the Playwright execution context. •&#9; browser = p.chromium.launch(headless=False) : Launches the Chromium browser in non-headless (visible) mode. •&#9; page = browser.new_page() : Opens a new browser page. •&#9; page.set_viewport_size({&quot;width&quot;: 1280, &quot;height&quot;: 720}) : Sets a fixed viewport size to ensure consistent screenshots. •&#9; page.goto(&quot; https://shop.qaautomationlabs.com/ &quot;) : Navigates to the application URL. •&#9; login_page = page.locator(&apos;.contact-form.bg-light&apos;).first : Locates the first matching contact form element on the page. •&#9; expect(login_page).to_be_visible() : Verifies that the selected element is visible. •&#9; assert_snapshot(login_page.screenshot(), name=&quot;contact-form.png&quot;, threshold=0.1) : Captures and compares a screenshot of the specific element. •&#9; browser.close() : Closes the browser and ends the test. In the next section, we will see how we can leverage AI for visual testing. Visual testing with GenAI tool Visual testing has evolved significantly with the integration of GenAI, transforming how teams validate UIs and detect visual regressions. This technology combines traditional screenshot comparison methods with intelligent AI-powered analysis to catch bugs that would otherwise slip through conventional testing approaches. Understanding AI-powered visual testing AI-powered visual testing uses machine learning models to analyze application interfaces, comparing them against baseline images or design specifications. Unlike pixel-perfect comparison tools that flag every minor rendering difference, GenAI can understand context, distinguish between intentional changes and actual bugs, and even generate test cases automatically. Key capabilities of AI-powered visual testing Some key capabilities of AI-powered visual testing are as follows: •&#9; Intelligent diff detection : Modern AI visual testing tools can differentiate between meaningful visual changes (like broken layouts or missing elements) and insignificant variations (such as anti-aliasing differences or dynamic content). This dramatically reduces false positives that plague traditional pixel-comparison approaches. •&#9; Cross-browser and device testing : GenAI helps normalize expected variations across different browsers, screen sizes, and operating systems. The AI learns what constitutes normal rendering differences versus actual defects, making cross-platform testing more efficient. •&#9; Accessibility analysis : AI tools can automatically detect accessibility issues like insufficient color contrast, missing alt text, or improper heading hierarchies by analyzing screenshots and comparing them against WCAG guidelines. •&#9; Self-healing tests : Some AI-powered tools use computer vision to identify UI elements based on visual characteristics rather than brittle selectors. When the underlying Document Object Model ( DOM ) changes but the visual appearance remains consistent, tests can automatically adapt. •&#9; Automated test case creation : By analyzing application screenshots and user flows, GenAI can suggest or automatically create visual test cases for critical user journeys, reducing the manual effort in test authoring. •&#9; Visual bug report enhancement : AI can generate detailed descriptions of visual discrepancies, suggest potential root causes, and even recommend fixes based on patterns learned from previous issues. •&#9; Design-to-code validation : GenAI can compare implemented UIs against design mockups from tools like Figma , automatically flagging deviations in spacing, typography, colors, or component placement. AI-powered tool for visual testing We have many tools available in the market for visual testing. In this section, we will discuss Percy (by BrowserStack ), which can be effectively leveraged for visual testing. Why Percy (by BrowserStack)? Percy brings the power of AI to your visual testing workflow, accelerates setup, eliminates visual noise, and streamlines reviews. Some key reasons why we must use Percy are as follows: •&#9; Accelerate visual test setup and coverage with AI : o&#9; Achieve up to 6× faster setup using the visual test integration agent to auto-identify, install, and configure requirements. o&#9; Enable cross-browser and multi-resolution testing to ensure consistent UXs across devices. o&#9; Add visual testing to existing test scripts directly from your integrated development environment ( IDE ) using a single prompt, no context switching. •&#9; Eliminate visual noise and stabilize tests with AI : o&#9; Suppress minor text and layout changes automatically using visual AI, reducing false positives. o&#9; Use Intelli Ignore to filter out dynamic elements such as carousels, ads, and banners. o&#9; Detect layout shifts early by tracking relative element positions to catch broken UI before release. •&#9; Streamline reviews and debugging with AI : o&#9; Cut review time by up to 3× with high-impact change prioritization and natural language summaries via the visual review agent. o&#9; Customize visual validation using plain-English prompts and snapshot or build-level rules. o&#9; Accelerate debugging with root cause analysis, pinpointing whether changes originate from the DOM, CSS, or layout. The following list outlines how Percy uses AI: •&#9; Visual AI for smart comparisons : o&#9; Percy does not rely only on pixel-by-pixel comparison. o&#9; Its visual AI understands UI structure, layout, and hierarchy. o&#9; This helps detect meaningful visual regressions while ignoring insignificant changes. •&#9; Intelligent noise reduction : AI automatically suppresses minor differences such as: o&#9; Dynamic text changes. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; Font rendering variation. o&#9; Small spacing shifts. •&#9; Intelli Ignore (AI-assisted ignoring) : Uses AI to ignore dynamic or flaky elements like: o&#9; Ads, banners, carousels. &#x2003;&#x2003;&#x2003; o&#9; Timestamps, animations, rotating content. •&#9; Layout shift detection : o&#9; AI tracks relative positions of elements, not just pixels. •&#9; Detects broken layouts such as : o&#9; Overlapping components. o&#9; Misaligned buttons. o&#9; Unexpected UI shifts after CSS changes. Visual testing with Percy Refer to the following steps to create the Percy project: 1. Sign up : a. Sign up for Percy https://percy.io/signup . b. In Percy, create a project of the type Web . In the following figure, you can see two option Web and App . Select Web , as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.8 : Create Percy project c. Name the project. After the project is created, Percy generates a token. d. Note down the token. You have to use it to set your environment variable in the next step, as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.9 : Generate token 2. Install Percy Playwright SDK and other dependencies: pip install percy-playwright npm install --save-dev @percy/cli 3. Use the generated PERCY_TOKEN and export it as an environment variable: a. macOS/Linux : export PERCY_TOKEN=&lt;your_percy_token&gt; b. Windows (PowerShell) : set PERCY_TOKEN &quot;&lt;your_percy_token&gt;&quot; 4. Create the test case : Now, let us create the test case for visual comparison, using the following code: browserstackpercy.py : from playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright from percy import percy_snapshot def test_homepage_visual(): with sync_playwright() as p: browser = p.chromium.launch() page = browser.new_page() page.goto(&quot;https://www.browserstack.com/&quot;) percy_snapshot(page, &quot;Homepage Snapshot&quot;) browser.close() The following list outlines a code walkthrough: a. Import libraries : sync_playwright is used to run Playwright in synchronous mode. percy_snapshot comes from Percy and captures visual snapshots. b. Define the test function : test_homepage_visual() is a test case (commonly executed via pytest ). c. Start Playwright : with sync_playwright() as p initializes the Playwright engine. d. Launch browser : p.chromium.launch() starts a Chromium browser instance. e. Create a new page : browser.new_page() opens a fresh browser tab. f. Navigate to the website : page.goto( https://www.browserstack.com/) loads the target URL. g. Capture visual snapshot : percy_snapshot(page, Homepage Snapshot) takes a visual snapshot and sends it to Percy for visual comparison. h. Close the browser : browser.close() shuts down the browser after execution. Run the command to execute the test case: npx percy exec -- pytest tests/browserstackPercy.py i. In this step, we run the sample test script to take a few snapshots using the percy.snapshot method. The goal is to have a visual build with which to compare a later build. In the following figure, you can see that as we run the command build, the receiving process starts: Figure 11.10 : Execution first build j. On completion, you see logs from Percy confirming that the snapshots were successfully uploaded and a direct URL to the dashboard. There are no visual comparisons yet. 5. Generate the second build, and run the test script again : Follow Step 5 to generate your second build. This takes new screenshots of our modified application, uploads them to Percy, and compares them with the previous screenshots to show visual differences. In the following, you can see the second run has started: Figure 11.11 : Execution second build In the following figure, you can see that when we open the second build snapshot, a clear difference is seen between the previously captured screenshot and the latest run by 54.43%. This comparison highlights the visual gap between the two executions, making it easier to identify and analyze visual changes or regressions. Figure 11.12 : Execution visual gap This way, we can find the visual gap between the two executions using the tool Percy. Conclusion In this chapter, we explored why visual testing is a must-have in modern QA and how it helps catch UI issues that functional and accessibility tests can easily miss. You learned the basics of visual testing, how screenshots are captured and compared, and how to implement visual checks using Playwright for both specific regions and full-pages. We also looked at how GenAI-powered visual testing tools, Percy, make life easier by reducing noise, lowering maintenance effort, and improving coverage, helping teams ship visually consistent and reliable applications with more confidence. In the next chapter, we focus on scaling Playwright automation using continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) pipelines to achieve fast, reliable, and repeatable test execution. You will learn how to integrate Playwright tests into your CI/CD pipeline and get introduced to GitHub Actions as a powerful tool for continuous integration and delivery. The chapter covers implementing GitHub Actions workflows, executing Playwright tests as part of the pipeline, and enabling cross-browser testing. You will also explore browser contexts for running independent browser sessions and learn how to use sharding for parallel test execution, helping you significantly optimize execution time and build robust, scalable test automation workflows. ## 3. Aplicacoes Praticas
- Overview of Playwright and how to setup Playwright with Python and get knowledge of setup and configuration Playwright with Python.
- Importance of web automation Automation testing is the process of using specialized software tools and scripts to execute predefined test cases on a software application, compare actual outcomes with expected results, and report the findings.
- Unlike manual testing, which relies on human testers to interact with the application, automation testing leverages scripts and tools to perform repetitive, time-consuming, or complex tasks with greater speed, accuracy, and efficiency.
- It is a foundation of modern software development, ensuring that applications meet quality standards in an era of rapid development cycles and complex systems.
- Automation testing is integral to the software development lifecycle ( SDLC ), particularly in Agile and DevOps methodologies, where frequent releases demand quick and reliable testing.
- It is used to validate functionality, performance, security, and user experience ( UX ) across various platforms, including web, mobile, desktop, and API-based applications.
- By automating repetitive, complex, or time- consuming testing tasks, it ensures software quality in an era of rapid development cycles and intricate systems.
- However, the journey of automation testing spans decades, evolving from rudimentary scripts to sophisticated artificial intelligence ( AI )-driven frameworks.
- As automation testing evolved over time, new tools and technologies emerged that offered more capabilities and flexibility for testers.
- Some of the current tools for automation testing include Selenium, Appium, Ranorex, Katalon Studio, TestComplete, UFT, Cypress, Playwright, Robot Framework, Cucumber and TestNG.

## 4. Topicos Avancados
- These tools support web, desktop, and mobile based applications with supporting the various programming languages such as Java, Python, C#, Ruby and JavaScript.
- They also support different types of testing such as functional testing, behavior-driven development ( BDD ) testing, UI testing, API testing and test-driven development ( TDD ) testing.
- In the next section, we will explore the four stages of test automation, from the early days of record and playback tools to the current state of AI/ machine learning ( ML ) based tools.
- Record and playback tools Record and playback tools are automation tools that allow users to record their interactions with an application (e.g., clicks, keystrokes, navigation) and then replay those actions to automate repetitive tasks, primarily for testing purposes.
- These tools capture user actions as scripts or sequences that can be edited and executed later.
- The following is how they work: •&#9; Recording : The tool monitors user interactions with the application (e.g., mouse clicks, data entry) and generates a script or macro representing those actions. •&#9; Playback : The recorded script is executed to replicate the same actions on the application, typically for testing purposes like regression or functional testing. •&#9; Editing : Some tools allow users to modify recorded scripts to add logic, parameters, or assertions for validation.
- The following are the key features: •&#9; User-friendly interface requiring minimal coding knowledge. •&#9; Ability to capture UI interactions (e.g., web, desktop, or mobile applications). •&#9; Support for basic validation and assertions (e.g., checking if an element exists). •&#9; Exportable scripts in languages like Java, JavaScript, Python, C#. •&#9; Integration with test management tools or continuous integration/continuous deployment ( CI/CD ) pipelines.
- The following are the use cases: •&#9; Automating repetitive UI-based test cases where there are minimal changes are required. •&#9; Functional and regression testing of web applications. •&#9; Quick prototyping of test scripts for non-technical testers. •&#9; Testing scenarios requiring exact replication of user behavior.
- The following are the advantages: &#9;•&#9; Ease of use : Minimal coding skills required, making them accessible to beginners or non-technical users (e.g., product owner, business analyst). •&#9;&#9; Speed : Rapid creation of test scripts through recording. •&#9;&#9; Low setup time : No need to write code from scratch. •&#9;&#9; Cross-platform : Many tools support multiple platforms (web, desktop, mobile).
- The following are the disadvantages: •&#9; Fragility : Scripts are often tightly coupled to the UI, breaking if the UI changes (e.g., element IDs or layouts). •&#9; Limited flexibility : Difficult to handle complex logic, data-driven testing, or dynamic scenarios. •&#9; Maintenance overhead : Recorded scripts require frequent updates for evolving applications. •&#9; Scalability issues : Not ideal for large-scale or complex test suites.
